SITTING OF TUESDAY, 17 FEBRUARY 1998

IN THE CHAIR: MR ANASTASSOPOULOS
Vice‐President

(The sitting was opened at 9.00 a.m.)


1. Approval of the Minutes

  President . – The Minutes of the last sitting have been distributed.

Are there any comments?

  Haarder (ELDR). – Mr President, allow me to tell you that we are two Liberals who forgot to sign in yesterday. I am sorry. You can confirm I was here.

  President . – Mr Haarder, I knew you were here yesterday since you took part in the Bureau's meeting and I also heard you speak a few times, so there is no doubt in this matter. Your presence has been noted.

  Anttila (ELDR). – (FI) Mr President, I am always amused when I hear that someone has not remembered to sign in. Now the same thing has happened to me. When I came in yesterday there was an enormous queue of people waiting to sign in. I thought I would leave it until later, but I forgot to do so. I did, however, participate in the meeting of the Liberal group which began at 4.00 p.m., and the evening sitting. I hope this will be entered in the Minutes.

(The Minutes were approved)

(1)

(1) Topical and urgent debate (subjects proposed) – Transfer of responsibility for a decision to a committee (Withdrawal): see Minutes.

2. Decision on urgency

Proposal for a Council Regulation on assistance to the applicant countries in central and eastern Europe in the framework of the pre‐accession strategy (COM(97)0634 – C4‐0010/98‐97/0351(CNS))

  Spencer (PPE). – Mr President, if I might say so, this is an unnecessary and rather impertinent request for urgency from the Council. Even by its own timetable, it knows that it does not need an opinion from us until the March plenary. It is fully aware that all ten rapporteurs for the Committee on Foreign Affairs, Security and Defence Policy are halfway through the task of commenting on these partnership agreements.

There are important issues here of parliamentary involvement and supervision of the enlargement process, but that is a debate for next month. It is not a debate for this month. I hope that all colleagues, including those in the Group of the Party of European Socialists, will feel that this is a request for urgency that we should treat with the contempt that it deserves and throw out and reexamine this issue next month.

I wish to say, in all friendship to my colleagues in the Labour Group, that I understand the attractions of affection for a Labour Government. But it does not do the credibility of this House any good if it looks as if the Socialist Group is being used as a whollyowned subsidiary of the British Presidency. We should throw this urgency out and return to this matter next month.

  Titley (PSE). – Mr President, the accession partnerships are the foundation stones of the very inclusive enlargement strategy which this Parliament overwhelmingly voted in favour of as a political priority. The Luxembourg Summit concluded that all the elements have to be in place by 15 March for enlargement to proceed. If we do not grant urgency today we will not therefore give our opinion until 11 March, the day before the European Conference. In those circumstances, and right up against a timetable deadline, it is inconceivable that Parliament's voice will even be heard, never mind listened to. If we give our opinion this month our message could be got across very clearly to the Council in plenty of time for it to consider it.

We also risk being seen as hampering enlargement. We have concerns which the Council and the Commission have sought to address, but we should be careful that we are not seen to be playing cute institutional games, which nobody outside this Chamber can understand, at the very moment when our commitment to the political priority of enlargement should be clear and unambiguous. By not granting urgency, this Parliament has nothing to gain and everything politically to lose. I therefore urge that we support urgency.

  Kellett‐Bowman (PPE). – Mr President, it may be you are going to prolong this debate until 9.15 a.m. However, the point I wish to raise is that the agenda states that we will be voting at 9.15 a.m. It is not yet that time. Since the Bureau is placing great importance on Members being present and voting, it is incorrect to be holding voting at the wrong time.

  President . – Mr Kellett‐Bowman, strictly speaking you are absolutely right. The agenda does indeed mistakenly state – and I will draw the attention of the services to it so that the mistake is not repeated – ’from 9.00 a.m. to 9.15 a.m.: announcement of the list of motions for resolutions tabled on topical and urgent subjects of major importance (Rule 47)’ and ‚9.15 a.m. to 12.00 noon: vote on the following request for urgent procedure: proposal for a Council Regulation...’.

I ought to postpone the vote for seven minutes. The purpose of that is to protect Members so that they may know when they will be voting. But since I see that the House is full, as people have come here earlier, I wonder whether I could ask Parliament's permission to speed up the vote, and I will of course ask our services, next time, to be more careful about these matters.

(Parliament gave its assent.)

  Hory (ARE). – (FR) Mr President, I would simply like to make it known that, in accordance with the new rules that are being enforced, I did not wish to take part in the vote.

  President . – This will be noted, Mr Hory.

  Pex (PPE). ‐ (NL) Mr President, I regret to say that my voting card is missing, so I was not able to take part in the vote. Could you make a note of that, please?

(Parliament agreed to the request for urgent procedure)


3. Programme to combat organized crime (Falcone)

  President . – The next item is the report by Mr Orlando (A4‐0017/98), on behalf of the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs, on the proposal for a Council Decision on a joint action establishing a programme of exchanges, training and cooperation for persons responsible for action to combat organized crime (Falcone programme) (COM(97)0528 – C4‐0572/97‐97/0916(CNS)).

  Orlando (V), rapporteur. – (IT) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, this morning Parliament is called to approve a measure that closes an initial important stage in the European commitment against organized crime. In December 1996, the European Council of Dublin clearly expressed its determination to fight organized crime with a consistent and coordinated approach at European level.

This choice marks a turning‐point: it marks the transition from the stage of occasional intervention to the stage of fundamental intervention. The high‐level group, suitably established, has drawn up a global action plan, a plan drawn up firstly in fact by the Justice and Home Affairs Council that met in Luxembourg on 28 April 1997, and subsequently included in the overall definition drawn up by the European Council that met in Amsterdam in June 1997.

On 20 November 1997, Parliament gave its opinion on several important proposals for joint action, particularly on the punishment for belonging to a criminal organization and the establishment of a mechanism for assessing the implementation, at national level, of international initiatives adopted to fight organized crime. While the Council of Ministers launched the proposed action on the aforesaid assessment mechanism in December 1997, we are still waiting for the launch of the proposal on the punishment for belonging to a criminal organization.

We have certainly witnessed a strengthening of the EU strategy to fight organized crime and recognize the efforts made by certain individual countries, particularly Italy, in recent years. The programme, for which I am the rapporteur, takes its name from the judge, Giovanni Falcone, who was killed by the Mafia in 1992 with his wife and escorts and who now stands as a symbol of the fight against organized crime both within as well as outside Italy.

The programme proposes to strengthen judicial and police cooperation among those responsible for fighting the Mafia and grants finance for apprenticeships, seminars and operational projects, as well as research and training. The Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs proposes several amendments aimed at including, along with the institutional figures and the police, those responsible for the cultural climate and various expressions of civilian society, in the programme, thus emphasizing the importance of the cultural and social dimension in the fight against organized crime.

The involvement of schools, universities and non‐governmental organizations is considered to be important, as is the involvement of notaries, lawyers, accountants and auditors. In such cases of recourse to non‐institutional figures, there should also be a provision for the financial involvement of local government authorities, for supervisory purposes.

I consider the proposal made by several Members in the Group of the European People"s Party for specific support and initiatives in applicant countries to be particularly significant, confirming the need to provide this delicate sector with harmonious legislation and cultural awareness not only among the Member States but also among the states applying to join the EU.

To conclude, I would like to point out the importance of this measure: an importance that is certainly specific but that is also symbolic, because it assumes the many positive experiences of individual states to be a reference point in some way and it assumes the Italian experience to be a positive factor.

I think that what should be emphasized today is no longer an individual experience in an individual state but the perception and awareness, at European level, that we are faced with a phenomenon – the Mafia and organized crime – that constitutes a battle that we can either fight together or lose irreparably.

  Nassauer (PPE), draftsman of the opinion of the Committee on Legal Affairs and Citizens' Rights. – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, the idea of actually bringing together people cooperating in the fight against organized crime in Europe, of training them together and of letting them gain experience together, is so undeniably desirable and necessary, that I support both this programme and Mr Orlando's report.

However, despite this basically positive view, I have a few critical comments to make about projects of this kind, although less so about this particular programme. This programme draws in a relatively large group of people from the European Union: judges, public prosecutors, police officers, customs officials, and so on. We can scarcely expect that with the limited funds available for this programme it will be possible to reach a significant number of people in the target groups. For that reason, I believe that we should limit the number of people involved and target the funding as far as possible.

Furthermore, we should not get carried away in defining possible subject areas. I personally have doubts about whether it makes sense to finance research projects from the limited funds available. We should also avoid things such as friendly weekend meetings coming under a programme of this kind.

We also need to remember that we already have a whole string of similar projects, so we must avoid duplication. For example, we have Matthaeus, a programme for customs officials. We also have Matthaeus Tax, Grotius, Stop, Oisin and so forth. We need to be very careful to make sure that these programmes do not overlap and that certain people who have a particular knack of getting on board such programmes do not get too many bites of the apple.

Then there is the question of funding: it was originally envisaged that European co‐financing would represent 80 %. The Committee on Budgets has suggested lowering this to 60 %. I personally would prefer to see 50 % financing, so that people making bids for funds would have to finance a significant proportion of these measures themselves. We have, in fact, opted for the 60 % proposed by the Committee on Budgets. One last thing: we need more stringent and specific checks on what happens to funds and the success rate of initiatives using these funds, and of course our Committee on Budgetary Control has a role to play here.

  Bontempi (PSE). – (IT) Mr President, I too would like to point out the importance of this programme and the vote we cast within the framework of a highly significant increase in attention, as well as proposals on the fight against organized crime. The name given to the programme, Falcone, is that of a judge who became well‐known, particularly after his death, for he was killed when he was in greatest difficulty and most isolated.

We should always remember that in fighting organized crime, along with an effective police and judicial action, it is very important to create a climate of commitment and watch‐keeping against organized crime. From this point of view, I have to say that Italy has been isolated for a long time. Our country, which has had to experience the effects of organized crime on its own people, as the Falcone case as well as the Borsellino and other cases have shown us, has had difficulty convincing Europe of the need for this priority. Fortunately, we can now see that this belief exists today, even if we are obviously far from satisfied with the results. For example, with the means that have been established and that are highly important with regard to organized crime, the joint actions are still ineffective!

In approving this programme, I would like to remind the Council and the Commission of the importance of using the means chosen, even if not by everyone, as we cannot implement everything at the same time; what has been chosen should be implemented, because the credibility of a European action that is finally becoming a joint action, a joint commitment against organized crime, is at stake.

With regard to the programme, I will limit myself to emphasizing the correctness of the two additions that have been proposed, those made by the members of the PPI on the extension to countries in the process of joining, and that proposed by Mr Orlando, who suggests using this programme to support the experience acquired in the field and, starting from local associations and institutions, managing to create the climate I was talking about earlier. Several months ago, the Italian association “Libera’, which groups together around 600 associations, held a very important and useful hearing in which we heard how important the work on education, prevention and awareness of the fight against crime was in creating the decisive alliances in society for an effective fight by the state authorities.

  Cederschiöld (PPE). – (SV) Mr President, organized crime is now actively targeting Europe. If we do not combat it strongly now, we are going to have to do so using drastic methods later. The action plan to combat organized crime shows that awareness of this serious threat to Europe is on its way to finding concrete expression.

In my report on organized crime, which was discussed by Parliament in November last year, I pointed out several areas in which cooperation needs to be strengthened. One of the most important areas concerns practical cooperation across borders. Practical cooperation towards common goals tends after a time to show what methods are the most effective. The Nordic countries have a long history of such a cooperation in the area of law. This has in fact led to systems which, if not identical, are certainly compatible. In the three Nordic countries which are in the Union, we have achieved many of the goals the Union is now trying to achieve jointly.

The Falcone programme is an important step on the path towards a successive coordination of European systems of criminal law and measures to combat crime. Such a coordination must evolve through practical cooperation. I am therefore in favour of the Commission's proposal which is based on training and exchange between judges, public prosecutors, customs and police. It is important that the professional circles are directly involved and receive this support amounting to ECU 10 million over five years, which is quite a modest sum in this regard.

In the long term the current distribution of Structural Funds is irrational and this should therefore lead to the legal infrastructure also getting some of the traditional Structural Funds.

I have opinions on some of the details in the programme, including the financing model. The Commission is proposing that 80 % of the costs should be financed from Community funds. But the usual principle in other cases is half and half, that is, 50 % from the Community and 50 % from the participating Member States. That model has advantages because the financial responsibility is shared and, more importantly, control is shared and can therefore be stronger. That is an important point in a Europe where the citizens occasionally doubt the fairness and effectiveness of EU subsidies. However, the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs has agreed to compromise with the Commission and is proposing 60 % from Community funds.

I believe there is reason to look further and in the long term to also prepare the Falcone programme for participants not only from the applicant countries, but also from countries which share common borders with the applicant countries.

Finally, it is important to stress that the Falcone programme is only one of the first initiatives in a series of measures which are going to be required to improve the quality of legal systems, and to increase understanding and cooperation between people from crime‐fighting and judicial administrations in the Member States and applicant countries. If the aim of creating reliable legal systems in all of the Union's Member States is not achieved, then it is possible that the large gains which all the parties involved stand to make from enlargement might come to nothing. The legal structure within the Union must be successful, which means that we have to invest in it.

  President . – I will repeat for the fifteenth time my comment to the political groups: they cannot allow their members a speaking time of one minute! There is no dignity in that. There should be fewer people speaking, so that they may have at least two minutes. I cannot accept that it is right to cut off a speaker after just one minute!

  Caccavale (UPE). – (IT) Mr President, thank you for that expression of solidarity with the speaker.

I would first like to thank Mr Orlando for his commitment to this work in memory of a judge. The programme bears the name of a judge who was a victim in the battle against the Mafia and who was killed so savagely.

I too believe that the adoption of the Falcone programme is a considerable step forwards, particularly with regard to the exchange of information between those acting in the fight against organized crime – police, judges and all others involved – which is increasingly more transnational and has knocked down frontiers.

It is important for the new countries wishing to join the EU to be involved, but the control of democracy is also very important. This is clearly stated in the Falcone programme: the European Parliament should establish control and the principles of personal guarantees, individual freedoms and equality between accusation and defence should not be forgotten, even in the fight against organized crime. The risk otherwise is that the exceptional laws, used to fight organized crime, may become ordinary laws, which would obviously be an enormous step backwards for the freedom and democracy of European citizens.

  De Clercq (ELDR). ‐ (NL) Mr President, according to a Eurobarometer survey, 70 % of European citizens think that decisions on combating drugs, crime and terrorism are better made at European than at national level. The citizens are right, of course. Cross‐border problems such as terrorism and crime require a global, joint approach with effective and coherent measures. The Falcone project forms part of this. It is, as has been said, one of the implementation measures in the action plan to combat organized crime, and currently, concrete interpretation of this is beginning.

As a result of the dramas which took place two years ago in my country, Belgium, a Parliamentary committee of inquiry was set up to find out what went wrong in the various judicial inquiries. One of the committee of inquiry"s final conclusions was that the failing judicial policy in Belgium could be primarily attributed to inadequate organization of our judicial system, a poignant lack of cooperation and coordination between the different police services, and the fact that the exchange of criminal information between judicial examining institutions is virtually non‐existent.

We must therefore categorically avoid Europe making the same mistakes which have led to the events in my country. This is exactly the reason why this Falcone project is so important. This five‐year programme is meant to combat organized crime in the European Member States through cross‐border exchange, training and cooperation among those responsible for combating it.

It is time we gave the European Union the necessary resources to act decisively on one of the biggest problems of our time: the guarantee of our security.

  Angelilli (NI). – (IT) Mr President, we must warmly welcome the Falcone programme. It is a programme which, I too confirm, bears the name of an exceptional Italian judge, savagely killed several years ago. This programme, as a joint action plan against organized crime, is first and foremost a valuable and positive contribution towards a process of real European unification which, in addition to the currency, should also provide a common internal policy for the safety and protection of citizens. Despite that, in my opinion but also in that of other Members, the programme needs to define more rigorously and specifically its objectives, fields of application, methods of participation and financing, to avoid dissipating initiatives and wasting money.

Secondly, we need to confirm that, to have the desired effect, the Falcone programme should incorporate its operating initiatives into Europol"s field of application, particularly with regard to drug trafficking.

The Falcone programme, if it does not involve too many conferences and seminars, but concentrates on implementation, innovation of information systems and vocational training for employees and research, may provide a great possibility for the modernization and development of the systems for fighting crime, and, above all, a great service for European citizens and, as I said earlier, European unification.

  Schulz (PSE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, we have heard many sensible comments about the programme itself and about the suggestions made by Mr Orlando. I have a fundamental question: how should we go about fighting organized crime in the European Union in the long term?

The Falcone programme, whilst preserving national responsibility for combating crime, seems to mean that officials responsible for such matters at national level will exchange experience gained within their countries, only to go home again and carry on doing things at national level just as they were before the exchange of information. That will not get us anywhere in the long term. Although it is a good programme, and the exchange of experience is worthwhile, Mr De Clercq has made a very important point. Members of this House can at any time discuss the freedom of movement of organized crime, and the fact that the cross‐border activities that it involves are becoming increasingly easier, because we are talking about international economic activity within the large free trade area which the EU constitutes, to which we are now going to add a single currency.

We shall soon be adding consolidation of finance policy to economic consolidation and consolidation of trade. The practical scope for abusing this structure is becoming ever greater, but we are not fighting this with any EU counter‐strategy. That is why I believe the Falcone programme is desirable – if it is viewed as a preliminary stage of subsequently making the fight against crime Europewide, that is, fighting it at Community level. That would be a step in the right direction which we would warmly welcome. I would like us to bear this in mind for future debates.

  Pirker (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, I agree with all the previous speakers who have said that organized crime has taken hold not only in the European Union, but worldwide, and I would like to quote a dramatic figure that makes the position very clear. The International Monetary Fund estimates that every year some ECU 550 billion generated by organized crime is transferred to the legal economy. We can all imagine what the implications are for legitimate companies, for state organizations and for society in general of transferring criminally‐generated money to the legal economy on this scale. It represents a massive threat to us all.

Our citizens think it is high time that the police and the judiciary matched the network set up by organized crime with a corresponding international network of their own. I agree with Mr Schulz that we should consider putting national sovereignty to one side in the interests of European justice and cooperation in the fight against crime. We must not allow our citizens to get the impression that European integration works in the interest of criminals rather than of ordinary citizens. So we must be clearly seen to be promoting European cooperation in the interests of citizens' safety.

This programme is a step in that direction, and I believe it would be much more valuable if this programme could benefit from cooperation with the many other programmes we have, such as Odysseus, Grotius or Sherlock. If we can manage to see the effects of cooperation with these programmes, then our citizens will really believe and sense that Europe is actually doing something in the interest of their safety, that Europe is not just a common economic area, but that it is also gradually becoming a common legal area and a common safety area!

  van Bladel (UPE). ‐ (NL) Mr President, the initiative for a joint approach against organised crime under the symbolic name Falcone is obviously to be welcomed. Yet I wonder how, during the coming local and national elections which are held all over Europe, we will be able to explain to the citizens that an action plan with fifteen guidelines, thirty specific recommendations, to be evaluated after five years, and a committee involving a wide range of people with a small budget, will be able to inflict a definite blow to a criminal sector characterized by creativity and flexibility. The approach strikes me as too cumbersome, too bureaucratic even. Like Mr Nassauer, I would have preferred if it had been more limited, for example only to people who catch real criminals. Because then you can go to the European citizens and tell them that the approach against crime is successful.

  Gradin, Member of the Commission. – (SV) Mr President, first I would like to congratulate Mr Orlando on a very thorough report. I value the support which the rapporteur is giving the Commission for this important initiative. Like Mr Schulz, the Commission hopes, of course, that the programme will mean further steps towards better European cooperation in the legal field, because Falcone is going to be significant in several ways. Among other things, the programme will offer a financial framework in which a majority of the recommendations adopted at the Summit in Amsterdam in connection with the action plan to combat organized crime are going to be implemented, and will monitor their implementation. As you know, the Cardiff European Council will monitor further how the work on the action plan is progressing. It is therefore, of course, particularly important that the Falcone programme gets going very quickly.

The Falcone programme is also going to substantially improve the conditions for cross‐border cooperation between public servants who work to combat organized crime in various ways in our Member States. In this way, contact between experts and people on the ground can be increased several times over.

As far as the Commission is concerned, I can note with satisfaction that it seems as if the amendments proposed in the report are all aimed at strengthening the proposal. Obviously it would be desirable if people affected by organized crime could be involved because these people have an important function, not least with regard to disseminating knowledge of the consequences of crossborder crime. However, I believe the important thing right now is that the Falcone programme is implemented and that in this way we make further concrete efforts to implement the action plan which has existed since the Amsterdam Summit.

  President . – Madam Commissioner, I note that you are setting an example by taking up as little time as possible with your comments, a thing which does not always happen with your colleagues.

The debate is closed.

The vote will take place at 12.00 noon.


4. Human rights in the EU

  President . – The next item is the report by Mrs Pailler (A4‐0034/98), on behalf of the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs, on respect for human rights in the European Union (1996).

  Pailler (GUE/NGL), rapporteur. – (FR) Mr President, I would like to highlight the contribution of the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs to this debate, in particular through the presentation of amendments.

I cannot provide an analysis of the respect for human rights in the fifteen countries of the Union in five minutes. A detailed study of the rulings of the European Court of Human Rights, of the petitions and of the NGOs all confirm the progress which remains to be achieved in order to be consistent with the commitments decreed by the international instruments for protection of human rights, whether it be the Charter of the United Nations, the Universal Declaration of Human Rights or even the two International Covenants on Civil and Political Rights and Economic, Social and Cultural Rights. Without being exhaustive, let me also mention the decisions of the conferences in Vienna in June 1993, Copenhagen and Beijing in 1995, and finally that of Stockholm in 1996 concerning child exploitation.

If I insist on this overall framework, it is because it has concerned the European Union for many years, and it would be ridiculous for Parliament to refuse to recognize the indivisibility and interdependence of human rights. I know there is a strong temptation on the part of some of our colleagues not to recognize economic and social rights. To remain the last circle of diehards regarding this question would deal a blow to our credibility both inside and outside the Union. Would we reject the Universal Declaration of 1948 which stipulates, in Article 22, that “everyone, as a member of society, has the right to social security, and is entitled to realization...of the economic, social and cultural rights indispensable for his dignity and the free development of his personality’? Would we disregard Father Joseph Wresinski"s analysis, the founder of ATD Fourth World, who forcefully declared that, “wherever people are condemned to live in misery, human rights are violated; to join together to ensure their respect is a sacred duty’?

So, this is our duty today, when the European Union has 18 million unemployed, more than 57 million living in poverty and more than 2 million homeless! But to this scourge must be added, on occasion, the misfortune of not being European. Too many Member States have misguidedly linked the issue of unemployment with that of immigration. Thus, daily racism, which is insidious but daring to display itself in the proper and figurative sense of the word, has increased to dangerous levels in our countries. I could quote you many figures in this respect, or rulings of the European Court of Human Rights, such as the ruling in the case of Ahmed against Austria, Chahal against the United Kingdom, or Gaygusuz or Remli against France, and many more.

We must fight racism with all our strength, but also by example. This is why I am requesting the Committee on the Rules of Procedure, the Verification of Credentials and Immunities to impose sanctions on Members who make racist comments whilst exercising their function as a Member of Parliament.

I would also like to sound an alarm and to condemn the municipal councils governed by the National Front in France, which are putting the right to culture and information in danger by withdrawing books from libraries, abolishing grants and demanding the closure of cultural centres such as Château‐Vallon in Toulon.

Measures must be taken to guarantee respect for cultural rights and to thwart the enemies of liberty who intend to make their cultural order supreme.

I would also have very much liked to talk about women"s rights and the rights of children, the right of asylum, the often terrible conditions of detention in our countries, the lack of respect for sexual orientation and so on. We must be on the alert for all of these violations and this lack of strict respect for human rights. We must be unceasing in our endeavours, since the future of peoples and of humanity is our responsibility.

Let us remember Félix Gualtari"s warning, which he gave shortly before his death in 1992: “in different forms, a micro‐fascism is growing in the pores of our society, showing itself through racism, xenophobia, the revival of religious fundamentalism, militarism and the oppression of women. History guarantees no irreversible clearing of a progressive barrier. Only human practice, a collective will, can protect us from falling once more into the worst barbarities. In this respect, it would be totally illusory to fall back on the formal constraints of human and people"s rights. Rights are not guaranteed by a divine authority. They depend on the energy of the institutions and formations of power which support their existence’.

So having proclaimed them, let us have the courage to make these rights a reality.

(Applause)

  Pirker (PPE), draftsman of the opinion of the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs. – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, the starting point for this opinion was our criticism of the 1997 human rights report. We agreed to refer the various topics discussed to the specialist committees and to restrict ourselves to actual infringements of human rights, because we believed that human rights as a whole, and this matter in particular, would be better served in that way. I therefore prepared an opinion as part of the draft report and presented it to the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs with precisely this in mind.

I investigated the infringements that had taken place in 1996 and discovered that in the field of social rights connected with nondiscrimination there had only been an infringement of the European Convention for the Protection of Human Rights in one country: in this case a social‐welfare benefit had been linked to a requirement by the public prosecution service. This misunderstanding has already been corrected in the country involved. I also investigated the extent to which the Council of Europe's Social Charter had been infringed in the corresponding areas, and established that there had been infringements in all countries in respect of trade unions and employees, although I must add that the Social Charter is of a declamatory, recommendatory nature.

I have drawn two conclusions from these infringements. The first is that infringements should be avoided where they occur and that adjustments should be made to national legal provisions. The second is that Member States should be called upon to make recommendations to ensure compliance with the provisions of the Social Charter in the areas of forced labour, freedom of association and the right to strike.

This proposal was accepted in the first debate, but during the second debate a great many amendments were tabled, which all pointed in the same direction as in previous years. I therefore voted against my own report for the following reasons, and I am now speaking as a member of the European People's Party: these amendments meant that the objective of focusing on a real discussion of human rights infringements was not achieved. We went down the same path as in previous years. The human rights report was used as a vehicle for peddling left‐wing ideological ideas in this Parliament, ideas that no‐one has any time for any more, because they are unrealistic, often obscure and above all unsuccessful.

Human rights are too important to be treated as an ideological football. If the human rights report turns into a theoretical discourse on Marxist economic theories, calls for unacceptable migration policies and denounces child protection provisions as antihomosexual laws, then the report just has to be rejected.

  Schulz (PSE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, until a few moments ago I was looking forward to an intellectually stimulating debate. Mr Pirker, the comments you made at the end did nothing to enhance your otherwise very good speech. So I am looking forward to other Members' comments, because we cannot justify reducing the human rights debate to little more than the level of bar‐room politics.

You cannot tackle the problems that we have to confront every year in the way Mr Pirker has just done. Every year we are unfortunately at loggerheads in this House about the human rights report and about the human rights situation within the European Union. When we come to vote it will be another example of trench warfare virtually incomprehensible to the outsider, because most people believe that there is no need to argue about human rights, because we should be able to work on the assumption that there is a basic consensus on this issue amongst democratic politicians, which covers most, if not all of those in this House. However, this basic consensus no longer exists in practice. It no longer exists because a few years ago some groups in this House embarked on a process of trying to find definitions of human rights which go beyond the traditional concept of human rights in the sense of civil rights, which must be definable in modern states such as the Member States of the Union in terms of fundamental ecological, social and cultural rights.

I would like to make one thing clear on behalf of my group: the Member States of the European Union, as highly modern, rich, efficiently administered, and, in terms of educational standards, highly developed states, are of course different from the states covered in our human rights reports. Mr Goerens gave a good example a few days ago, on child mortality. We cannot formulate a constitutional right concerning the protection of the new‐born child against all possible dangers in a country like Guinea‐Bissau, because the state simply cannot finance what is needed in terms of infrastructure, hygiene and sanitation there. But in a country like Germany, the state can finance them. In Germany, every new‐born child can be cared for in optimal conditions. So that in my country a family believes it can rightly claim this kind of protection, regardless of its own particular social situation, in contrast to a family in that African country.

This difference in prosperity alone indicates that we need a new debate. Another issue is whether what we are demanding here will eventually lead to fundamental rights of this kind being embodied somewhere. However, it would be wrong not to have this debate. In particular, the Group of the European People's Party tries every year to derail this debate on the basis that it has nothing to do with civil rights, with human rights. Wrong! It has everything to do with those rights.

Furthermore, we in this House cannot afford – and this has been said often enough already, but it needs to be said again – to point the finger of blame at all sorts of human rights abuses all over the world, drawing up resolutions and generally lamenting the situation, if at the same time we refuse to talk about the torture, rape, suppression, abuse and psychological terrorism practised by the institutions of the European Union and its Member States. That would deprive us of any moral legitimacy in making accusations of human rights infringements in other parts of the world. For that very reason we need to be particularly alert to human rights abuses within the European Union itself.

I know that all this is very unpleasant if you proclaim your country to be a democratic and constitutionally governed state, but protecting fundamental rights is more difficult in practice than writing paper constitutions. Yet a democratic and constitutionally governed state that does not strive every single day to ensure that its constitution is not a mere symbol but an everyday reality is bound to lose its own legitimacy.

  De Esteban Martin (PPE). – (ES) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, once again we are faced with a report which unfortunately fails to focus on the real and legal aspects of respect for human rights in the European Union. Once again we find ourselves looking at a project whose content and structure have been spoiled, probably because of motives – perhaps political – other than those which should shape this document"s objectives.

It seems people need reminding that Parliament"s resolutions are the resolutions of a legislative assembly, and we cannot approve reports which lack a legal base and are a response simply to political and programmatical aims. It is our duty to endow these resolutions with a strict respect for ideological impartiality and for the law in its widest sense. That is why we in the Group of the European People"s Party have been calling for absolute respect for our original law: the Treaty. We have always stood up for our duty to use as a basis the European Convention on Human Rights, which was signed in Rome. Unfortunately, however, we are faced with a report which confuses the most basic principles of the law, and fails to make a distinction between basic law and Member States" obligations towards their citizens.

We in the Group of the European People"s Party reaffirm our tradition of being in the forefront of the defence of human rights. We repeat our absolute respect for the dignity and value of the person, and the equal rights of men and women. And we repeat that all human rights are universal, indivisible and independent. So in this report we will vote in favour of those paragraphs which actually reflect human rights problems, such as individual freedoms, the rights of the defendant, political rights or the right to life. We share the concern to promote and protect children"s rights and to guarantee the protection of the rights of people who may be particularly vulnerable to mistreatment or abandonment, including the young, the disabled and the elderly. Nevertheless, we will have to oppose those paragraphs of the report which fail to respect the concept of human rights.

This House cannot be used to approve resolutions which attempt to use the subject matter for political ends, especially when those subjects are as sensitive as fundamental rights. In the future we must reconsider the structure and content of this sort of report, so that we can act with the greatest possible consensus on such a serious issue as this. To do that, perhaps we will need to be able to organize hearings with the judges of the European Court of Human Rights, so they can tell us which are the commonest types of appeals made by our citizens before the Council of Europe, and assess the sentences delivered during the year and examined by the report, or establish other methods for dialogue to allow us to study human rights from a common perspective.

Therefore, Mr President, before I finish I want to say that I hope and trust that common sense will prevail in future debates on these problems, and that the political groups will be able to forget their differences for the sake of the people of Europe, in a matter as important as human rights.

  Schaffner (UPE). – (FR) Mr President, on reading Mrs Pailler"s report on human rights in the European Union, one can but be divided between two contradictory attitudes: being impressed, on the one hand, by the work that has been carried out and, on the other, exasperated by its litany. In effect, this report has become a sort of exercise in style, almost a catalogue of catch‐alls, a mixture of incantatory affirmations, good intention and disjointed imprecations.

Who would dare to affirm without batting an eyelid that they were hostile to a total respect for human rights, that the European Union, and thus its Member States, should not keep guard over the respect for freedom of thought, of conscience and of religion? Why are such paragraphs here, if not to try to get the backing of as many Members as possible regarding this text and in the process to get far more questionable paragraphs adopted? Can we reasonably admit that the duties of the individual are not tied to any obligations? Can we imagine rights without duties? This is the ideal that Mrs Pailler is trying to get passed.

A tendency towards generalization is too often noted in this report. All homosexuals are persecuted, all employers of household staff are pro‐slavery, all members of the forces of law and order are nothing but grim brutes and all prisoners have to undergo inhuman medical examinations. Furthermore, the report omits to mention a whole series of rights which do not appear fundamental in the eyes of the rapporteur: the right to live in security, to come and go as you please, to go out in the evening without fear, to know that your property, often acquired through much hard work, will not be stolen, for parents to know that their children will not fall prey to sexual or mental diseases or the right of victims to claim fair compensation. The report contains no less than twelve paragraphs on the situation of prisoners, but regarding the victims, not a word!

Finally, this report, if considered from the outside, gives a totally negative image of the situation of human rights in the Union. Of course, nothing is perfect, but this should encourage our Parliament, which sets itself up as the universal censor, to show less arrogance.

Nevertheless, until citizens of the European Union start emigrating to Algeria, Cuba or Afghanistan, while our Member States remain the object of so many requests for asylum and while they have to protect themselves against such high levels of illegal immigration, I remain reassured about our record!

  Goerens (ELDR). – (FR) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, the European Year against Racism was another opportunity for us to confirm that nothing is ever definitively gained with regard to respect for others, their dignity, their differences and their cultural identity. This is one of the main reasons that justifies the holding of an annual debate on human rights in the European Union.

A second reason relates to Europe"s situation in the world. The rights of minorities, the flood of people requesting asylum and the situation of illegal immigrants are all making Europe seem like a permanent construction site and thus an area in which respect for human rights must be affirmed.

In this respect, Europe must try to be irreproachable, for it is not sufficient to include a clause regarding respect for human rights in all agreements between the European Union and third countries. The Union, which wants to include the said clause in its agreements, must first of all prove its good conduct within its own borders.

The Liberals have always favoured a wide debate on the inclusion of second and third generation rights into the reports relating to human rights. Given their progressive nature and the need for codification, we advocate their inclusion as well as the continuation of the compilation of the reports that we have presented on this issue.

The European Union is only credible in this field if it is open to self‐criticism. Indeed, the control and follow‐up mechanisms put in place by the Council of Europe in the area of human rights are a permanent invitation to vigilance on the part of Member States and their citizens.

It is in relation to the weak, the marginalized and the most vulnerable members of our society that our desire to respect human rights is measured. Considered from this point of view, the work accomplished by Mrs Pallier is excellent and is worthy of our full consideration.

  Mohamed Alí (GUE/NGL). – (ES) Mr President, first of all I would like to congratulate the rapporteur on her excellent report, and express my full support for each and every one of the proposals included in it.

Having said that, I would like to focus my short speech on the fight against racism and xenophobia. Racism is a crime and, without losing sight of that fact, the Member States need to strengthen their laws to combat all types of racist acts or statements. It is often said that unemployment and economic crisis are the very worst conditions for fostering racism and xenophobia, and that is not untrue. However, we must not forget that social exclusion has a strong cultural component. Both these factors should be taken into account in the fight against racism.

Finally, I should like to draw attention to the novel suggestion Mrs Pailler makes in her report: that the European Parliament itself could act against any Members who promote racism or xenophobia in any way. We need to do away with what some people call institutional xenophobia, and with measures like this I think we are on the right track.

  Roth (V). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, millions of people, for an unlimited period – limited perhaps only by their death – have only a few rights: the right to misery, or to misery sooner or later, frequently the right to lose the roof over their heads and lose any social respect and any self‐respect, apart from an uncertain or failed identity, and the right to that most basic of feelings, shame. These are the words of Viviane Forester.

What we need is a renaissance of fundamental rights, of human rights here in the European Union. We must put a stop to the carelessness with which basic democratic principles are challenged. In practice, we are living in an era in which democratic processes and solidarity are dwindling. The foundation for human rights here is not stable, it is starting to crumble and to break up. So the annual report on human rights is all the more important, and it is all the more easy to understand the reasons for what has now become a traditional ideological debate on the definition of human rights and the repeated attempts to reduce, diminish, and relativize it.

A report on human rights has to deal with the unvarnished truth and nothing else. I really wish that Christian‐Democrat Members like Mr Pirker would reflect on what the major churches regard as fundamental rights. They demand the right to work, the right to dignified work. They call for justice in society, justice as regards equitable distribution of wealth and holding a stake in society. A state which refuses the homeless the right to vote and the right to have a say, is refusing them the right to human dignity, since an important part of the concept of democracy is that the right to share in decision‐making is not linked to any other criterion except that of simply being a human being – it has nothing to do with gender, skin colour or how much money you have in the bank.

Rights are there to be exercised. It is a cynical distortion to talk about the misuse of rights and to attack exercising rights as misuse. We hear the word “misuse’ everywhere – “misuse’ of the right to asylum, “misuse’ of social protection, “misuse’ of journalists' right not to reveal their sources. And Mr Pirker talks about misuse here in this House, when we are discussing human rights.

So we must ensure that we guarantee the freedom to exercise rights instead of attacking “misuse’. That is what democracy is all about. I do not have enough time to mention individual cases, Mr President. I would have liked to talk about Austria and discrimination against homosexuals. I would have liked to talk about Germany and the contemptuous treatment of refugees in that country. That is what a human rights report should be about, not about constructing an empty façade.

Thank you very much, and thank you, Aline!

(Applause from the left)

  Pradier (ARE). – (FR) Mr President, we will of course vote for Aline Pallier"s report. Certainly human rights, the definition of which everyone is agreed upon, must be promoted and we should all be amongst the first to fight to have them respected. But the vital importance of economic and social rights must be insisted upon. It is somewhat indecent to use the rapporteur"s membership of a certain group as a pretext to challenge the content of paragraphs dealing with these rights. Nobody would consider challenging the right of a couple to have children or not, so what about the right of a family to give their children a minimum education, to guarantee them good physical and mental health and to give them the minimum means for development?

The way in which our world is organized, whereby in the Union more and more money is produced along with more and more poor people, must be very closely scrutinized, for the violation of these basic human rights is the daily bread of life in the European Union today. So, in order to promote rights that we were once able to describe as definitive, a minimum basis of respect for men and women is necessary. It is on this basis that we will be able to witness the development of a harmonious society. The right to live in dignity is a very strict minimum.

  Buffetaut (I‐EDN). – (FR) Mr President, the annual report on respect for human rights in the European Union is a ritual to which all members of this House must be able to sacrifice without hesitation, in particular we French, since it was a Frenchman, King Louis XVI, who was the first European to promulgate a declaration of the rights of man and citizen.

Unfortunately, after reading what has been submitted to us, we can but remain sceptical. Is it a text on respect for human rights or a political manifesto from a member of the French Communist Party?

(Protests)

Mrs Pallier insists on economic and social rights but is strangely silent on the right to institute proceedings and the right to property, which are nevertheless fundamental in the fight against poverty and exclusion.

That said, apart from the articles concerning children"s rights, which can be unreservedly supported, and a certain number of articles on freedom of expression and individual freedoms, the report which has been submitted to us is more or less composed of largely tendentious ideological affirmations, with a strong hint of Marxism‐Leninism, ...

(Mixed reactions)

... a criminal ideology responsible for more than 100 million deaths in Europe since 1917. Under these conditions, it is clear that it will be impossible for us to vote for the majority of the articles in this report, which mix political affirmation with true questions regarding human rights. On the other hand, we will support the UPE and PPE amendments.

In conclusion, we consider that such a text, far from confirming the European Parliament"s role as vigilant guardian of human rights, can only give an image of an irresponsible House which tragically lacks gravity.

  Le Gallou (NI). – (FR) Mr President, the symbolism is strong, and as my colleague, Mr Buffetaut, has highlighted, it is scandalous. Our Parliament made Mrs Pailler responsible for the annual report on human rights, an elected communist, elected to the French Communist Party"s list, a party which continues to hold a Marxist definition of rights.

So a lesson on human rights has been given to us by the representative of a group who approved of Solzhenitsyn being sent to the Gulag, of the Berliners fleeing the Soviet paradise being fired upon by the East German People"s Police and of the military invasion of Afghanistan. And the French Communist Party has not repented. They continue to defend their position, for in the municipalities which they govern there are “Lenin’ streets everywhere, that is, roads, boulevards and avenues honouring one of the greatest criminals humanity has ever known: the creator of the Soviet secret police, the Cheka, and the theoretician, as well as practician, of the use of famine to bring down the numbers of political adversaries or the reputedly hostile classes.

Thus, ladies and gentlemen, we are today in the middle of a debate worthy of George Orwell"s novel, “1984’. We are in an Orwellian logic where truth is lies for, under the pretence of defending human rights, the issue is really one of depriving political adversaries of their freedom of expression. For freedom of expression is not the freedom to think as everyone else does, but the freedom to think differently from others. As Voltaire said, “I do not share your ideas, but I will fight for your right to express them’.

Similarly, Mrs Pallier"s report, under the guise of human rights, relates, in fact, to depriving people of their right to selfdetermination by controlling their immigration policy.

  d"Ancona (PSE). ‐ (NL) Mr President, I only have a few minutes, so I will not devote them to the sad remarks by Mr Buffetaut and Mr Le Gallou who year after year use this platform to insult the rapporteur instead of sticking to what it says in Mrs Pailler"s report.

The fact that we have such a report on an annual basis is a good thing: firstly, because we want to show that we are a decent continent; secondly, because we are alert enough to point at others when their human rights policy is no good; and thirdly because, during this week of all weeks, we are not only discussing Burma, Senegal and China, which is terrible enough in itself, but because we are also looking at what is not right here, and a lot of things are not right here. It is right that we are giving this matter our attention.

It is unfortunate and disgraceful, I also have to say, that year after year we differ in our opinion on the definition of the term human rights. I think it is therefore good that the committee I chair has decided to start next year by agreeing a definition, since a new generation of human rights is to be presented in June. It would be good if we took a very close look at the wording which is used there.

But before then, I would like to advise everyone to have a good look at chapters such as those on asylum and migration, and to have a good look at the chapters devoted to racism and xenophobia. If this report is taken seriously and if there is a desire for the outside world to understand that the European Parliament is also prepared to look at itself, this report will have to be agreed on the basis of these three chapters alone.

(Applause from the left)

  Nassauer (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, we should not fool ourselves: this debate does not reflect any credit on the European Parliament. This is not because there is a stormy debate about a report, nor is it because we cannot reach a basic consensus on human rights issues. There is no reason why such a consensus should not exist. Nor should the concept of human rights be limited to classic civil liberties. The two major conventions on human rights already include social rights, so that should not be a problem.

No, I think that the problem is that, up to now, very few rapporteurs have been able to resist the temptation to bestow the title ‚human rights’ on their own personal political agenda, and to ask us to approve it. Mrs Pailler has not been able to resist this temptation either. I will cite just two examples. In the case of asylum seekers, which Mrs d'Ancona has just referred to, Mrs Pailler demands that the jus soli principle should be recognized without exception as from birth, and that dual nationality should be granted. There are good reasons for arguing this, but it certainly has nothing at all to do with human rights.

I would also like to mention economic and social rights. In this case, Mrs Pailler says that the extent of poverty in Europe, coupled with a rigid policy of controlling government spending, is aggravating unemployment. If you carry on talking about unemployment as a consequence of spending controls and of restructuring budgets, my dear Mrs Pailler, you will end up pretty much out on a limb, as you will by saying that the process of impoverishment is directly connected with the way the economy works.

It is easy to find fault with this criticism of the way the market economy works, but it certainly has nothing to do with human rights. Let us go back to human rights proper and talk, for instance, about what Mr Schulz said. I am referring to the psychological terrorism practised by the EU institutions, an interesting turn of phrase. Whatever does he mean? Mr van Miert's competition policy perhaps? We should talk about specific cases for once, then we might even agree on a human rights report.

  Caccavale (UPE). – (IT) I have to say that Mrs Pailler"s report is a very broad report, too broad even, given the problems this hotchpotch and jumble of laws obviously causes, which need to be discussed together. I should, however, examine several important points of the report, such as those relating to the question of prisons – an opinion which I fully share – and that relating to the abuse of detention and the lack of equality between accusation and defence in legal proceedings: these are all points made in this report which we share.

However, I am not going to join in the ideological debate on whether we need to include other rights in this report, extending the scope of human rights, such as economic and social or ecological rights. In this respect, I would like my friends in the People"s Party to know that this is not the point. I think that human rights are developing, and that we need to dispute the contents of Mrs Pailler"s report: for example, in the part relating to economic and social rights, the answers given by Mrs Pailler are totally wrong, obsolete and superseded by history. To say that poverty is the result of market policies does not take the actual facts into account and gives a contrary answer to reality itself. This is what we are fighting against.

  Wiebenga (ELDR). ‐ (NL) Mr President, I would like to join the chorus of those who think it is a good thing that we are discussing and making decisions about the human rights situation within the European Union. I would like to add that it would be important if we in this House could reach a large majority on this point.

In my view the report contains many good things – let there be no misunderstanding on this – but it also contains a few issues which stand in the way of reaching a large majority. I mention just a few, not too many, as I do not have much time.

A fundamental issue is paragraph 14, for example, which deals with nationality right, with the jus soli, or jus sanguinis. These are issues which have a constitutional dimension in various Member States and my question is: we might say we are in favour of the jus soli, but is it a violation of human rights in 1996, because that is the year the report covers, if one country has a different system of nationality right? Obviously the answer is no.

It also contains a few good paragraphs on immigration and asylum policy, but it also has the odd controversial paragraph, like the one on legalizing illegal foreigners. Non‐legalization in itself was no violation of human rights during 1996. I hope it will be possible that by amending the report we will have a decent majority.

  Ojala (GUE/NGL). – (FI) Mr President, Mrs Pailler has done an excellent job in preparing this important report. An annual report on human rights is something of a barometer to measure the current situation in various Member States.

Romanies and travellers make up a significant minority common to the whole of Europe. They have lived in Europe for centuries. Nonetheless, their rights are being flagrantly dashed to pieces still today in many states. Their basic economic, social and cultural rights are being ignored.

It is a great shame that the Council of Europe and the European Union are unable to achieve better cooperation with Romany people to improve living conditions for them. The Council is extremely knowledgeable concerning the Romany situation throughout Europe, but is greatly lacking in resources. By combining their strengths and resources the EU and the Council could achieve great results. I appeal to Commissioner van de Broek for greater cooperation.

  Ullmann (V). – (DE) Mr President, in my country an application for asylum by a young African woman who was being threatened with sexual violence and physical mutilation by her own family was rejected as being manifestly unfounded. In the German Länder of Baden‐Württemberg, the practice of deportation denounced in the report before us as double punishment is universally applied, even to EU citizens.

Injustice of this kind is not the fault of officials or courts, but rather the result of poor law‐making, which has transformed fundamental rights into privileges. However, anyone who transforms fundamental rights into privileges is simply abolishing them. And anyone who abolishes fundamental rights is undermining the legal system, which is based on fundamental rights, and is shifting the responsibility for observing these fundamental rights, as is the case with the third country clause.

I cannot understand why this House cannot speak with one voice in totally rejecting a policy of this kind, and I am saying that as someone who has read Solzhenitsyn and has had personal experience of the East German People's Police.
