  Seillier (I‐EDN). – (FR) Mr President, along with some legitimate aspects and very useful reminders, this report on human rights in the European Union has a number serious gaps and unacceptable points.

For example, the mistaken concept of regulated prostitution, which would be a true regression for European humanism, a humanism for which the body is not exterior to the person and cannot be the object of commerce and which, if it were accepted, would contribute to spreading the scourge of prostitution in all its forms.

Secondly, the demand for civil union contracts for homosexuals. Do we need to be reminded of the obvious? Life and the development of society, of any society, is based on the difference between the sexes. A homosexual orientation, which is an individual practice, cannot claim social recognition and thus generate individual rights which would create confusion amongst the young in particular, in terms of the basic reference point of the difference between the sexes.

Finally, where is there mention about the most scorned right in our countries, especially in my own, in France, at the moment, thanks to which we are all here, both you and I, since it was granted to all of us: the right to develop and to be born?

Truly, Madam, in such a report we would have liked a less selective and more coherent approach.

  Hager (NI). – (DE) Mr President, we would find it helpful if the human rights report dealt with specific cases and did not attempt to articulate a broad sweep of political demands. Its artificially over‐inflated scope simply weakens the report. The rapporteur's concerns are in many areas far removed from reality. The criticism of prison staff, to name just one example, is wide of the mark in many Member States. In fact, in Austria we recently had a plea for help from guardians of the law, who are overloaded because of the tolerant approach increasingly being adopted, and whose working conditions, which are deteriorating all the time, need to be investigated.

The inherent contradictions in the report are demonstrated by the fact that it calls both for better protection against dangerous criminals and simultaneously for improved rights for law‐breakers and for alternatives to imprisonment. Particularly at a time when we are generally welcoming the integration of national parliaments, I do not think it is very helpful to interfere with national legislative powers and make unjustified criticisms. For these reasons, we shall not be voting for the report.

  Elliott (PSE). ‐ Mr President, clearly we are going to have the same sort of problems with this report on human rights in the European Union as we have had on previous occasions. I appeal to anyone who has any degree of mental flexibility in the PPE and the ELDR groups to reflect on whether they ought not support a report which rightly emphasizes that human rights stretch well beyond fundamental civil rights, such as of freedom of religion and freedom of expression. Issues like poverty, homelessness and unemployment are equally denials of human rights, and we should say so.

I particularly want to refer to three paragraphs which deal with what are perhaps rather minor issues but are nevertheless very important to the people affected by them and which I am delighted to find in this report. Firstly, paragraph 57 refers to the plight of a number of domestic servants brought in outside the usual immigration rules by wealthy people, who then keep them in nearslavery with bad conditions and very poor – if any – wages. It is hard for them to do anything about this because if they leave their employer they would be deported. That is a situation that needs to be put right.

Then there is paragraph 89 which spells out the problem of children who are legally resident in the European Union but whose parents are not EU citizens. They often have considerable difficulties at border controls when they go on school trips and that puts them in an invidious position as compared with their classmates. That too needs to be put right.

Finally, I refer to paragraph 99 which highlights the difficulties which are often experienced by people held for long periods on remand in countries other than their own on suspicion of having committed an offence. Of course if they have, they should be prosecuted and punished but not held for long periods and then often freed because of bureaucratic problems like difficulties in translation or in reconciling the judicial systems of Member States. That is an important consideration too. There is very much in this report that deserves our support and I hope it will be adopted by Parliament.

  Casini C. (PPE). – (IT) Mr President, the first words of the Universal Declaration on Human Rights, whose fiftieth anniversary we celebrate this year, form the basis of freedom, justice and peace in the world as they recognize the dignity of every being belonging to the human family.

These first words of the Declaration have already been commented on many times to emphasize its originality compared with the previous declarations on rights in France, America and England, since 1215; this Declaration aims to be a peace plan and not just a legal limit to the sovereign"s power, and aims to give an answer to the thousand‐year‐old problem of justice that is often confused with the force of those with power.

It is therefore very important to examine the meaning of these words in depth. In my opinion, the report we are voting on contains many positive parts but also a negative part because it omits the fundamental question of who the members of the human family are to which these first words of the Universal Declaration refer.

Only by answering this question can we give strength and certainty to the principle of equality. We are all equal, but who is equal? It has already been said that wealth, health, intelligence, age and size do not count. In this House, however, we have to have courage to talk about humans at the beginning of their lives: the unborn children. In using this expression “unborn children’, I am using the language of the 1989 Convention on the Rights of the Child which states that “...the child...needs special safeguards and care...before as well as after birth’ in the preamble.

This language is therefore that of the international charters; silence is unacceptable! This obviously does not mean that we should not review the legislations that have given rise to disputes, but means that we need to affirm rights and at least talk about education, assistance and sharing. It is not enough to declare that we are against discrimination; instead, we must ensure that we do not discriminate between what Mother Teresa of Calcutta called the poorer from the poor!

  Andrews (UPE). – Mr President, human rights must be a basic tenet of the European Union. The case of Roísin McAliskey who is currently on conditional bail in a secure psychiatric hospital cannot be ignored and has not been ignored in this report.

Paragraph 106 of the resolution is welcome. The practice of tying up and handcuffing prisoners during gynaecological examinations or after giving birth is abhorrent. It has no place in our society and amounts to torture. It has driven Roísin, who I believe to be innocent, to the edge of insanity. The physical and mental suffering which she has undergone amounts to torture. She has suffered enough. I want to appeal to the British Presidency and to the German Government to release her immediately.

On the issue of human rights, I appeal to the British Presidency yet again not to break up the peace talks in Ireland, no matter what the provocation – and there has been provocation by both sides. Human rights demand that all involved in the search for peace continue that search by talks. I appeal, from the bottom of my heart, and on behalf of all Irish people, to the British Presidency not to break up the talks taking place in Dublin at the moment.

  Theonas (GUE/NGL). – (EL) Mr President, first of all I would like to congratulate Mrs Pailler and also to give her particular thanks for the comprehensiveness of the report she has presented to the European Parliament.

I think what has disturbed and is disturbing some colleagues is the reference to economic, social and cultural rights, which they do not classify as human rights. Let me say that the issue is not a technical, but an ideological one, a political and ultimately a humanistic one. And that is what disturbs some, while I, if you will allow me ladies and gentlemen, am disturbed by the hypocrisy that often emerges, even in this House, when we see the most rudimentary human rights being suppressed all around us and we do nothing about it. At this very moment a new genocide is being attempted against the children of the Iraqi people. When will we cry out? When will we clamour against it, granted that this attack coincides with the European Parliament's debate on human rights? When will we repudiate this attack which offends against human rights?

  Lambraki (PSE). – (EL) Mr President, I too in my turn want to congratulate Mrs Pailler on her report. The 20th century is drawing to a close and we see, with real sorrow, that the violation of human rights is not an unfortunate ‚prerogative’ of some remote and new states but, sadly, takes place in our own countries.

Ladies and gentlemen, daily life itself and especially the development of technology show up new aspects of the suppression of basic personal and social citizens' rights, related to the right to work and to private life. That is why we ought, if we want to retain a level of respect for personal rights, to broaden the concept of personal rights with sensitivity and an open mind, so that we can deal with the new forms of violation threatening those rights. And Mrs Pailler has contributed much in that area. We also ought to look into the methods and timing of our reports. We are often overtaken by events, with the result that our reports are not topical so that the level of interest in public opinion is reduced and, in particular, governments are given the opportunity to ignore the reports and avoid complying with the European Parliament's recommendations. Besides, I believe that the annual report should be followed up and that we should monitor the way in which the governments respond to our observations about improving the situation or going ahead with legislation. Today, when the clouds of fascism and xenophobia abound in our countries and threaten acquired democratic rights of citizens, the European Parliament must not remain content with an annual report. We must find ways to exert pressure on governments which ignore our positions and views.

I would like to say to Mrs Pailler, who has heard some voices raised on the opposite side, that at times Liebknecht must have heard similar things. He made a speech in the German parliament and was applauded by his opponents. And when he stood down, he said: ’how mistaken I have been!’ You, Mrs Pailler, have made no mistake at all.

  Lucas Pires (PPE). – (PT) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, the subject of fundamental rights is crucial and, indeed, constitutes a prime factor of the European identity and of European integration. In this regard, I am much gratified by three recent developments that carry us in the right direction: the progress achieved in Amsterdam, particularly regarding non‐discrimination; the designation of 1997 as European Year against Racism; and the recent success of Pope John Paul II"s actions in Cuba to further a freedom that is perhaps the first of all freedoms – that of opinion, and the spiritual freedom inherent in it.

While Mrs Pailler"s report is informed by a different philosophy, it nonetheless presents many positive points which we can support. However, in other regards, it goes too far, or adopts an approach that is not the most appropriate for a document in the nature of an annual report for the European Parliament, which should be more informative than prescriptive, more legal than political, more of a statement of accounts and act of transparency and more consensual, although no less rigorous for all that. Otherwise, it cannot contribute to forging a genuine European identity in such a crucial sphere as that of fundamental rights.

I further consider that the report should concentrate more attention on rights of a personal nature, which constitute the backbone of the European Convention on Human Rights. We also value economic and social rights, but tragic historical experiences have shown us that, whenever these are placed over and above rights of a personal nature, freedom as a whole suffers. It is dangerous to mix formulas for combating unemployment with fundamental rights. For this reason, we consider that such rights, and indeed fundamental rights, should be dealt with in a separate report. Meanwhile, it would make perfect sense in this report to devote greater attention to the right to life, the rights of the unborn child, the rights of victims and the rights of families which have unaccountably been omitted.

  Ford (PSE). – Mr President, I congratulate Mrs Pailler on her report which was drawn up in difficult circumstances. I would like to make four brief comments. I support my colleague Martin Schulz in saying that we have no option but to produce such a report on human rights in the European Union. We spend time every month talking about human rights outside the European Union and if we never look inward at all – never look at the plank in our own eye, compared to what is happening outside – we are justifiably criticized for having a rather Eurocentric view of the world.

Secondly, let me say that it is clear that when the Amsterdam Treaty is ratified, the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs is going to have a much more important role. We will be able to speak with authority providing we can reach a degree of consensus. I welcome the comment by Mrs d'Ancona, the chairman of the committee, that in future we should try to reach a degree of consensus in this report and not have the two political blocs battling against each other. It seems to me that we considerably weaken the points we make.

Thirdly, I would like the report in future to be more positive. There are good things happening in the world. For example the town of Badolato in Italy has welcomed a large number of Kurdish refugees with enthusiasm. The mayor of that town will be giving a press conference at 3 o'clock this afternoon. There are a number of other similar kinds of actions we could welcome in the report, rather than the slightly negative approach.

There are omissions. I would like to have seen a reference to the European Observatory to monitor racism, xenophobia and antisemitism which will be commenting implicitly on human rights in the European Union from outside. However, I have to say that I was amazed by the hypocrisy of some of our colleagues – Mr Le Gallou actually quoted Voltaire – when members of his party in Orange, Vitrolles, Marignane and Toulon are going through the libraries stripping out the books and the periodicals they disagree with.

Finally, I would like to comment on cheque‐book eugenics being practised in these towns where people are being paid to have white children. It seems to me that is also a violation of human rights.

  Van den Broek, Member of the Commission. ‐ (NL) Mr President, let me start by thanking Mrs Pailler for her report, and by joining those who have spoken appreciatively about this effort, but especially about the fact that this year, too, a report is being issued on the human rights situation in the Union. This does not mean, and this became crystal clear during the debate, that everything which was taken as a position in the report, was even shared by a majority in this Parliament. In that sense, what was said by various Members appeals greatly to me, namely the desirability of reaching a greater consensus in this House, both on the definition of human rights, and on the subjects which are discussed in this report.

Mr President, the Commission is not here to teach Parliament what human rights are, what they ought to be and to what degree they are observed or not. You know that the European Commission, like Parliament in fact, has very little to say on the human rights situation within the Union. But what does appeal to the Commission as well is the position that it is no good exchanging views here during every part‐session about violations of human rights in the world – and this part‐session will be no exception to this fitting custom –, whilst turning a blind eye to the shortcomings which are happening in the sphere of human rights within the Union. But I would like to add that we can count ourselves lucky that all our Member States, as members of this large Council of Europe, are bound by the European Convention on Human Rights. There is no treaty in the world with so many enforcement instruments for human rights violations as the European Convention on Human Rights.

In other words, if we have a debate here together, if we exchange views on these kinds of shortcomings, then we have at least the safety valve of the Council of Europe which should be a symbol of democracy and of the protection of fundamental rights and freedoms within Europe.

Mr President, I repeat, this does not mean that the responsibility of this House or of the European Commission, let alone the Council and its President‐in‐Office, is no longer under discussion. No, Mr President, we also feel called on to contribute something to these kind of debates towards further awareness‐raising about human rights, also within the Union. The Union is founded on the principles of freedom, democracy and respect for human rights, and, as you will remember, in 1993 the European Council laid down for the first time the provision for stable institutions to guarantee the democracy, rule of law, human rights and respect for and protection of minorities, as a condition for entry to the European Union. It goes without saying that conditions laid down in this area for new entrants, must also be observed fully by the existing Member States.

Like the previous report, the 1996 report brings up the problems the citizens in the Union, and all those who have settled there, are confronted with. But the report also formulates a number of requests which are primarily aimed at the Member States. Of course, the justification for this can be found in the fact that the Treaties do not assign general powers to the Union as such in order to guarantee observance. The Community for its part is merely authorized to implement Community regulations which are founded on observance of these rights. In the report a few requests are directed at the Commission. I will return to these shortly.

I would also like to underline what has been said about the universality and indivisibility of human rights: political and civil rights on the one hand, and socio‐economic and cultural rights on the other. This principle constitutes in my view the cornerstone of the efforts of the Union on behalf of the observance of human rights. I can therefore appreciate that Parliament has committed itself to dealing with these rights in their entirety when judging the efforts made by the Member States on behalf of the observance of these rights.

As far the Commission"s specific requests are concerned, Mr President, with regard to asylum policy I can inform you that we totally agree that asylum seekers who are deported to safe third countries must have access to fair, efficient and transparent asylum procedures. This will almost certainly be one of the conditions included in the Commission"s coming proposal for an asylum treaty. We are still investigating, however, whether separate regulations for asylum seekers will be required for this, in other words, without use of the usual re‐admission agreements. We think it is likely, and this is also the opinion of the High Commission for Refugees, that we will conclude that separate regulations will indeed be required.

The systematic consideration of the consequences of Community policy for cultural aspects is pursuant to Article 128, Clause 4 of the Maastricht Treaty which laid this down as an obligation. On account of this, in April 1996, the Commission approved a report on the observance of cultural aspects during Community actions. We are working on a new report at the moment.

Furthermore, the improvement of access for journalists and the public to data from national and Community institutions is also a permanent concern for the Commission. With this objective in my mind, information channels close to the citizen are given specific and clear information. In addition, a special unit has been given the task of improving access to information from the Community"s institutions.

Mr President, I would like to conclude by pointing out that the Union"s Member States must set the example as regards observance of human rights, as the Union regularly declares that this observance constitutes an essential factor in international relations. It forms the basis for European cooperation and relations with third countries. The Union strives for universal observance of these human rights, and it has proved this clearly by including in agreements with third countries systematic references to democratic principles and human rights, which form an essential part, as described, of these agreements. Moreover, the agreements contain a suspension clause for cases where there is a case of serious violations, and this theme is systematically raised as an essential part of the political dialogue with third countries. The Union will not be able to consolidate its action as regards compliance with the international agreements entered into by third countries in this area, unless a calm debate can be held on shortcomings, including those within our Union.

  Schulz (PSE). – (DE) I have a point of information, Mr President. In his speech, Mr Nassauer referred to part of my own speech. In any case, the fact that as coordinator of the second largest group he is not even here for the end of this debate shows how seriously he takes this subject.

5. Implementation of Structural Fund appropriations

  President . – The next item is Mr Berend"s report (A4‐0016/98), on behalf of the Committee on Regional Policy, on the implementation of Structural Fund appropriations.

  Berend (PPE), rapporteur. – (DE) Mr President, Commissioner, ladies and gentlemen, this own‐initiative report on the implementation of Structural Fund appropriations aims to highlight the reasons for the problems in ensuring that EU Structural Fund appropriations are routed from Brussels to the final recipients at local level, to draw conclusions from this examination and to suggest solutions for the new programme planning period from 2000 to 2006. Whereas the 1989 to 1993 programme planning period for EU structural projects was generally successful, the new 1994 to 1999 planning period got off to a hesitant start. One of the reasons for this was a string of uncompleted projets from the previous period; ECU 13 billion still remained to be paid out. There were also more high‐profile delays in adopting new legislative provisions in Brussels. These delays led in turn to a lack of confidence on the part of national, regional and local authorities and thus to a slowdown in the outflow of funds to potential final recipients.

The actual application process involving regional authorities and the project leaders only got properly under way months later. Some of the applications were of very poor quality because they were rushed. In many cases, this led to further negotiations which resulted in a further delay in the outflow of funds.

The situation has returned to normal since 1996. Appropriations are essentially being spent by the specified deadline. However, the Parliament wishes, by means of this report, to warn both the Commission and the Council of the risk of another poor start for the forthcoming planning period. We do not want a repeat of the situation at the end of 1996, when a total of ECU 26.7 billion had accumulated in the form of amounts still to be paid, from the previous and the current programme planning period.

The report lists a series of further causes for non‐implementation. For example, a significant proportion is due to Community initiatives which only took effect after a considerable delay because of administrative and resource problems at European level.

With reference to the current revision of the Structural Funds and preparations for the new programme planning period, I have reached the following conclusions in my report.

Point 1: decentralized decision making on regional policy tends to result in a superior level of planning and to improved utilization rates.

Point 2: it is essential, to guarantee successful implementation of programmes, to secure cofinancing, and regional financial autonomy is conducive to success here.

Point 3: an improvement is needed in consistency between national and European policies, with better coordination of objectives, strategies and measures between the various levels – European, national and regional.

Point 4: the time factor, as I have indicated, is particularly important. Regulations must be published at the beginning of 1998 and must be adopted quickly so that local, regional and national authorities have sufficient time to draw up programmes, avoiding a repeat of 1994. At present we are making good progress with preparations.

Point 5: reductions in appropriations by the Council for budgetary reasons should be rejected, as this is not the answer to the nonimplementation of appropriations.

Point 6: rebudgetization must still be allowed, and I believe that the tentative proposals in Agenda 2000 in this respect should not be pursued any further.

Point 7: it would be greatly advantageous if we could introduce the principle of a leading role for a single fund, Commission Directorate‐General and ministry for each Member State having responsibility and being in charge of implementation.

I would like to conclude by thanking my colleagues from the Committee on Regional Policy for their constructive cooperation. I would also like to thank those colleagues who made many suggestions for improvements, and I would also like to thank the committees asked for opinions for the contribution they made to improving the report.

  Chanterie (PPE), draftsman of the opinion of the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs. ‐ (NL) Mr President, I would like to start by congratulating Mr Berend on the important work he has carried out in the investigation of the current programme period of the Structural Funds. I would like to add a few aspects from the European Social Fund and on behalf of the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs.

It appeared that at the end of 1995, ECU 2 billion had yet to be allocated from the Structural Funds, of which three quarters had been allocated to the European Social Fund. An investigation, meanwhile, has shown that it primarily concerned Community initiatives, chiefly in four Member States which had a backlog in implementation, namely Spain, Italy, France and Germany.

At the end of 1996, it transpired that the initial under‐spending had been reduced, and that the European Social Fund was able to carry through a full expenditure of the available budget resources, but that the Community initiatives and the four countries mentioned earlier plus the United Kingdom continued to cause trouble. Six months ago more than 72 % of the payments for 1997 appeared utilized. This will stand to contradict the notion that there is a chronic implementation problem for the European Social Fund.

It should therefore be considered, Madam Commissioner, whether a transitional year should be included in order to provide a solution for these significant surpluses. So, on this issue of policy, the double question arises with regard to the spending degree of the European Social Fund: how can we stop the accumulation of non‐utilized resources in 1999, and how can we try to avoid this happening again?

Then we come to questions about efficiency and effectiveness, questions concerning objectives and area descriptions which should not be too tightly linked, and the implementation of the principles of additionality and cofinancing as far as the Member States and the regions are concerned.

  McCartin (PPE), draftsman of the opinion of the Committee on Fisheries. – Mr President, I want to congratulate Mr Berend for a well‐put‐together report that is very informative. It shows that the problem of under‐utilization which caused so much concern a couple of years ago has receded and is not so serious now. The Commissioner and the Commission should be congratulated on achieving a major increase, a doubling of the Structural Funds, without any significant increase in the cost of administration, and a lot of lessons have been learned about how to proceed with the next phase.

In the fisheries sector the main concern was the deficiency of the resources rather than the take‐up rate. In 1994‐5 utilization was very satisfactory, above‐average. Although there was a slowing down in 1996, we do not expect there to be any problem with under‐utilization in the main part of the fisheries guidance programme for the period of the programme. Problems exist however with the Pesca programme which is criticized mainly because it is too small to achieve the wide‐ranging objectives that were set for it; half of it is designed for the Objective 1 regions. It was intended to diversify economic activity in regions depending heavily on fishing. That objective could easily be achieved within the mainstream of our regional policy for peripheral regions. Alternatively, you could push the money back into the main part of the fisheries programme, develop more aquaculture and achieve better prices for fish through better marketing.

Post−2000 we would like to see one single global measure in the fisheries sector with a higher percentage than the 1.5 % we have at present. All the fisheries regions are peripheral and disadvantaged and the Community is only 50 % self‐sufficient in fisheries products, so there is plenty of scope for education and training and for the development of aquaculture.

  McCarthy (PSE). – Mr President, Commissioner and Members of the House, I welcome today's debate on the Berend Report, the own‐initiative report, which in fact followed my group's request that we do more work on this area and commission the research report on the non‐utilization of funds.

Any one of us here today can give chapter and verse of situations in their own area or region where the Structural Funds are not getting through to the final beneficiaries, thereby causing serious cashflow problems and even threatening project viability.

Mr Berend raises several key issues which require solutions for future reform of the funds. In relation to the perennial problem of match funding, we require an innovative and flexible approach by all partners and we may need more support for public‐private partnerships, for example in the form of European Investment Bank loans and EIF loans. If it is possible to build the Paris Hilton on soft loans, it surely must be possible to support more public infrastructure and Community business development within the same funds.

Commissioner, I want to take the opportunity to make a few comments on your proposal for an efficiency reserve because today is the time to do that, particularly with the ideas on efficiency and yesterday's report in the Financial Times on the EU's intention to reward the frugal. We eagerly await your detailed proposals and the criteria for this new reserve. However, to date we have had no discussion in committee on the precise details of how this will work and therefore it is no surprise that in our report we do not give unconditional approval to such a reserve. I would like to sound a few notes of caution in this area.

It is difficult to conceive of how the EU can set up objective criteria. It will also be difficult to have the EU as a final arbitrator of efficiency, and efficiency of criteria must of course reflect the operation and objectives of regional and local actors. It may be therefore better to take Mr Berend's view that decentralization is the agenda and let the regions and the Member States together run such an efficiency reserve. We are also concerned of course that efficiency may be linked to speedy implementation which rewards the big spenders without taking into consideration quality and innovation.

Finally, let me just say that we cannot give carte blanche to Mr Berend's paragraph 32 in which he intends to reallocate all unspent resources to Member States with the capacity to absorb resources without any clear criteria on this issue as well. We have to be careful not to penalize the local and regional partners who are the victims of poor administrative structures and I am surprised in fact that Mr Berend, representing a Member State which is a poor spender, is quite happy to give away money to Spain and Greece if his own Member State cannot spend those funds.

  Rack (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, Commissioner, ladies and gentlemen, for the citizens of the European Union, and especially for those less well off, the Structural Funds are the most visible indication that the Union takes the idea of solidarity seriously, and that the Union is more than an economic and capital‐focused organization.

In this light, it is particularly important that Structural Fund appropriations are implemented where they are needed. In order to achieve this, and the Berend report does an excellent job of demonstrating this, Structural Fund appropriations need to be well planned, appropriately cofinanced and then implemented in good time. In this respect, things have been in a sorry state. Overcomplicated rules and structures and excessively short deadlines meant that implementation was particularly unsatisfactory in 1994 and 1995. However, the situation was significantly better in 1996, and we can only hope that this will continue in the remaining years.

The situation has been somewhat different for the three new Member States than for the other twelve. Our planning periods have been even shorter, and in our countries the time allowed for implementation has been even tighter, comparatively speaking. Nevertheless, the three new Member States have planned their Structural Fund appropriations well and spent them promptly. I see this as confirmation of a point I made a little while ago in my report on structural policy in the new Member States, which is that there are also problems in the relatively affluent Member States that justify the use of Structural Funds money.

In this context I would like to refer to something else germane to the present debate. Member States bordering the CEECs are definitely among the winners from the European unification and enlargement process. But there are also problems in the regions bordering the CEECs, and there are those who stand to lose as well, there is therefore a role for Community and national structural policies in helping to solve these problems.

  Collins, Gerard (UPE). – Mr President, I consider the Berend report to be a realistic and objective analysis of the reasons for the under‐utilization of Structural Funds. Mr Berend says the Structural Funds are one of the most important contributions to the visibility of the European Union and to a close relation with its citizens. I agree with that.

That said, however, I have the gravest doubts that those same citizens understand the political and administrative niceties which were used to explain, and in many cases justify, the under‐utilization of available Community funds.

Solving the problem of unacceptably high levels of unemployment in the Union is a major priority for all our Member States. The rapporteur is right to highlight the fact that the Structural Fund interventions contribute to job creation, and yet there is underutilization of available Structural Funds.

The Funds serve to strengthen economic and social cohesion; they serve to encourage ‚economic activity in the most vulnerable rural and less‐favoured regions’ and, not least, to reduce ‚disparities between the levels of development of the various regions’. And still there is under‐utilization of available Structural Funds. The Council of Ministers consistently uses this argument to justify reductions in appropriations, and will continue to do so unless we get our act together.

There are those who would like to see unused funds repaid to their national exchequers: this is not acceptable now or in the future. In this context I particularly support paragraph 32 of the Berend report where he suggests that unspent money should be reallocated to those Member States which show a capacity to absorb EU resources.

The Commissioner will not be surprised to hear that Ireland is a case in point. My country has a remarkable record in the uptake of unallocated Structural Funds. However, the work is still far from done in Ireland: there will be a continued need for Structural Funds for years to come to consolidate what has been achieved and to finish what still remains to be done.

  Vallvé (ELDR). – (ES) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, I should first like to congratulate Mr Berend on having produced a good and timely report. It deals not so much with how the Structural Funds have been managed during the last six years, as with an analysis of how that management can be improved in the next seven years.

In that sense I think it is important to stress the need for decentralization in Structural Fund administration, paying attention to the principle of subsidiarity and the important role which regional and local authorities should play. So I think it would be a positive move to reward efficient management of the Funds, and I think it is a good idea to propose a special reserve for those areas where the Funds are well managed, and to talk of administrative authorities which allow for maximum efficiency in their administration.

  Novo (GUE/NGL). – (PT) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, Commissioner, this report fails to mention one of the essential reasons why the implementation of Structural Fund appropriations is sometimes unsatisfactory.

The budget cuts intended to obtain the results required to achieve nominal convergence in order to join the third phase of EMU has meant that some Member States are not fulfilling pledges corresponding to Community cofinancing. This has also led to delays in the implementation of structural activities which cannot and should not prompt the erroneous conclusion that there are no projects, no situations of need or, still less, that acute infrastructure disparities no longer exist.

The implementation of Structural Funds would be improved by reformulating macro‐economic guidelines, and not by placing inadmissible obstacles in the way of the automatic rebudgetization of allocations, or of the indicative allocation of Structural Fund appropriations by Member States.

  Schroedter (V). – (DE) Mr President, a partnership only works if everyone sticks to the agreements. This applies even more to agreements between Member States and the Union, when it comes to overcoming disparities between the countries of the European Union. We therefore agree with the rapporteur that quality should be the deciding factor. We cannot tolerate a situation in which the Council reacts to difficulties in utilizing Structural Fund appropriations in Member States by reducing these appropriations in the budget, thus preventing a uniform outflow of Structural Funds money. At the end of the programming period the Member States are faced with the task of utilizing unspent appropriations in a very short time frame. It is therefore important that in the new period local and regional partnerships, which are vital for successful implementation, should participate in good time. We must give them a chance to participate at an early stage, and given the delay which is currently looming in the new programming period, we need an interim solution. We need this in order to sort out participation rights now, because planning is already under way in the relevant offices and the result could well be that the social partners and the regional and local authorities, may again be confronted with a fait accompli .

I have one last, but important point: we need to have a monitoring committee in order to achieve the necessary flexibility. Its powers certainly need to be increased if we are to avoid a similar delay in the next programming period.

  Ewing (ARE). – Mr President, in my Objective 1 area those 12 stars on roads, harbours and bridges are the most visible sign to the citizenry that Europe tries to have a real impact on peripheral areas.

Structural Funds are often intended to address an immediate problem, such as the demise of the steel industry. However, in this case, it is the demise of a people we are talking about: half of the population of the Highlands and Islands was lost this century. The Objective 1 status is trying to address this problem and, for the first time, we have just begun to retain our young people in the area.

Mr Berend speaks of a smooth transition. As regards under‐utilization, I should like to emphasize that the Highlands and Islands have an excellent record in using up the money it has been given. The Commission will verify that. I would like to associate myself with the new Secretary of State for Scotland, Donald Dewar, who is fighting to retain Objective 1 status so that there will be the smooth transition that Mr Berend speaks of.

  Cellai (NI). – (IT) Mr President, the structural causes preventing a rational and satisfactory use of the funds include the far more harmful administrative and procedural causes. These depend on the quality of the administrations and their coordination as well as the quality of the programmes and their execution.

The allocation of duties and the subdivision of the three administrative levels make greater coordination necessary. The multiple nature of the initiatives taken, the complexity of the matter, the lack of specific information and the inflexibility of the Community regulations make a full awareness and satisfactory balancing of the Structural Funds by public and private persons extremely difficult.

The principle of partnership should therefore be fully reinforced to facilitate new solutions and increase cooperation and coordination at all levels. Its direct consequence will be an increased transparency of the procedures and a better adaptation of the measures taken in respect of particular objectives, with the greater involvement of local and regional participants.

Finally, I wish to say that I am totally against any reduction in allocations of payment to structural policies by the Council of Ministers. The problem of use of the Funds would not be resolved and this Council attitude would clearly be contradictory to the decisions taken in Edinburgh and would bring the actual economic and social cohesion policy into discussion.

  Walter (PSE). – (DE) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, ladies and gentlemen, we have 18 million unemployed in the European Union, and yet we recently witnessed the spectacle of over DM 1 billion of Structural Fund appropriations available for financing appropriate counter‐measures being repaid to national exchequers in order to solve budget problems in those countries, including countries which are normally regarded as the strongest in the Union.

For this reason alone, there are grounds for examining why these appropriations were not properly implemented. Why can we not move more quickly? What is to be done about this? Mr Berend has submitted a very good report with proposals for tackling these problems. What can we do? First, and this is one of the key points, we must make sure that a decision on future Structural Funds is taken as quickly as possible – we have to learn from our past mistakes here. So we must ensure that the next rounds of deliberations on Structural Funds reaches a swifter conclusion. However, I am a realist. I know how difficult that will be, but I think that rapid progress is necessary here. We as a Parliament can also give notice that we expect to take part in this process, at least in accordance with the rights accorded to us under the Amsterdam Treaty, if not more so. I therefore call upon all concerned to allow us to participate in the decision‐making process.

Second, we need to take a close look at the administrative and budget structures and improve them. We have seen that some aspects are in a sorry state. This debate about Structural Funds cannot just be limited to how much money is spent and where. If we are to be taken seriously we also need to discuss how a more appropriate distribution can be achieved.

Third, we need to discuss how we in the regions can reinforce partnerships and how we can strengthen the monitoring committees, so that money is really allocated where it is needed and actually used. I am sure that an effective judgement on this is better made at local rather than European level, and for that reason we need to enhance the role of the monitoring committees by putting them on an equal footing with the economic and social partners and with regional and local partners.

My fourth point is that we must of course create a system of incentives, be it in the form of a reserve, as suggested by the Commission, or in the form of a mid‐term review. We must ensure that appropriations are implemented and that anyone who leaves them unspent runs the risk of a penalty, for example by receiving less money. All this needs to be discussed and seriously considered. I consider this to be a vital issue regarding the further development of the Structural Funds.

  Varela Suanzes‐Carpegna (PPE). – (ES) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, ladies and gentlemen, I want to begin my speech by congratulating the rapporteur on the quality of his report, given the difficulty of precisely analysing the shortcomings in the implementation of the Structural Funds. This difficulty arises from the problems being so diverse – the fact that they affect different Member States, different regions, different funds, different objectives and different systems of administration. This makes the problems difficult to study, and also makes it hard to establish general guidelines.

Nevertheless, it has to be said that it is a very good report, which emphasizes – among other things – that during the 1989‐1993 programming period the payment appropriations reached 84 % in the Community support frameworks and only 70 % in Community initiatives. However, during the current period, after a disappointing start (1994‐1995) there has been a notable improvement in 1996, although it varies according to the instrument and the objective.

It is more satisfactory in the ERDF and EAGGF‐Guidance than in other funds – the Community initiatives show an implementation of only 90 % – but I want to stress that it is precisely Objective 1 which shows a more positive implementation compared with the other objectives. The experience we have gained should help to improve the current system in the face of the immediate challenge of reform. So the Berend report is a timely and valuable contribution, and we stress the need to establish a precise timetable in sufficient time, as soon as the regulations have been published, so that in 1999 the various administrations can adapt to the new programming. The need for greater concentration, simplification, decentralization, and cooperation with the social partners and the local and regional authorities means that correct programming will take longer, and that must be taken into account. Continuous assessment and effective control with incentives – as proposed – for those Member States which implement their appropriations optimally will also be positive measures which must be taken into account.

In conclusion then, I want to say that a lot of important work remains to be done, but the Berend report has come at a very good time and the rapporteur must be congratulated on that.

  Ryynänen (ELDR). – (FI) Mr President, in the present review of Structural Funds it is vital to correct the errors which have been responsible for ineffective and delayed utilization of appropriations, as we simply cannot afford to allow structural policy to become ineffectual. The challenges to EU regional and structural policy are ever growing.

The biggest problems are connected with fragmented management, bureaucracy and actually getting started on the various phases of a programme. Getting started depends on local partners being involved right from the planning stage. The principle of partnership really must be driven home and internal coordination in administration must be made better. We can no longer waste time and energy with complicated bureaucracy, where the benefits are eventually meagre and mainly involve training and consultancy agencies.

In rural regions which are being deserted, in Finland and elsewhere, the situation is especially critical. Unless the decline in population in such areas is checked it will soon be too late. The Commission is right on this point. We must give more weight to the importance of independent development for the regions.

  Sierra González (GUE/NGL). – (ES) Mr President, the under‐utilization of Structural Fund appropriations is a very serious problem, in view of the fact that difficulties in implementing the appropriations lead the social partners to seek expensive solutions based on intermediate funding, which affects the general effectiveness of the projects. Obviously this situation cannot continue but must change, and we agree with Mr Berend there. However, we think some of the measures he proposes in order to resolve this difficulty are counter‐productive: specifically, the measure described in paragraph 32 seems to us to be counter‐productive – the reallocation of resources in favour of those Member States who have shown an ability to absorb the EU resources which have been assigned to them – because in fact that means setting up a new system based on penalties.

I think we need to approach the question in a more flexible manner. Otherwise, economic and social cohesion, which we are trying to benefit, could suffer more harm than good.

  Howitt (PSE). – Mr President, the public hearing we held on behalf of the Committee on Regional Policy last year for the east and south‐east of England underlines the main findings of Mr Berend's report. Essex, Bedfordshire and Hertfordshire councils said current management systems actually deter potential project bidders. A very good project proposal on new towns from SouthEast Economic Development Strategy remained unanswered for over a year. The East Anglian authorities involved in the 5b rural development programme talk of a flurry of complex queries always raised at the eleventh hour.

The Association of Eastern Colleges points out that the annuality of European social funding is a bureaucratic nightmare, whilst cooperative businesses in the eastern region say that in each and every one of the last nine years there has been delay and underspend.

Member States and local partners must take their share of responsibility, but today we must call on the Commission to end the vicious cycle of delay and underspend and to publish evaluation criteria in advance and then stick to them, which has been a particular problem in our 5b programme. It must also ensure assistance to claim the funds goes to the voluntary organizations and do everything possible to break the mould and to ensure that the new programming period from 1999 starts on time.

  Hatzidakis (PPE). – (EL) Mr President, I think Parliament's initiative to look at the issue of how the Structural Funds' resources are used was a very good initiative, and Mr Berend's work on that important subject has been very good and worthy, I believe, of sincere congratulations.

From the work done by Mr Berend and all those whose amendments have helped this report, what emerges as certain is that, in general, with the passage of time, the problems have been confined to the utilization of resources but that they still exist. The problems associated with the Social Fund and Community initiatives are characteristic, and so is the fact that ECU 3.4 billion remain unused, a figure which speaks for itself.

The reasons, I think, have been given: delays in the submission of the rules at the start of the programming phase by the Commission, delays on the part of the Member States in submitting proposals, the large number of programmes, etc. We must therefore learn from our experience so far for the future, Mr President, and I think we ought to move in two basic directions: concentration and simplification, in other words, on the one hand, fewer programmes, fewer Community initiatives, unification of the procedures and mechanisms of the Funds so that we can achieve better results, and on the other hand, simplification of the procedures, although without going as far as an abdication by the Commission of its responsibilities, allowing everyone to do what they want without any checks and balances.

Besides what I have said, I want to mention the idea of the reserve, which is an idea raised by the Commission in Agenda 2000. It is an idea which we, in principle, believe should not be rejected because it could lead to greater competition between interested agencies to absorb the resources. The Commission, however, must make it more specific so that it stops being a carte‐blanche authorization as it is at present. Parliament will never agree to a carte‐blanche authorization. We want more details about this idea from the Commission.

  Ephremidis (GUE/NGL). – (EL) Mr President, I think we have to accept that there is something rotten in the Community's regional policy so far as the financial resources set aside for it are concerned. On the one hand, there are many very acute problems in many regions, and on the other hand, there is an unused and unabsorbed surplus of around ECU 25‐26 billion. That is where the anomaly, where what is rotten lies. This is pointed out in the report, especially in Mr Berend's explanatory statement, as well as in the reports by the Committee on Agriculture and the Committee on Budgets. Indeed, these last two add the special point that nowhere is there any mention in this policy of a proposal on the future of the agricultural world in the context of Agenda 2000, while the Committee on Budgets notes that little interest has been shown in frontier regions. I want to add that there is also little interest, amounting to disregard of the island regions. In that respect, indeed, there is a shocking exclusion, namely that the Aegean islands are excluded from the operational programmes in relation to transport, energy, communications, etc. And finally, Mr President, and I will conclude with this, the distribution is unequal. The regional policy seems to have discovered that Germany has become poorer than Greece and Portugal, and is for that reason getting a 45 % increase from the funds, while those two poorer countries are to suffer cuts of 17 % and 20 % respectively. That is unacceptable and unless it is put right you should expect no progress towards Community cohesion.

  Karamanou (PSE). – (EL) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, ladies and gentlemen, a Europe with huge discrepancies between its various regions in terms of their level of prosperity can be neither credible nor viable. For that reason, the policy of economic and social cohesion is one of the Union's most vital essentials. The Community resources that support the structural activities have produced particularly encouraging results in the Union's less developed regions, improving competitiveness, development and employment in those areas.

Today, there is certainly a need for the Structural Funds to become still better and more effective. Yet, to focus attention and effort on absorption and not efficacy is, in my view, a mistaken approach. It is known that in the past there have been parts of the Union which absorbed 100 % of what was available, but without at the same time showing any increase of their per‐capita GDP. So the issue is how to make use of the Structural Funds' resources in the surest and most effective way. Every ECU provided must yield a positive result in the areas of development, competitiveness and employment. I think that Greece today provides a very good example of the honest and effective utilization of Structural Fund resources.

We would, of course, like to express very serious reservations about the Commission's intention to place no restrictions on the management of 10 % of the Structural Fund resources for the next planning period.

  Botz (PSE). – (DE) Mr President, the rapporteur has quite rightly targeted his main criticisms at the considerable delays during the programme's start‐up. I would like to support him in this. There are a number of serious reasons why we should not tolerate similar delays in the forthcoming 1999‐2000 period.

First, the fight against growing unemployment in our Member States requires rapid and complete implementation of all European Structural Fund appropriations. Second, this will equip us to deal better with the substantial pressure to adjust that will be experienced both by individual sectors and also by whole regions. Third, the envisaged enlargement towards the east of the EU will be a real test of the European instruments designed to assist underdeveloped areas.

One thing is quite clear: we must heed the voices of our unemployed EU citizens and speed up this whole process, from the most local level all the way up to national and European level.

  Myller (PSE). – (FI) Mr President, in many speeches and reports it has been observed that EU regional and structural policy have not been entirely successful. Mr Berend"s report also points to solutions to problems. It is very important, as has here been mentioned, that the Structural Funds review be realized according to schedule. This means that the vital decisions have to be taken by the end of 1998 at the latest.

The report clearly points out that the greatest problems, linked, as they are, to this phase of the programme, are rooted in the delay in start‐up owing to hesitancy in making decisions. This must not happen again and this should be the clear message to the Council and others.

However, spending money is not the most important thing. The most important thing is the quality of the programmes, and achieving real, measurable progress through them. This is a particular challenge to those regions and agencies that hope to benefit from future reviews of Structural Funds.

  Wulf‐Mathies, Member of the Commission. – (DE) Mr President, I would first like to thank the rapporteur, Mr Berend, for his thorough study and his very pertinent report on the implementation of Structural Fund appropriations. The Commission is conscious that the rate of utilization was relatively low in 1994 and 1995, and that there is accordingly a risk that outflow of funds will be concentrated towards the end of the programme planning period.

This experience gives us good grounds for reorganizing the financial management of the Structural Funds for the programme planning period starting in the year 2000. Nevertheless, I would like to stress that, as you have been able to see for yourselves up to 1996, the level of appropriations still to be executed for Community Support Frameworks, which account for 90 % of total commitments, was considerably lower in 1996 and 1997. However, the implementation rate for Community initiatives is still a cause for concern, even if one of the main reasons is of course that many of these programmes were not adopted until 1995 and 1996.

The Commission is currently examining the extent to which reprogramming of Community initiatives is both possible and necessary, and has brought this to the attention of the Member States. In December I approached the Member States and asked them for detailed information on the implementation rate of Community initiatives at local level. In view of their capacity to absorb funds, we have to consider possible changes in the content of programmes and the transfer of appropriations within and between programmes.

In the middle of 1998 the Commission will bring forward proposals for redistribution within Community initiatives throughout the EU, and we will at the same time endeavour to take this Parliament's requests into account. A number of points remain unresolved following the debate on the distribution of the reserve.

Last but not least, as a consequence of major administrative problems relating to implementation, in Agenda 2000 the Commission proposed a dramatic reduction in Community initiatives by merging them into the mainstream programmes. The present level of commitments still be to implemented is by no means abnormal. It is quite logical that there should be an increase in the level of commitments remaining to be implemented, in view of the rise in commitment appropriations from year to year, while payment appropriations may not precisely match these. Nevertheless, in recent years payments from the respective financial years have also been increasingly used to implement commitments from previous financial years.

Unspent commitments from the period before 1994, which amounted to ECU 3.26 billion by the end of 1997, relate either to actions where an extension of the implementation period is considered justified and is approved, or to actions which have been suspended for legal reasons and which have not therefore been completed. As extensions of payment periods were only granted up to the end of 1997, we are now simply talking about final settlements rather than commitments which will continue to burden the Community budget because of their due dates. I am referring in particular to paragraph 30, in which you quite rightly request clarification.

Like Parliament, the Commission is anxious to work towards complete implementation of appropriations right from the beginning of each programme planning period, and to avoid any delays of the kind you have described.

As regards Parliament's concern about the large amount to be implemented once again under the 1999 budget process, I would like to remind you that the Community institutions, in accordance with the Interinstitutional Agreement and the Framework Regulation on the Structural Funds, is clearly obliged to make the amounts agreed at the Edinburgh European Council available to the Funds. I want to emphasize that here.

I would also like to stress that an extension of the current programme planning period would contravene both the Framework Regulation on the Structural Funds and the Interinstitutional Agreement. The Commission has therefore stated unambiguously that no overlap between the two programme planning periods will be allowed.

The Commission will be issuing its legislative reform proposals in March, and it will then be up to Parliament and the Council to consider and adopt these proposals promptly. The Commission will do everything in its power to ensure that the new programmes are implemented promptly as from 2000. The Commission therefore believes that it would be counterproductive, in terms of prompt consideration and adoption of the regulations and programmes, if it were to consider interim solutions at this point.

The Commission generally shares Parliament's views on the simplification of Structural Fund implementation. Further decentralization and greater participation by local and regional authorities and by the social partners right from the start of the programme planning process is therefore essential in the interests of greater efficiency. The new proposals for regulations provide for a clear allocation of responsibilities between the various parties, an enhancement of the role of the monitoring committees and for reinforced Commission powers as regards checks and sanctions against contraventions of the regulations. In this respect we are in complete agreement, not only with the requests in the report but also with the relevant comments made in this debate.

The Commission believes that it is necessary to create a performance‐related financial reserve as a means of increasing the efficiency of structural measures, and I thank you for the basic support for this which has been a feature of many comments here. Of course no‐one wants to write a blank cheque before this subject has been carefully examined against objective criteria which we shall be putting forward, and it is obvious that such a reserve cannot be a blank cheque for the Commission, which will have to follow transparent rules. However, I do believe that this is one way of complying with the suggestion that unused Funds should, where appropriate, be transferred to other parties instead of repaying them to national exchequers. I therefore believe that an indepth discussion of this is needed, and I would like to specifically invite the Committee on Regional Policy to participate.

The new budget structure proposed by Parliament, with implementation shown according to programme planning periods, would lead to a greater number of budget headings, which would in turn complicate administration, just as with Community initiatives now, and would be inconsistent with the aim of simplification.

To conclude, I should like to remind you that the Commission will issue its proposals on 18 March. We are convinced that these proposals for decentralization and simplification will facilitate efficient implementation of appropriations and the Commission will, as before, fully involve the Parliament in this process from the outset.

  President . – Thank you, Madam Commissioner.

The debate is closed.

The vote will take place at 12.00 noon.

IN THE CHAIR: MR COT
Vice‐President


6. Votes

  Falconer (PSE). – Mr President, I have asked for the floor on the instructions of the Quaestors prior to the first roll‐call vote. Are we now about to take a roll‐call vote?

  President . – I propose to take your point of order immediately, which will enable us to clarify the issue without interrupting the voting session.

  Falconer (PSE). – Mr President, I am acting in accordance with the instructions of the Quaestors. Will you please call me then prior to the first roll‐call vote?

  President . – It is not the Quaestors who are chairing this sitting, it is I. I therefore now give you the floor.

  Falconer (PSE). – Mr President, if that is the case, will you please record my presence in the Chamber at every single roll‐call vote in accordance with the College of Quaestors' note which we received last week? Can you also pass on to the Bureau that I believe that Parliament is sovereign and not some meeting of 13 individuals deciding for themselves what the Rules of this Parliament should be?

(Applause)

I hope and trust that every colleague who feels as I do will follow my example. You do not have to participate in roll‐call votes, you just have to make sure that your presence in the Chamber is recorded.

  Sturdy (PPE). – Mr President, my point is very similar to Mr Falconer's: that this is the first sitting of this House when we are going to have a roll‐call vote. I would like to see a booklet on the new regulations that are coming out by the Conference of Presidents so that Members can actually understand what is going on. A lot of Members do not know. We had a situation this morning when a vote was taken five minutes before it should have been. We should have some regulations so that Members do not miss votes.

  Ewing (ARE). – Mr President, I would like to associate myself with Mr Falconer and make the same point as he did. As the mother of this House, I find it contemptible that Members are allowing themselves to be treated with no dignity. This puts the Cash Office in a very difficult situation, because, for instance, a group could, at a midnight sitting, double the number of roll‐call votes that were held at voting time: this would mean that the Members would all have half their attendance money removed. Can you imagine the chaos that will result from this? So I wish my presence to be noted, whether I partake in a roll‐call vote or not. I should like to protest in the strongest terms and ask everyone: are you sheep or are you Members of a Parliament?

(Applause )

  Gallagher (UPE). – Mr President, I wish to be associated with the comments of my colleagues and to have my presence recorded today irrespective of whether or not I take part in a roll‐call vote.

  Killilea (UPE). – Mr President, as chairman‐in‐office of the Quaestors, I wish to inform Members – and particularly Mr Falconer – that the instructions he received last week from the Quaestors were in fact handed down to them by the Bureau. I wish to be quite emphatic about that. Let it be known to every Member of this House that we Quaestors were applying the regulation as decided by the Bureau.

  Falconer (PSE). – Mr President, Mr Killilea's remarks seem to be at odds with your earlier remarks. In response to my request to be called every time there is a roll call vote, and referring to the College of Quaestors' note, you said the College of Quaestors was not running this meeting. I agree with you. Mr Killilea has made it perfectly clear that the College of Quaestors was acting on the instructions of the Bureau, so the Bureau is running the meeting and you are in the chair. I repeat my request that I wish to be called prior to each roll‐call vote.

  Antony (NI). – (FR) Mr President, we have all heard of rump parliaments. Well, today you want to create a parliament of pawns. Every member is free to vote or not to vote. With the disputable and bureaucratic procedures that you are creating, you are making a total mockery of this Parliament.

  Santini (UPE). – (IT) Mr President, after this series of speeches, I would like to make a proposal. As I do not think we can calmly deal with and apply this new regulation in this part‐session, I would like to put to the vote the proposal to at least postpone the application of this regulation to the next sitting or even to discuss it at Parliament before enforcing it.

  President . – Ladies and gentlemen, firstly I would like to thank everyone who has spoken, which has enabled us to have a debate on this important issue. Secondly, in order to reassure some of you, I would like to clarify that you are clearly free to vote or not to vote. This is something which is fundamental to every Parliament and to every Member. The right to vote or not to vote is a basic right for Members of Parliament. Thirdly, I am nevertheless surprised that you should criticize this method of encouraging the presence of a large number of Members at the vote, for the vote is not only a right, but a parliamentary duty. I am therefore happy to note that I believe there are more of us here than there have been for a long time.

The Bureau will consider what has been said, being understood that it will re‐examine the question in a few months" time, as was stated in its decision. I therefore propose that we try out the system, we put it to the test, and we will see how it works in practice.

Recommendation for second reading (A4‐0051/98), on behalf of the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs, on the common position adopted by the Council with a view to adopting a Council Directive on the protection of the health and safety of workers from the risks related to chemical agents at work (fourteenth individual directive within the meaning of Article 16(1) of Directive 89/391/EEC) (C4‐0531/97‐00/0459(SYN)) (Rapporteur: Mr Blak)

***

(Following the vote on Amendment No 16, Mr Falconer asked for the floor)

  President . – Mr Falconer, as I indicated to you, I have noted your comments and, for each roll‐call vote, your presence will be recorded. Please be assured. But for the rest, I am not going to allow myself to be distracted by this kind of abuse of procedure.

‐ On Amendments Nos 10 and 18

  Blak (PSE), rapporteur. – (DA ) Mr President, I would advise voting against Amendment No 10 and voting in favour of Amendment No 18 instead. We made an error on the committee, and deleted a sentence which should not have been deleted. The sentence in question specified how information and training would be organised.

‐ On Amendment No 11

  Blak (PSE), rapporteur. – (DA) There may be some doubt as to the wording of the English text. We would like to amend “workforce’ to “workers' concern’. If we retain “workforce’, this means that the entire staff of a company will vote on whether an individual employee is to undergo a health examination. If we change it to’worker's concern’ it is the individuals themselves who can decide. So this is merely an amendment to the wording in that language to avoid any doubt.

  President . – Is anyone opposed to this oral amendment?

(The President noted that there was no opposition to the introduction of the oral amendment)

(The President declared the common position approved (as amended) )

Report (A4‐0017/98) by Mr Orlando, on behalf of the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs, on the proposal for a Council Decision on a joint action establishing a programme of exchanges, training and cooperation for persons responsible for action to combat organized crime (Falcone programme) (COM(97)0528 – C4‐0572/97‐97/0916(CNS))

  Orlando (V), rapporteur. – (IT) Mr President, I would just like to make one comment: this morning, many Members, starting with Mr Nassauer, noted the shortage of resources available for the Falcone programme and some mentioned the consequence of limiting the extent of intervention to the activities of the judicial and repressive authorities. The funds provided are very small – Mr Nassauer is right – but even if the resources are small, I think it important for the European Parliament also to allocate them to schooling, university and civilian society to show that the battle against the Mafia and organized crime is not just a repressive judicial and police phenomenon but is also, and above all, a phenomenon and a process of renewal and cultural awareness.

‐ After the vote on Amendment No 27

  Oddy (PSE). – Mr President, this is typical of what is going to happen now. Both Eddie Newman and myself pressed our buttons but nothing happened. With the new Rule we have to keep on interrupting to tell you.

  President . – Please be assured, Madam. This is not a roll‐call vote and therefore this vote is not included in our complicated accounting procedure.

(Parliament adopted the legislative resolution)

***

‐ De Luca (A4‐0040/98) and Wemheuer (A4‐0049/98) reports

  Samland (PSE), chairman of the Committee on Budgets. – (DE) Mr President, there is a problem. It stems from the two reports we have before us, the De Luca report and the Wemheuer report. Both reports contain an identical piece of text. There is an amendment to this text for the Wemheuer report, but there is no corresponding amendment for the De Luca report. It is therefore possible that this Parliament could approve one form of words in the one report and in the other report just the opposite. That makes things rather complicated.

That is why I have suggested that we need a separate vote here, and that has been moved. We need to ensure that the form of words is changed. The problem is that the House is supposed to be voting first on the one report and only after that on the amendment to the other report. It would have made things simpler if it could have been the other way round, then we would have known where we stand. The problem with Mr De Luca's report would not arise then. It would actually be simpler if you could let us vote on the Wemheuer report first. We would then know the outcome and could adapt the De Luca report accordingly.

  President . – Very well, if Mr De Luca is in agreement, I propose to move first of all to the vote on Mrs Wemheuer"s report, which will enable us to solve this problem neatly.

  De Luca (ELDR), rapporteur. – (IT) Mr President, I have asked to speak on this point. The problem raised by Mr Samland is not a real problem because both reports, mine and Mr Wemheuer"s, ask for a committee of inquiry to be set up. The amendment proposed relates to something entirely different, that is not opposed to a committee of inquiry; it asks for an examination to be made and for the Committee on Budgetary Control to be permanently assigned similar powers to those of the committee of inquiry. There is therefore no conflict.

That is why I am amazed by the request for separate votes on point 10 of my report. Yesterday, we all agreed in denouncing the Commission"s obstructionist behaviour towards Parliament. But then what should have been the logical consequence, the settingup a committee of inquiry, has not happened. It seems to me that Parliament is being rather hypocritical in assuming certain attitudes towards the Commission and then being inconsistent.

As I do not object to the committee of inquiry and the new powers for the Committee on Budgetary Control, I do not think it necessary to change the order of the two reports or to make a similar amendment to my report; but rather, I would ask for the request for separate votes on point 10, which is the most descriptive part of the report, to be withdrawn.

  President . – Thank you, Mr De Luca. I nevertheless propose to start with Mrs Wemheuer"s report, because everything depends on the decision regarding the amendment. It may be that the problem disappears of its own accord.
