  Hallam (PSE). – Mr President, I want to place on record that the new ruling by the Presidency has my 100 % support. This has been one of the best‐attended votes in this Parliament.

  Falconer (PSE). – Mr President, obviously I do not agree with Mr Hallam. Parliament has its rights in these matters. That is what sovereignty is about. On the first roll‐call vote I did not vote but my machine did. I do not know why that happened. Perhaps someone took matters into their own hands.

On the second point, you prevented me from having the right to declare which votes I wished to participate in. I would advise you that I participated in certain roll‐call votes, in particular those on the human rights issue.

Thirdly, surely you will take into account the feeling of this Chamber which, irrespective of Mr Hallam, is that there is something wrong in the House of Denmark – something drastically wrong.

Fourthly, I noted that President Gil‐Robles was not present in the Chamber during voting time. I presume 50 % will be docked off his allowances. Lastly, I congratulate you on the way you have handled this matter today.

  Ewing (ARE). – Mr President, on a point of order under Rule 163. This Rule makes it clear that any Member may propose amendments to the Rules but amendments shall be translated, printed, distributed and referred to the committee responsible which examines them and decides whether to submit them to Parliament. That would be the Committee on the Rules of Procedure, the Verification of Credentials and Immunities.

I would also like to make the point that voting must be the most fundamental issue affecting a Member of Parliament. That includes the right to vote and the right not to vote. At one point, Mr President, you used the phrase that we had ‚a duty to vote’. I dispute that. We do not have a duty to vote. We have a duty to know what the vote is about. Then it is up to us whether or not we vote. This is interference with a fundamental right and that is a very serious matter. This is not just an administrative matter. It has political ramifications which affect our right to be re‐elected by the citizenry. It should never have happened the way that it has. By the way, there are no exemptions for presidents in the chair in the strange note we received from our groups.

I would ask that this goes to the Rules committee. Immediately. You saw the feeling in the House. It is not limited to me. It is not dignified to tell us we have to be here and we have to vote. That is our decision and our electors will decide whether or not we are doing our duty, and not the Bureau of this Parliament.

  President . – Mrs Ewing, I think we nearly agree. Like you, I was happy to see so many of our Members were here and exercised their right to vote or not to vote. I gave the example of what a Member who does not want to vote should do. He should say so to the sessional services and proceed with the sitting. But I think you would agree with me – and you are not only the mother of the House but also one of the most active people in this Parliament – that it is a delight to have so many colleagues for such an important vote.

(Interjection from Mrs Ewing)

Mrs Ewing, you may certainly propose a change to the Rules. So far no change has been made.

Explanations of vote

 

  Papakyriazis (PSE), in writing. – (EL) Today too, as always, and as part of my participation in Parliament"s entire procedure, I am taking part in the vote – including the roll‐call votes among others.

However, today I feel that – under the “new system’ of checking attendance – I am in an unacceptable position: voting only in order to be seen to be “doing my bit’ and so to avoid missing out on any payments!

I consider that this situation insults me personally. I feel that the situation undermines the authority and political perspectives of the European Parliament.

I protest most strongly.

‐ Elimination of hexachloroethane

  Kirsten Jensen, Blak, Sindal and Iversen (PSE), in writing. – (DA ) The Danish Social Democrats have today voted in favour of the Commission's document on hexachloroethane in the non‐ferrous metals industry, because it is essential to restrict the use of these substances for the sake of the environment. But it is regrettable that the original proposal by PARCOM, which included more stringent provisions on the marketing and use of hexachlorethane, has been watered down by exceptions. It is very sad that this has happened, as a majority of the European Parliament proposed these exceptions themselves!

‐ Recommendation for second reading (A4‐0051/98) of the Blak report

  Titley (PSE), in writing . – I am happy to vote in favour of this report today. The protection of people from risks related to chemicals in the workplace is a vital health and safety issue, given the number of our fellow citizens who work with chemicals every day.

It is right that the Commission should assess how well EU governments have enforced indicative safety levels for exposure to chemicals at work. We need to see whether mandatory safety levels are needed to ensure that standards are met.

This report is also right to call for people at work to be given proper information and safety training on the chemicals they use. The best enforcers of safety laws are well‐informed staff whose own safety is at stake as well as that of the general public who live close to companies where potentially dangerous chemicals are used.

Nothing done at EU level should stop Member States going further if they wish when setting safety levels for the workplace. But our single market needs minimum common safety standards as well as open competition. Safe companies are usually also successful companies.

‐ E. Mann report (A4‐0025/98)

  Ephremidis (GUE/NGL), in writing. – (EL) Following the dissolution of the Soviet Union, Ukraine and all the other former USSR Republics have seen their industrial and social fabrics destroyed and suffered a rapid increase of their balance of payments deficit, with the result that the peoples of those republics are in dire economic circumstances. The immediate need to reverse that course of events cannot be satisfied by the continual granting of loans which, on the contrary, increases their balance of payments deficit in the long term and reduces their productive potential.

The granting of such loans, which mitigates the problem in the short term, is done essentially to enable exports from European companies to Ukraine to be paid for. The third consecutive aid package to Ukraine in recent years aims mainly and primarily to foster the export of the European model of development, to further market penetration in that country, and to control political and social developments by virtue of the very conditions imposed for the granting of those loans and the subsequent control of their use.

It is no matter of chance that this loan is being given – or that the European Union is promising to give it – just a few weeks before the parliamentary elections in Ukraine, with the aim of intervening directly in the country's political life and controlling the economic policy implemented. It is also no coincidence that the loan is being offered at a time when social conflicts are more acute because of the delay of many months in the payment of salaries.

With the experience our own people have of similar loans, we ought to say: ’beware of Greeks bearing gifts!’

Finally, the condition and safety of the sarcophagus of the destroyed portion of the Chernobyl nuclear station, and the continuing operation of the rest of it, must not become an excuse both for the European Union to intervene in Ukraine's internal affairs, and for the Ukrainian government and Mafia to milk resources supposedly provided for the protection of the safety and health of the citizens and the country's development; nor must it become an alibi to foster the economic war unleashed by Western nuclear technology companies pleading the excuse of public safety.

  Kirsten Jensen, Blak, Sindal and Iversen (PSE), in writing. – (DA) The Danish Social Democrats will be voting for Mrs Mann's report and assistance for Ukraine. The economic situation in Ukraine continues to be very difficult, and we in the EU have an obligation to help Ukraine solve its problems. On the other hand, we would like to emphasize that we are very concerned that Ukraine is not meeting its obligations to abolish the death penalty. Information from Amnesty International shows that there are 264 people awaiting execution in Ukrainian prisons and the courts are still imposing the death penalty. This is definitely unacceptable. We urgently demand that the Ukraine government abolish the death penalty and also take the necessary steps to improve the human rights situation in Ukraine.

  Lindqvist (ELDR), in writing. – (SV) I voted for the report, but I think that the loan should be issued after an assessment of how the programme for the closure of Chernobyl is progressing. In other words, Ukraine should also fulfil its part of the agreement on the closure of Chernobyl before the funds are distributed.

  Van Dam, in writing. ‐ (NL) Ukraine is struggling with a painful economic transformation process. The fact that the prices and exchange rates have been released, the government deficit considerably pushed back, inflation reduced and an extensive privatization programme started, is positive. Yet, in her report Mrs Mann rightly expresses doubt about the current economic situation in Ukraine.

Opinions vary about the progress of the reforms in Ukraine. The IMF was very positive concerning the situation in Ukraine, and therefore resumed granting credit. The EBRD, on the other hand, was much less positive and concluded that the reforms in Ukraine had come to a stop. Doubts concerning the clean‐up of the Ukrainian economy are thus totally justified.

Switching from a planned economy to a free market economy does not depend solely on economic factors. Above all, sufficient political support is required, and this is what is presently missing in Ukraine. The controversy between President Kuchma, who believes in reform, and the country"s parliament hinders the reform process.

I feel I should make a comment on the Commission"s proposals. The question must be raised as to whether it is responsible to give aid in the form of loans. Does this not mean that Ukraine might run the risk of greater payment problems in the longer term? We think issuing part of the aid in the form of donations should be considered.

This does not alter the fact that we were able to agree with the Mann report and with the proposal to grant macro‐financial aid to Ukraine once more, in addition to the support made available by the international financial institutions and bilateral donors. It is hoped that this will give Ukraine a little more scope to continue its reform of the economy in a more dynamic way.

‐ Orlando report (A4‐0017/98)

  Andersson (PSE), in writing. – (SV) I would like to thank the rapporteur for a thorough piece of work in an area of importance in the fight against organized crime. With this explanation of vote, I would like to underline my support for Amendment No 29 concerning special financial considerations for applicant countries.

  G. Collins (UPE), in writing. – I welcome the provisions in the Amsterdam Treaty which give the European Union, as a political entity, real power to fight organized crime and drug trafficking.

In a European Union, where there is an internal market with a free movement of goods, persons, services and capital, there is a need for increased police and judicial cooperation to combat drug traffickers who can travel easily within a frontier‐free Europe.

There has been a substantial degree of cooperation on justice and home affairs issues between the Member States of the Union since the Maastricht Treaty of 1992. However, these provisions have rightly been restructured and re‐adapted by the Amsterdam Treaty to take account of the changing nature of international crime.

The Amsterdam Treaty is taking the fight to the drugs barons on three different fronts: increased police cooperation between the Member States of the EU; increased powers for Europol (the European Police Agency) and increased judicial cooperation between the Member States of the EU.

While I fully support these hard‐hitting provisions to fight the European drug barons, there are also extensive and important powers within the Amsterdam Treaty given to the European Union for the funding of drug prevention and information programmes relating to the public health dangers of drugs.

The people of Europe must be persuaded that a vote in favour of the Amsterdam Treaty is a vote to give the European Union the necessary powers to defeat the twin evils of international drug trafficking and organized crime.

  Cushnahan (PPE), in writing. – The Irish Presidency gave a high priority to tackling organized crime. These proposals that we are currently debating are part of the action plan for combating organized crime that originated from the Dublin European Council.

It is imperative that we provide opportunities for exchanging experiences, particularly in the areas of cooperation, intelligencegathering and training for those responsible for confronting international criminal activity in Member States of the European Union. Unfortunately, although there has been systematic and regular contact between officials and civil servants from different Member States in areas of traditional EU competence such as agriculture, environment, social affairs etc., the same cannot be said for those working in the ministries of justice and internal affairs in the various Member States. This programme will help remedy this situation.

I also support Mr Orlando's recommendation that representatives of applicant countries should be involved.

Finally, it is appropriate that this programme should commemorate the Italian judge, Giovanni Falcone, who was murdered by international criminals for defending democracy and the rule of law against their nefarious activities. His legacy will inspire others to continue the fight against evil wherever it is to be found.

  Theonas (GUE/NGL), in writing. – (EL) We consider that the main drive of the combat against organized crime should be directed at prevention, and we consider that it is of prime importance to identify its causes in the hothouse created by the economic, social and political situation in our countries, with tens of millions of people unemployed, homeless, hungry, and generally in a wretched social and personal condition.

A one‐sided approach to the problem, that is, one concerned only with suppression, has not only been proved ineffectual after all these years of implementing it, but also essentially ignores, if not actually encouraging, conspiracies between the police, the judicial, administrative and other authorities, even at the highest levels, and organized crime.

The proposals put forward in Mr Orlando's report for the Falcone joint action programme are interesting, but they do not alter the substance of the proposal, which, being caged within the more general concept of the Amsterdam decision, is characterized by ineffectuality, bureaucracy, overlapping of responsibilities and activities with other joint actions. It also contains suspect or dangerous provisions which, if adopted as a new legal base, could extend the field of application provided in the Treaties and in international agreements on cooperation and joint action between the law enforcement authorities.

We are particularly opposed to any direct or indirect attempt to involve private companies and security organizations in the procedures envisaged by the Community's activities and their funding by the European and the Member States, especially attempts to involve them in the unacceptable procedure of electronic filing.

Appropriate training of the relevant staff to extend, control and suppress economic crime perpetrated via high technology, public competitions, the bribery or intimidation of certain professions and the laundering of dirty money by legitimizing the proceeds of illegal activities, are of course necessary. The exchange of experiences, to achieve the maximum possible efficacy of related national provisions and assessment of their application is also very important.

Unfortunately, however, those efforts, even when they are not made to mislead public opinion or for demagogic purposes, conflict with the implacable laws of profit for major interests and multinational enterprises, which are the governing factor of the orientation, design and limits of these interventions.

‐ Wemheuer report (A4‐0049/98)

  McMillan‐Scott (PPE). – I wish to place on record on the debate on the Rosemary Wemheuer report on fraud in the European Commission's Tourism Unit of DG XXIII my most profound concern and disquiet that, as this debate has proceeded, representatives of the Commission have continued to mislead this House. The statements made by the Commission misrepresent the true facts and represent a continuation of the cover‐up and refusal on the part of the Commission to accept that senior officials sought to avoid criminal inquiries by the police into their suspicions of wrong‐doing and maladministration.

This gives no cause for confidence in the actions that the Commission may take in the future and the record must show that, notwithstanding MEPs' reluctance to see a committee of inquiry, serious doubts remain.

I refer specifically to the confidential letter sent by the Commission's then Secretary‐General, D.F. Williamson, to the French and Belgian Ambassadors to the European Union which they have used to support their claim that they did call in the police.

The police in Belgium deny this. The letter dated Bruxelles, le 13 décembre 1994, amounts to no more than an official notice to the national authorities that the Commission was aware of irregularities related to subventions in the field of tourism.

As both national authorities have made clear to representatives of this House, the letter gave no instructions and made no formal request for action and, as such, was not acted upon.

The first clear complaint to the police was made by me to the Belgian Assistant King's Prosecutor, Dirk Merckz, in a letter dated February 11 1995.

  Trizza (NI), in writing. – (IT) Fraud is facilitated by the administrative mechanisms: the abuse of direct subsidies instead of tenders, unclear selection procedure, inadequate training for employees and excessive delegation of decision‐making duties.

The factors preventing the necessary clarity on acts of corruption are also administrative.

Like the rapporteurs, we denounce the attitude adopted by the Commission which has not just been late, but has provided a series of incomplete pieces of information tending to limit the extent of fraud involving real corruption within the Commission"s services.

Unfortunately, this falls into a culture of the public and administrative office that seeks to avoid criticism in favour of an image which, as events have ultimately shown, has been doubly damaged.

Public opinion is thus distancing itself even further from the institutions.

We therefore believe it necessary for the European Parliament, as a democratic, representative body, to actually have new powers of democratic control (we are thinking, for example, of giving the Committee on Budgetary Control powers of information and investigation similar to those held by the committees of inquiry) that would serve to restore within the Commission, which is now used to a bureaucratic culture with no transparency, the principle of responsibility that will serve to give it actual visibility and authority.

Apart from these considerations, we therefore declare that we are prepared to vote in favour of the reports.

‐ Pailler report (A4‐0034/98)

  Le Gallou (NI). – (FR) Mr President, the main infringement of human rights in Europe today is the infringement of the liberties of those who do not accept the dominant ideology, whether it be economic, cultural or media.

The main infringement of human rights is therefore the dictatorship of the “politically correct’, which limits the political freedom of those who want to defend their national identity. In France, Belgium, the Netherlands, Austria and Germany in particular, infringements of the political rights of nationals can be observed in the form of judicial proceedings for expressing an opinion contrary to the ruling party, political or politico‐judicial manipulation, the prohibition of public meetings, the violent attacks on party offices, the distribution of leaflets on the markets or in the cities by militants and the prohibition of unions which do not conform to the official ideology.

The state of human rights in a country is measured by the freedom granted to the opposition. In many European states, the opposition suffers from a lack of freedom. The European Union, which gives lessons to the entire world, often needs a lesson itself in this matter.

  Cushnahan (PPE). – Mr President, the European Parliament has regularly protested about the abuse of human rights in other parts of the world. To ensure that we can do so we need a significant degree of moral, as well as political authority, it is important to examine whether or not there have been abuses of human rights within our own Member States. There are a number of issues in my own country which are of concern to me.

Firstly, our treatment of refugees and asylum seekers leaves a lot to be desired. Secondly, I am also concerned at the implications arising out of the current industrial dispute at Ryanair. Workers at Ryanair are being denied the opportunity to organize and have trade union representation. The company has reacted to a reasonable and legitimate request with an attitude reminiscent of employers in Dickensian times. I am very disappointed that neither European nor national Irish law enshrines directly the basic rights of trade union membership or representation.

I would urge Commissioner Flynn to intervene in this dispute. He should convene a meeting of the relevant national authorities of the EU countries into which Ryanair fly to ascertain whether or not their national legislation can be used to protect the rights of Ryanair employees. In his role as Commissioner for Social Affairs, he can consider what other course of action he might be able to take to bring this dispute to an end, in particular as it threatens the social partnership in Ireland which has been so vital to the successful performance of the Irish economy.

  Frischenschlager (ELDR). – (DE) Mr President, I have voted for this report on human rights, even though the concept of human rights as presented in this report does not conform to my own precise and rather legalistic idea of human rights. However, I see nothing wrong in our including political objectives in a political debate and in a report about human rights. That is why I have supported and voted for this report.

  Ahlqvist, Andersson, Lööw and Theorin (PSE), in writing. – (SV) We would like to thank the rapporteur for what is in many areas an important report. We think that attention must be paid to respect for human rights in the European Union. However, we believe that before the forthcoming discussions on the subject, the European Parliament must ensure that there is a greater concentration of questions.

The present report covers far too many areas, which in large parts makes the report too generalized. What may be seen as justified criticism concerning circumstances which apply to one or more Member States, can, for other Member States, appear as unfounded opinions which lack clarity about what is actually intended. A number of questions concerning asylum policy and the question of not allowing local authorities to ban begging are examples of this.

We are strong supporters of a humane and generous asylum policy. We also think that the social problems which create a need for people to support themselves through begging must be combated. However, flaws in the asylum policies of one or more Member States cannot form the basis of demands for measures which apply to all Member States. The differences between the countries are too great for that. Similarly, differences in the Member States' abilities to deal with the problem of poverty must be respected in the view on begging. We would particularly like to emphasize our support for Amendments Nos 36 and 41. The view of prostitution put forward in paragraph 86 of the report is totally unacceptable.

Finally, we would like to underline our support for the report's clear repudiation of racism and xenophobia.

  Blokland (I‐EDN), in writing. ‐ (NL) Observance of human rights within the Union is a serious affair, not least because our credibility towards the states outside the Union would be at stake if we failed to observe human rights ourselves. So I think it is regrettable that today, as in other years, I had to vote against the report.

I did not vote gainst the report because I disagree with everything in it, but because, to my mind, its intention undermines the credibility of the Union in this field. Over the years the report"s contents have become so politically coloured that by now it has little to do with a neutral and fair monitoring of the observance of human rights as we know them from the EHRC. Classic human rights are lumped together with social, cultural and economic rights. Social and economic rights are presented through a politically coloured interpretation as fundamental human rights. This is also the case with asylum policy. Please note that I do recognize the right to asylum as a fundamental human rights, but the interpretation of this down to the last detail does not belong in the report. The Member States do retain a certain degree of policy freedom in this.

These are merely a few examples. Are the rights to divorce and to abortion really the most fundamental human rights which we would like to hold up to the world? Where is the right to life, granted to us by God? What kind of values and value patterns is indicated by this one‐sided approach? I could imagine that the developing countries might not be able to take us so seriously after reading a report of this kind. In any case, I, for one, cannot. All qualification is missing, and political party thoughts are turned into fundamental human rights. The notion of human rights used here is very different from the one I adhere to. We see human rights primarily as rights granted by God, whereby rights can never be claimed without honouring the notion of responsibility. That is why my colleague, Rijk van Dam, and I in the end voted against this report.

  Fayot (PSE), in writing. – (FR) I approve of this report for the most part, even if it sometimes gives the impression of being a cover‐all for every good intention in the world.

But clearly, given the worrying political tendencies in some Member States – xenophobia, racism and nationalism –, it is useful for Parliament to reflect deeply every year on all of these problems as a whole.

For my part, I would like to praise in particular the section of the report which deals with poverty. With 16 % of people below the poverty level in the EU, some 57 million people, according to the definition of poverty – having an income of less than half the country"s average –, the EU, one of the richest areas of the world, has reached an unacceptable level of misery. Poverty is linked to social exclusion, and thus to the unemployment which Europe has not managed to stamp out.

I join with the rapporteur in her vigorous denunciation of certain states, in particular Germany, which refuse to implement the EU action programmes on poverty. This does not bode well for the implementation of the recommendations of the Luxembourg Summit on employment.

I also join with her in her demand for a policy of growth and revival of purchasing power and employment.

  Gahrton, Holm and Lindholm (V), in writing. – (SV) The fact that human rights should be protected and respected is, of course, self‐evident to Members of the European Parliament. That is why we are also voting for the report as a whole.

Unfortunately, it also includes a number of demands and requests with which we do not agree, such as the following:

‐ we do not think that the EU should sign up to the Council of Europe's Convention, because that would require the EU to be a legal person, a state;

‐ we do not think that wages, social welfare systems and so on, should be regulated at Community level. However, it should go without saying that the Member States should make greater efforts to comply with the international conventions which they have signed;

‐ we do not agree with the report's view of prostitution, whether in the assertion that adults ‚...of their own free will, choose prostitution as an occupation...’, or that the Council should lay down measures and possibly regulate such activities.

‐ we distance ourselves from paragraph 127 which is much too vague and leaves a lot of room for interpretation, so there is a risk that freedom of religion could be restricted.

Generally, it can be said that the report contains a number of demands and requests which should not be seen as human rights, but rather as general political opinions.

  Kirsten Jensen, Blak, Sindal and Iversen (PSE), in writing. – (DA) The Danish Social Democrats in the European Parliament have today voted in favour of a report which takes the pulse of the human rights situation in the EU in 1996. It is a very good tradition that the European Parliament ensures that we sweep our own doorstep in the EU once a year. But if this is not simply to be empty words, we should focus our criticisms and look at how serious conditions are at present. It is very unfortunate that, in two of the matters on which Denmark was criticized, the questions are not relevant. The provisions on forced labour in Danish criminal law have never been used and are in the process of being abolished. The system of limited rights to strike for civil servants is also being changed.

Denmark has also been criticized for allowing exclusive agreements. It is worth noting, firstly, that the Danish have not had any referrals from the committee of experts of the Council of Europe, and secondly, judgments in Denmark have found in favour of exclusive agreements. Exclusive agreements concern the right to enter into collective agreements and the basic right of employees to be given notice of dismissal, minimum wage, labour market pensions, and so on. Exclusive agreements are one of the reasons why Denmark has a peaceful, efficient labour market with a record low number of working days lost.

It is therefore regrettable that the middle‐class forces in the European Parliament are pursuing a political populism in such an important report on human rights. There are enormous numbers of infringements of human rights within the Union. EU citizens face discrimination on the grounds of ethnic origin, religion, sexuality and disabilities. It is this discrimination which we would like to draw attention to in the annual report on human rights in the EU.

  Lindqvist (ELDR), in writing. – (SV) The battle for human rights is one of the EU's and the European Parliament's most important tasks. The rapporteur, Mrs Pailler, has done a very praiseworthy job in the area of human rights in the EU. However, the report extends the concept of human rights to cover a number of political areas, which tend to take the form of political programmes, such as economic and social problems. Even though we can agree with most of this, it could easily detract from criticism of breaches of human rights on the basis of the way the concept is defined in the UN and in the Council of Europe.

The programme should have been more concerned with the rights of minorities, such as Austria's treatment of homosexuals, the Slovak Republic's treatment of Hungarians, Hungary's and Romania's treatment of gypsies, and so on. An obvious human right such as safety on the streets is not touched on at all. However, I have still voted for the report, since my criticisms are of a more formal nature.

  Moretti (NI), in writing. – (IT) Sound the alarm to the people of Europe! The Council is carrying out a plan involving organized mass immigration to the EU.

A disproportionate organized exodus against nature is presented here under the false name of solidarity and respect for human rights.

The Union proposes the protection of the rights of peoples from outside Europe peoples and at the same time oppresses the rights of the peoples of Europe which are just as sacrosanct .

This Union decidedly defends minorities, nomadic populations, their freedom of expression, printing and information. All this is very commendable! However, I would like the rights and freedoms of all the European peoples and citizens who have elected us to represent them in this House to be defended just as decidedly.

All too often, the Union forgets the European peoples, the Irish, the Scottish, the Flemish, the Basques, the Catalans, the Corsicans, the Po Valley inhabitants and their legitimate requests for freedom and self‐determination.

When, dear Europe, will we begin to defend and safeguard the rights of our peoples?

I will shortly be presenting a draft resolution in this respect to understand and see how the Union will behave with regard to the rights and freedoms of all the peoples of Europe.

  Ribeiro (GUE/NGL), in writing. – (PT) The annual report on human rights in the European Union cannot be allowed to degenerate into the routine fulfilment of a ritual. For this very reason, Mrs Aline Pailler"s excellent report is very welcome since it very eloquently prevents this from happening. It is for its commendable intention that it was approved by the Committee on Civil Liberties and Internal Affairs by only one vote and reached the House with so many amendments, some of which seek to enhance it and many others which wish to deprive it of its essential character.

This does not mean that the draft resolution did not ensure that it would win the approval of the House. It opted to take a frontal approach, to provoke controversy rather than consensus and the possibility of arriving at a resolution that allows action and practices to be reinforced, with a view to improving the human rights situation in an area of the world where some people consider that they have the standing to give lessons on human rights to those who live in other places, forgetting that their own houses are made of glass.

This effort to ensure that the European Parliament arrives at a resolution that is worthy of it and that has practical effects is borne out by what may be considered to be an imbalance between the explanatory statement and the draft resolution. Care was taken not to include in the draft resolutions matters that might be inferred from explicit references in the explanatory statement, particularly as regards specific countries and situations.

For example, Portugal is mentioned several times in the explanatory statement – in connection with child labour, the situation of prisoners, particularly as regards drug dependency, and the excessive length of court procedures. But care was taken not to reflect in the text of the draft resolution any explicit reference to what underlies it. This makes it possible for anybody who accepts the principles and rejects such situations in the abstract, but who is reluctant to condemn them when they feel jointly responsible – or those whom the cap fits – to vote for the text.

Finally, I would like to emphasize another point that has emerged very clearly from the debate, both in committee and here in the House. This concerns the concept of human rights. We agree entirely with the perspective of the report"s author when she adopts the concept in its broadest sense and does not lay human rights on the doorstep of the economy, as though social rights such as the right to work, housing, education, social protection and culture had nothing to do with the economic policy selected in the service of very specific and private economic and financial interests. In the same way, we do not accept that anybody should seek to counter the rapporteur"s position by invoking an insidious symmetry which makes the human right to work equivalent to the so‐called right and freedom to enterprise based on the exploitation of the labour force, as though this were a right of some men and women with a status equivalent to the right all men and women have to a dignified life on the basis of their contribution to the social whole.

  Sandbaek, Bonde and Lis Jensen (I‐EDN), in writing. – (DA ) The signatories to this explanation of the vote fully support the contents of points 52 and 59 of Mrs Pailler's report on the terms of freedom of association and organization which need to be dealt with. In reality, however, implementing these provisions would undermine the efficient Danish trade union system and the rights associated with it. For this reason, we cannot vote in favour of points 52 and 59, although their content is otherwise good.

We also find it meaningless for the EU to ratify the European Convention on Human Rights, as it would put the EU on an equal footing with the independent states of Europe. As such, it would merely be an attempt to present the EU as an independent state, a development we cannot support. At the same time, for the EU to ratify the European Convention on Human Rights would undoubtedly be to water down the status of the Convention, and would mean that European states would be less worried about meeting its requirements.

  Vanhecke (NI). ‐ (NL) It is typical of the lack of this Parliament"s seriousness in general, and of the Committee on Civil Liberties in particular, that a report on human rights in the European Union is entrusted to an elected member of the French Communist Party. This remark is not intended personally; I only observe that the report deemed it necessary to lace this report with MarxistLeninist business which does not belong there.

In particular – in the name of human rights, if you please – a repression of freedom of speech for all those who do not agree with this multicultural dogma is argued for. Mrs Pailler obviously has not learnt any lessons from the communist ideology which she represents. On the contrary, she wants to guarantee freedom of speech only for those who agree with her. The others are at best allowed to remain silent, or are punished. When I read this I am no longer amazed that the French Communist Party to this day has not distanced itself from the worst communist crimes of the recent past, just like it no longer amazes me that this Parliament"s socialist group supports the Pailler report.

The socialists have always been the communists" perfect fellow travellers; the latter from conviction, the former petrified they will break the rule of political correctness.

On behalf of human rights, Mrs Pailler also wants to curtail freedom of speech within this Parliament. Like her colleagues, Mr Schulz and Mr Ford, it bothers her that in this hemisphere, for merely a couple of minutes, a few representatives of the Flemish Bloc or the National Front have a dissident voice. It is truly typical that it has to be a report on human rights which argues for the gagging of democratically elected people to this Parliament and within it. It is even more typical that none of the representatives of the Christian Democrats or the Liberals opposed these proposals. They may vote against, but they lack the courage to say so openly.

It is this cowardice which makes it possible for totalitarian regimes to install themselves.

  Wibe (PSE), in writing. – (SV) In my opinion, a report on this subject should deal with attacks on fundamental human rights such as the freedom of speech, freedom of the press, freedom of religion and freedom of association. Similarly, it should examine and remark on whether, for example, the right to legal representation or the right to be allowed to do non‐military service are fulfilled in the EU's Member States. But to do as this report does, and extend the concept to include the designation of European days for the rights of the child and for the 150th anniversary of the abolition of slavery, and so on, is not what this subject area concerns. The designation of European days is solely aimed at trying to create a common culture to facilitate the building of a ‚United States of Europe’. That has no place here. In an international context, ’human rights’ has a relatively clear meaning.

Paragraph 56 of the draft report condemns municipal authorities which ban begging in their areas. If you express yourself in such a way in a report, it means that you accept begging as an income‐earning activity for a number of citizens. I cannot accept that. Begging should not happen in the Community. Every citizen should be guaranteed a basic level of security in the Community through social services.

Paragraph 85 of the draft report urges Member States to revise the existing rules which permit forced sterilization, and it calls for a moratorium on forced sterilization until this revision has been completed. In connection with this, it may be observed that Sweden already banned forced sterilizations in 1975.

There are also a number of other formulations in this large report which should not be there at all, or should in any case have been worded differently. To express yourself as the draft report does and urge the Member States and the Council to ‚lay down measures to protect...adults who, of their own free will, choose prostitution as an occupation and possibly to contemplate regulating such an activity.’ In Sweden we regulated this activity until the beginning of the 1900s in a way which is in line with the report's intentions. It proved to be a totally unsuccessful policy.

  President . – That concludes Voting Time.(1)

(The sitting was suspended at 1.35 p.m. and resumed at 3.00 p.m.)

IN THE CHAIR: MR D. MARTIN
Vice‐President

  Corbett (PSE). – There should be no other meetings of Parliament taking place at this particular moment while we are sitting in plenary and are about to hear a statement of major importance from the President‐in‐Office of the Council. I gather – and we can see by looking around – that there are indeed many other events taking place at the moment. Not least a number of intergroups meetings: the intergroup on duty‐free, for example, seeking to undermine a long‐standing policy position of the European Union. Can you investigate this situation and make sure that in future no organs of Parliament and, if possible, no informal intergroups are allowed to meet at strategic times that we have decided should be kept free of such meetings?

  President . – Mr Corbett, you are quite right that no organ of Parliament should meet at this time but, as you know, the chair has no control over intergroups which are informal organizations.

(1) Topical and urgent debate (subjects selected): see Minutes.

7. Environmental policy following Kyoto

  President . – The next item is the statement by the Council and Commission on environment policy, in particular climate change following the Kyoto Summit.

  Prescott, President‐in‐Office of the Council. – Mr President, Commissioner, honourable Members, right at the beginning you pose a dilemma for me as an ex‐seaman. On shipping, I can tell you that I am concerned about duty‐free and the consequential effects of it. So, whilst I would like you to hear the speeches we have to make about Kyoto, I also want you to fix your attention on the implications of that duty‐free rule in regard to the travelling public. I will not say any more in case I get myself into trouble with finance ministers in these matters – but it has given me the opportunity to make the point and I hope the Commissioner has taken it on board.

I am pleased and, indeed, honoured to be able to address you today both as the President‐in‐Office and also as a former leader of the British Labour Group, a vice‐president of the Socialist Group in the European Parliament, admittedly in the non‐elected days and some time ago. Britain has the privilege of holding the Presidency of the European Union at an important time and in particular with regard to the growing environmental problems. These problems, as we know, are often man‐made. They can usually only be resolved by a common approach. Kyoto showed us how the European Union can lead the world in tackling global problems.

I was one of those who was involved with a number of other members in the troika and the European Environment Council, and make no mistake about it: Europe as a partner in those negotiations was a considerable force acting together to achieve a global solution. I do not think that can be underestimated. The Commission will confirm that was a very important influence in securing this agreement.

Working closely with the European Parliament, we believe we can make a real difference to the environment and improve people's quality of life in the European Union. I greatly admire your Committee on the Environment, Public Health and Consumer Protection with its constructive and very comprehensive reports. For example, the committee is giving a high priority to the ambitious programme to review and update the legislation on water quality. Indeed, I look forward to coming back to Parliament during our Presidency. With Ken Collins in the chair I know the committee will continue to keep the Council on its toes. (That's what I was to say, Ken, was not it?)

(Laughter)

We have taken three issues of real concern to the people of Europe as key themes for the British Presidency: jobs, crime and the environment. We want to place the environmental considerations at the heart of the European Union's decision‐making process. Integrating environmental issues into all policy areas of the European Union will be a major task but one that we have to face. It will be an exciting process and in the long term more effective in bringing greater accountability. It will challenge a great deal of the traditional thinking about our decision‐making. That is the same whether in national parliaments or in the decision‐making process of the European Union. We have made a big step forward with the Amsterdam Treaty and I look forward to Commissioner Bjerregaard reporting at the Cardiff European Council in June. I shall return to this important issue later.

We have also identified six major environmental issues to move forward on during our Presidency: transport and the environment – a matter I am very much concerned with as the Secretary of State in my own country for transport and environment, amongst the many other titles I am given from time to time; air pollution; biodiversity; fisheries; enlargement of the European Union; and climate change.

Today, in this debate, I shall focus on climate change but will touch briefly on other issues. As far as transport and the environment are concerned, the new government in Britain has merged our two departments. We believe we can deliver a more sustainable transport policy. I have extended this initiative to our Presidency by planning to hold a joint transport and environment council in June, preceded by an informal session in April in Chester.

This morning in Strasbourg I saw for myself how an impressive tram system tackles the problems of congestion and pollution that afflict so many of our towns and cities and encourages greater use of public transport and less use of the private car. We need to pursue a twin‐track approach. We need to reduce the environmental impact of car use. That is why the Auto‐Oil programme will make a major impact on reducing emissions.

The Presidency will also launch a new initiative at our informal council in Chester. This will encourage low‐emission or noemission vehicles. It will bring together European cities prepared to give preferential access rights to these vehicles. We will also hold an exhibition of clean car technology in Chester. We are all agreed that it is essential to cut CO2 emissions from transport, which are growing faster than in any other sector. We must progress the Commission's strategy to reduce CO2 emissions from new cars.

However, the Commission's negotiations for a voluntary agreement from the European car industry remain deadlocked. The industry's offer to reduce emissions is inadequate and we have to make that clear. If the voluntary approach cannot be made to work, we have said that we will consider regulatory or fiscal alternatives. I am delighted that Michael Meacher, the British Minister of the Environment, and the Commissioner will be here tomorrow for a workshop on CO2 emissions from cars.

Our second issue is air pollution. To achieve a reduction, action must be taken at Community, national and local levels and the air quality daughter directive is a main priority, as is the Auto‐Oil programme that I have already mentioned. As for acid rain, further Community measures are needed to reduce the sulphur content of certain liquid fuels. Reaching agreement will be one of the priorities for our June Environment Council.

The third issue we wish to progress is that of biodiversity on which we now have a European Union strategy. We will discuss this at the March Environment Council as we prepare for the fourth meeting of the Conference of parties in Bratislava in May.

With regard to fisheries, environmental integration is absolutely important. We must review the action taken to follow up commitments made at the March 1997 meeting of the North Sea Fisheries and Environmental Ministers. This will include steps to stop over‐fishing in the North Sea, and it is particularly important in this, the International Year of the Ocean, to phase out the use of high seas drift nets which kill so many dolphins and other non‐target species.

(Applause)

Our fifth issue, namely enlargement, will focus on the integration of environmental considerations during those negotiations.

Finally, on the most important issue, that of climate change, I would like to comment on the talks held in Kyoto last December. As Parliament itself predicted, the Kyoto Conference was indeed a watershed in the global climate change debate. Seeking consensus between 160 countries on issues which could not be decided by a vote was always difficult, as I am sure people are well aware in a democratic assembly such as this. That is why we had a lot of pre‐discussions. The Commissioner and many of our colleagues held talks with key countries prior to Kyoto. I myself travelled twice around the globe meeting leaders of the developed and developing countries and was criticised by the NGOs for creating so much pollution.

In Kyoto, despite the positions of the major players starting poles apart, as indeed they were, after gruelling negotiations we came away with what I believe is a historic agreement. It was not everything that we wanted, but it was an important, historic first step. We should not underestimate that, nor the role that the European Union played in bringing that about. Legally binding reductions of more than 5 % in the emissions of greenhouse gases by the developed countries was agreed. And Europe, as I said, can be proud of the leading role it played. The Troika ministers and the Commissioner had tremendous support and back‐up from our officials. Please do not underestimate: we had the best official back‐up team that any negotiating body could have had. I would like to place on record our great appreciation because they played no small part in bringing about that agreement.

I would also like to pay tribute to all the European Union ministers at Kyoto – the environment ministers – whose solidarity and commitment played such a vital role in Europe's contribution to securing a successful agreement. It was not easy going; we were not all agreed; we had different points of view. There was one occasion when the Japanese minister was complaining to me that, ’you do not understand, Mr Prescott’, when I was trying to force him along with the Troika to a better deal, that ‚I have to explain it to my Prime Minister’. I had to explain that I had 15 representatives of 15 Prime Ministers in the room next door and that was a much more difficult proposition. But their solidarity was a key factor in getting across a united European approach that gave us the leading position in those environmental discussions and agreements. Indeed, when you look at it, the United States was persuaded to move from zero – when most people said they would not move from that, that it was their bottom line. They moved to 7 %. Canada and Japan moved from a 2.5 % to a 6 % cut and the European Union set the top standard with a cut of 8 %, a target which was actually adopted by a total of 26 of the other 38 countries who took part in the targets. They adopted our target and because they did, we were able to make a substantial cut in greenhouse gases over the period of time for this negotiation and agreement.

Success at Kyoto was down to consensus among governments that climate change is real and that something has got to be done about it. Nobody questions any more the science. It is clear that we have to do something. That was particularly clear at Kyoto. As I said before, it is only a first step, but an important first step. It is nowhere near sufficient to tackle the problem in the longer term. It falls to the British Presidency and our successors, Austria, to prepare for the important fourth Climate Change Conference of the Parties in Buenos Aires in November.

The first priority must be to complete the unfinished business of Kyoto, particularly the rules for the flexibilities of the Protocol. This is still the most important role that the European Union has to make. Emission trading, carbon sinks, joint implementation, the modalities of a clean, development mechanism all need to be worked out and agreed. There is great suspicion that these flexibilities might provide the opportunities for certain big countries to trade off their obligations without doing something to their domestic economies. The European Union needs to make clear that that is an unacceptable proposition; that every country has an obligation – particularly developed nations – to make that contribution to the cuts in their own domestic economies. Therefore we must ensure that these deliver real, cost‐effective and verifiable environmental benefits and do not create loopholes to be exploited and abused and which undermine the objectives of the Protocol. It is my view that if these rules are not satisfactory, countries will refuse to ratify. We will not achieve what got off to such a brave start at Kyoto if we do not have the courage to make clear that this is not a back door; these are not loopholes to avoid the commitment to make the changes in our domestic economy to achieve the reduction in greenhouse gases in order to secure the climatic change benefits that will come from it.

Indeed this is why we pushed the European initiative – what we call the window of credibility – to buy time for a proper framework to be put into place. The concept of a window of credibility stems from our realization that these rules could not be agreed at Kyoto in time, but that the period from signing in March to ratification, however long that may be, gives us an opportunity to work out these rules. The next challenge is to see if we can achieve this within the window of credibility. We now have to prove that those rules are workable.

Beyond that, we need to start discussing with developing countries how their emissions can be reduced in a way that does not conflict with their aspirations for economic growth and eradication of poverty. There is much suspicion in the developing countries that we wish to impose a different level of economic growth and a different process of industrialization to their disadvantage. But many of those countries can see that the production of greenhouse gases, that comes with the early stages of industrialization, contributes as deadly a climate change challenge as they do to developed countries. We have made a start by showing we are prepared to take action. But make no mistake: developing countries growing like China have their contribution to make. That will be an important matter for us to look at. It was right that we, as developed countries, took the lead at Kyoto in committing ourselves to those legally binding targets. But it is a fact that China will overtake the United States as the largest emitter of greenhouse gases in the next 20 to 30 years. We have to work out how to handle developing countries' emissions in a very fair way. The European Union, therefore, has an opportunity to act as a broker between the developed and the developing countries. The UK will be using the Presidency to drive this challenging agenda forward in the European Union over the coming months.

We will also be hosting various major international meetings: the European Union‐ASEAN Summit in April; the G8 Summit in May; and the EU‐US Summit in the same month. We will ensure that climate change is high on the agenda for discussion.

For the two Environmental Councils during our Presidency we have three priorities on climate change. First, we must share out the EU 8 % reduction target between Member States under the rules of our European bubble. I will bring forward a proposal for agreement at the June Council.

Secondly, we must decide which policies and measures are necessary at the European level to meet that 8 % target. Setting a target is one thing; getting the policies implemented to achieve it is quite another. We have to begin to face that. The Commission's strategy will be absolutely critical here.

Thirdly, we must prepare the European Union position for the international negotiations with Buenos Aires later this year. It will cover the unfinished business from Kyoto. Issues such as emissions trading, the clean‐development mechanisms, sinks and joint implementations sound like a range of sound‐bites, but they are absolutely critical if Kyoto is to be translated from a statement of aims and legal objectives and targets to one of actual implementation for the achievement of reducing greenhouse gas emissions in order to secure the environmental changes we are committed to achieve.

I also hope that the Community can sign the Protocol during our Presidency. The Commission is bringing forward a proposal for this. Ratification is a longer‐term issue and will depend, amongst other things, on satisfactory progress on issues such as the rules on trading. There is much to be done but, with the close cooperation, and indeed a shared purpose, which exists between the Member States, together with the Commission and other Community institutions, we can and must succeed.

Kyoto will be remembered as a place where the world faced a crucial decision, made the right choice and demonstrated the willingness to take collective action and to recognize also that this world does not just belong to our generation: we hold it in trust for our children's children. That is the obligation: to pass on a world that is safe in sustainable environment circumstances.

Climate change shows just how important it is for the environment issue to be addressed across a wide range of policies. It underlines the importance of the new strategy which I mentioned earlier, and which I wish to say a couple of words about. This is almost a challenge to our decision‐making processes. We can set the targets, we can establish the policies, we can achieve those objectives we have set for ourselves. But there is also, as with change, a challenge to our whole decision‐making process. We can do better by changing our decision‐making process as much as by changing policies and establishing higher targets.

The Amsterdam Treaty recognized this and introduced a new requirement for the integration of the environment and other policies. I recall my early days here in the late 1970s as a Member of this institution. We then used to argue whether it was called an assembly or a parliament. I will not get into that argument now: it is clearly called the European Parliament. I remember the debates of that time when we talked about regional policy, and I belonged to a fishing port that had lost all its fishing possibilities by joining the European Community: we lost our distant waters. We wanted to restructure the fishing industry in Hull.

I could go to one department which would tell me that I could have redundancy money for fishing, something for training, something for restructuring, and something for looking to some other facility, but we were unable to bring them together in an integrated way that could develop the assets which were being made redundant by that industrial change.

That is the challenge for us on the environmental issues. The Community already has a fifth environmental action programme which does some of this. I know that a lot more has been done since those times and I congratulate Parliament on what it has done. There is likely to be a successor after the year 2000 with regard to the fifth environmental action programme.

In the meantime, the United Kingdom Presidency is ensuring that the environment is discussed in such Councils as agriculture, fisheries and energy and it is holding a joint Environment/Transport Council which will begin to integrate the necessary policies to achieve the targets that we have set and establish what we have to do in those policies that will be decided by the European Union.

The Heads of Government have asked the Commission to produce a specific strategy on integration and present it to the Cardiff Summit in June, which the Commissioner will do. I know Commissioner Bjerregaard is working on this and I would like to express my deepest appreciation for the Commission and for the role that she and her department played in the settlement of the Kyoto agreement. It was a very good example of how the political forces and the Commission can work together to achieve an objective, not only to the benefit of Europe, but a better global solution as well.

Therefore, in my view, a strategy that will give high‐level political impetus to integration is needed. I expect that it will highlight some of the major areas of policy where the environment has to be fully integrated. Areas like climate change, energy and transport, for example, are so integrated you cannot make a decision in one area without implications for the other. It is important we understand the integrated process under which you can achieve a far higher objective than by dealing with them separately. It is my hope that we will also be suggesting a new integrated mechanism to carry things forward.

This strategy can be seen as phase 1, and we will need a phase 2 and phase 3. We need to have something to ensure that the Heads of Government and the Council of Ministers return to this regularly and can be judged upon their actions. It could be a form of a rolling programme of environmental audit, and I am looking forward to discussing this with the Commissioner. I also hope to discuss it with the next three presidencies taking us up to the year 2000. Those who give political leadership must see the whole picture and make the connections between the economic, environment and social issues. We have to break out of the habit of thinking in a fragmented way.

We have set ourselves a demanding Presidency agenda, as indeed each Presidency rightly claims. But this reflects the high importance we attach to tackling environmental matters.

Success will depend very much on the relationship between the Commission, the Council and this Parliament. I hope this will be a close and productive relationship. I have every reason to believe that it will be. I am glad to have had this opportunity to speak to you today and thank you for hearing me.

(Loud applause)

  Bjerregaard, Member of the Commission. – (DA) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, I would like to start by thanking John Prescott for his comments on the Presidency's priorities on the environment. There are many of these, and they are important, and I am very happy about working together. But I would also like to take this opportunity to thank John Prescott for his contribution to the climate conference in Kyoto. He gave the climate debate colour and substance, and with his personal commitment, he helped achieve the results which we are now discussing a second time here in Parliament. I had – as you will remember – an opportunity to discuss the results immediately after we came back in December. I gave my initial assessment of what we had achieved and I also felt we had a good debate, so I will try and avoid repeating what we discussed then.

It is clear that the Protocol is the first decisive step towards a global solution of the climate problem. It is the first time the industrialized nations have approved a text which contains legally binding limits on their emissions of greenhouse gases, and so now, as we are trying to do, the next step is to turn the Protocol into law and ensure that the tools for implementing it are effective. As you will know, this assumes the major industrial nations ratify the agreement.

It is important – as John Prescott emphasized – that we do not forget the developing countries in this process. The Kyoto conference affects them too. As we are aware, they are not at fault in the present accumulation of greenhouse gases, but of course they also have to live with the consequences of our policy, and so these countries are clear about the need for a viable development and expect – rightly so, I think – that the industrialized nations will take the first step. But, apart from ratification and any considerations as to when the appropriate time for such ratification is in political terms, there is still a lot of uncertainty which must be clarified.

So what has happened since then, and what are we doing to ensure that the follow‐up to Kyoto is fast and effective? The first thing to do is to prepare for the Community's signing of the Protocol. The Protocol will be available for signature from 16 March 1998 to 15 March 1999. And it is vital that, as the leaders in the negotiations, we Europeans ensure that the Community signs the Protocol as soon as possible. In doing so, we will be sending the right signals out to the other parties involved, some of whom of course may be inclined to take their time over things. It will also give industry and other economic players, as well as the Community's citizens, more clarity about the situation. The Commission has a proposal ready for signature, and it will be presented to the Council in good time for the Ministers to approve it at the next Environmental Council on 23 March. And of course we must hope that Member States also sign the Protocol soon.

The positive results we achieved at Kyoto were largely due to the EU's leadership, and – as we have emphasized here today – the outstanding cooperation between the troika and the Member States. It is important to sustain this leadership, especially when we see the situation in the US. We have to work at two levels: we must ensure that our own arrangements, such as distributing the load, finally take shape at the Council meeting in June and we can draw up a strategy for meeting our obligations. We must also ensure that our approach matches the negotiating position we will be adopting at the Buenos Aires meeting. Such a consistent, coordinated approach can also help us in our relations with other parties in the coming months, and so I would like to look briefly at the different components of this strategy.

Like Parliament, I would like to see the EU adopt more ambitious goals and go further than an 8 % reduction. It is important that the EU retain its leading role. The Presidency has said that it hopes that the Environmental Council can reach unanimity, which John Prescott has also emphasized here today. On this point, we have to bear in mind that three new gases have been included. Before the Kyoto meeting, the Commission produced a communication which showed that the Community had significant potential for reducing emissions which would not have adverse effects on the economy. We drew attention to CO2 and showed that it was possible to achieve a more viable and purer energy consumption by tightening up policies and arrangements and providing the appropriate incentives. I propose to submit a communication which can be taken up at the Council meeting in June. We will raise all these factors and the Commission's attitude to the flexibility provisions. In this way, we will have defined the guidelines for the Community when it is time to start meeting the obligations in the Protocol.

On this point, I would very much like to emphasize the provisions of the Protocol requiring the industrialized parties to show that they have made progress in meeting their obligations by 2005. This means we must get to work implementing national policies and arrangements. If any Member States believe that they can just make do with dealing in emission quotas, which have not even been set yet, they will be unable to meet their commitments. The flexibility provisions will not give any major results – in fact, virtually no results whatsoever – by that time. The Protocol clearly states that these mechanisms are designed to be supplementary to countries' own efforts. This concept is not defined in more detail in terms of percentages. Only one of the flexibility mechanisms, that is, saving credits under the clean development fund mechanism may enter into force by the end of the first commitment period which starts in 2000.

The flexibility provisions concern – as John Prescott underlined – trading in emission quotas, joint implementation and mechanisms for clean development, the so‐called clean development fund. The Commission is in the process of preparing an analysis of the Protocol for the Environmental Council meeting in March, at which a number of these questions will be examined in more detail. On the other hand, the analysis will not have all the answers, as the more detailed economic and legal work is still incomplete. We must ensure that the international rules on flexibility mechanisms are justified and that the Community and the special position of the single market are taken proper account of. It is vital that the Community and its Member States resolve these questions as soon as possible before the preparatory meeting in Bonn in June and the Buenos Aires meeting in November.

The Protocol will not take effect until it is ratified by 55 parties, not least the industrialised nations, which accounted for over 55 % of the nations' CO2 emissions in 1990. The attitude of the USA and Russia is of decisive importance here. The two countries together account for approximately 45 % of the industrialised nations' CO2 emissions from using energy in 1990. Unless these countries are on board, it will put implementation of the Protocol at risk. How Russia responds will presumably depend on the US, and the news from Washington is not good. The US must now live up to its responsibilities and ratify the Protocol and we must use all our efforts to bring this about. It is important to convince the US administration, Congress and people that the Protocol is in their own interests in the long term, and that the USA should sign and ratify it as soon as possible. Likewise, we must use all the opportunities available to get Russia to come round to our views, and I am very pleased at the remarks which John Prescott made about the Presidency's plans to bring the subject up at the different meetings which will be held under the British Presidency. Members of the European Parliament also have an important role to play here. One of the key factors for ratification by those countries is that we find a solution to a number of vital questions which the Protocol leaves open, the so‐called flexibility mechanisms to which I have just referred.

As far as the developing countries are concerned, the Community has always sought to take account of their particular situation and needs. We have always emphasized that, under the Berlin mandate, we did not expect the developing countries to commit themselves to reducing emissions. This is the reason why I, for one, am pleased that the attempt to get commitments out of the developing countries at Kyoto failed. Nevertheless, we must recognise that climate change is a global problem and that we will have to include the developing countries in the process at a later stage. I believe the Community will have to act as a bridge between the industrialized nations which are demanding more involvement now and the developing countries themselves. With this in mind, we must be clear about our position. We must also be clear that we cannot treat the developing countries as a single group: we have to take account of their different views and priorities. Parliament also has a major role to play here, such as through the EU‐ACP Joint Assembly.

Mr President, I hope my statement has answered some of the questions you would like. I have tried to give you food for thought on a number of questions which have to be solved if we are to ensure that the Kyoto Protocol achieves the desired effect. I believe that, thanks to the Commission's work at present, I will be able to give you more news of developments in the coming months. I am confident that Parliament will continue to support the approach of the Commission and Presidency and I look forward to working with both the Presidency and Parliament in the run‐up to the Buenos Aires conference.

  K. Collins (PSE). – Mr President, first of all may I welcome to Parliament the President of the Environment Council and Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom and thank him for his kind comments directed at the Environment Committee.

It is certainly heartening to see that the United Kingdom has made environment a priority of the Presidency. I think that is very good indeed and we can all support it. However, although, if I may say so, I have no wish to pour cold water over the President's ambitions for the next six months, I know he is well aware that the activity of one Presidency can only be successful if it involves the activities of the succeeding Presidencies. I can tell from his own speech and from what he has stated earlier that he is well aware of that, as well as of the need to cooperate with the Austrians, Germans and then the Finns. Nevertheless it has to be emphasized that presidencies do play an important facilitating role.

I would now like to move on to the issues of climate change and air quality, the latter being an issue which I know is of some considerable interest to my friend and colleague, Mr Miller. It is clear that environmental protection is a cross‐sectoral and international issue. Climate change is an apposite example to illustrate the case. The burning of fossil fuels in one Member State affects not only the local environment but global weather patterns. The El Niño phenomenon is a graphic example of the potential impact of climate change on a global environment.

We in Parliament were very pleased that an agreement was reached at Kyoto on cutting CO2 emissions. However, one or two points nonetheless need to be made. It is a pity, from our point of view, that the agreed limits for CO2 emissions were not even more stringent, although I do accept entirely how difficult the task facing the European negotiators was. What we need now, however, is for the Commission to consider how we are to disaggregate responsibility for cutting CO2 emissions. Specific proposals setting out how Member States are expected to achieve standards must be brought forward and specific limits for each Member State set so that we know how it is we are going to achieve our targets. This is a matter of the utmost urgency. Without it, we have the prospect of a set of standards agreed across the European Union based on an agreement that each State will share the burden of reaching limits – the so‐called ‚bubble’ which some Member States, left to their own devices, will do very little to implement. If we allow this to happen we will not achieve our European Union targets.

These considerations must also be taken into account at a broader global level in the context of the agreement on emission trading. All of this involves the Auto‐Oil programme. It means bringing together our energy and transport policies as well as our agricultural policies. We really need to have much more integration of environmental considerations into all policy areas.

The draft Treaty of Amsterdam provides a crucial first step towards this integration. We need to start preparing for Buenos Aires now in order to ensure that we have a clear and coherent strategy in place for COP 4. Mr Prescott's statement is a good first step on the way and demonstrates the Presidency's awareness of the need to make progress.

In conclusion, I hope that when we get to Buenos Aires the European Parliament's delegation will be closer to the heart of the EU delegation than at Kyoto because Parliament has an even more important role to play in environment policy in the future than it had in the past.

  Spencer (PPE). – Mr President, can I start by thanking the Commissioner both for her statement today and for the kind words which she addressed to the members of GLOBE for our activities in Kyoto when she spoke to this Parliament in December.

Can I then turn to the President‐in‐Office and thank him for his statement and say that he places me, as a man who regards tradition as important, in an almost impossible position. The tradition to which I refer is, of course, the honourable Westminster tradition of seeking to oppose someone who is sitting opposite you. I find myself in the embarrassing position of being in almost full support of what you are doing on the climate change exercise. Indeed I see you very much as a kind of heavyweight John Gummer, ploughing the same furrows that John and the previous administration ploughed on this matter.

Let me however, just for the purpose of tradition, render you at least two moments of mild criticism, actually neither of them are mild. The first is to say that I share the considerable concern in my constituency over the confusion, to put it mildly, which has been felt in terms of this government's approach to the green belt. I represent Surrey, the county that invented green belts; this is an appallingly important issue to us and I would ask you in your domestic manifestation to make sure that if this concern is misplaced you get out there and reassure people rapidly.

Secondly, I would express some concern at the reversal of the previous government's push away from the use of coal and energy generation. It seems to me that the targets which we are going to have to set ourselves in reducing fossil fuel are so tough that any sign of weakness and moving away from the increased use of gas rather than coal is something that we will pay for in the future.

Having said that, can I return to the question of Kyoto. It was, as Ken Collins has indicated, something of a leaky bucket. I think there is a great deal of work to be done in the months that lie ahead particularly in ensuring that work in the subsidiary bodies of the convention takes place well before Kyoto.
