As you and I will recall from the early morning of Kyoto, there is a limit to what you can do in those last few hours of negotiations, if the work has not been done – the work on plugging the leaks, the work on sorting out how to talk to the developing world, the work on how to deal with, for instance, emissions trading.

I think emissions trading is going to be the most difficult issue for us all to cope with, because it is going to require a revolution in our mind‐sets. We, as Europeans, went into Kyoto as the ‚good guys’ determined to keep the target up as far as possible. The Americans engaged in a very cynical exercise and were determined to get emissions trading in place. I think that good emissions trading is worth having, but that means emissions trading that has a proper institutional framework and is subject to proper controls. None of that preparatory work is being done. We as a Union are the group in the world that has the most interest now in making sure that it is done. I wish you well and I look forward to your sustained interest in this, not just during the British Presidency, but during whatever months or years remain to the Labour government.

  Baldi (UPE). – (IT) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, I too would like to thank Madam Commissioner and the Council for taking the work of our Parliament into consideration. Undoubtedly, climate change has serious, destabilizing consequences for the entire planet and for the life of all living beings.

Recently, El Niño has led to an irregular overheating of the surface waters of the equatorial Pacific, the contrast between the hot water from the south and the cold from the north and very intense storms with exceptional winds. Real catastrophes have affected the Pacific coast, and waves 8‐9 metres high have lashed San Francisco. We should not forget that it is difficult to warn the populations in similar crises owing to the speed with which unforeseeable tornadoes arise.

It is therefore urgently necessary to achieve specific and more satisfactory results at global level with regard to a reduction in greenhouse gases and the overheating of the planet.

At the Kyoto Summit in December 1997, steps were taken in that direction, as the need for worldwide intervention was recognized. However, the Protocol contains results which, although they may be considered sufficient, are not satisfactory.

Several countries are still reticent over the need to reduce greenhouse gases. At this third conference, the Union reached a more binding agreement than the previous one, but ratification of the Kyoto Protocol by the Union should take place as a result of the progress recorded and on the basis of specific measures, before the next meeting in Buenos Aires in November 1998.

Parliament considers it important to record positive developments to achieve the objectives laid down on the parameters, principles and procedures, and considers it essential for the EU to continue to play a leading role in international negotiations to try and implement a common policy aimed at halting the current climate change.

Finally, it would be advisable for Parliament to send a larger delegation to Buenos Aires, noting the work carried out by other institutional and international bodies, such as the Africa, Caribbean and Pacific Assembly, which is formed of small insular states which are currently suffering from the harmful effects of the climate change.

  Eisma (ELDR). ‐ (NL) Mr President, back in December we already expressed our appreciation for the role the Commission and the Council played during the negotiations in Kyoto, and we would like to reiterate this. But let us not be satisfied for too long about the results which were achieved during the negotiations. Now we really have to get to work. We are therefore anxiously awaiting the Commission"s document on the implementation of Kyoto. Six gases are more than the three we had in mind, after all. My question to the Commission is, when may we expect this document?

A guiding principle for this will have to be that the original negotiations of the European Union continue to be the starting point. The Commissioner said so himself: the stakes will have to be raised higher for Europe than those which were accepted in Kyoto. To reach these objectives for each country by means of burden sharing, extremely costly measures will be required, along with significant social efforts and technological breakthroughs.

The bills for the Netherlands, for example, show that ECU 10 billion will be needed, as well as a lowering of the maximum speed limit to 100 km/h, a doubling of taxes on petrol, and so on and so forth. Even then, in 2010 the emissions in our country can be expected to be 13 % above 1999 levels, whilst we need a decrease of 10 %. This is a difference of 23 %. Modal shifts in energy, expensive solutions to CO2 in the soil, and a drastic increase in government expenditure in this area are vital. This incredibly heavy task is what we are presented with if we want to fulfil our promise.

  González Álvarez (GUE/NGL). – (ES) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, in its communication at the end of last year the Commission – a leader at Kyoto – was already saying that there was a need for urgent and effective measures, and that it was economically and socially possible to reduce CO2 emissions by 800 million tonnes by the year 2010, that is, by about 15 %.

It is true that since there are six greenhouse gases the percentage can vary. As Mr Eisma said, it is likely that we will need to have that study to hand. We also welcome the Commission communication on preserving biodiversity, and the fact that at Cardiff the Commissioner is going to defend the integration of environmental policy into all European policies. However, we believe it is all interrelated. If we cannot go to Argentina with another leading position in favour of a clear and transparent reduction of greenhouse gases; if, simultaneously, by the year 2000 we have not yet managed to put a clearly delimited Natura network in place; if we have not similarly put in place measures for the preservation of biodiversity; and, above all, if we are not able to include environmental measures in the EU"s other policies, then it will be very difficult to make progress in any of the envisaged objectives.

We are seeing day by day that in transport, agriculture and other sectors, measures are being taken which flagrantly conflict with the preservation of the environment. And day by day, in our own countries, we are also seeing the adoption of specific measures which go against our ultimate objective. So at Cardiff, I think the Commissioner – who is getting used to being forceful – should clearly, once and for all, fight for the European Union to include environmental measures in all European policies, so that it is not just a headline again, but is put into practice effectively.

  Lannoye (V). – (FR) Mr President, all the speakers, or nearly all of them, have noted the fact that, for the first time, we have a regulatory framework at international level, and I support this positive move.

Having said this, we must immediately put this into perspective with regard to the objective, which should be to stabilize the climate. The figures arrived at through consensus are really very limited. This is one more reason why we should be vigilant with regard to observing, examining and defining the factors which may constitute real loopholes in this regulatory framework. I am referring here to flexibility measures – trade in emission rights, carbon price evaluations – which are really difficult to quantify. It therefore falls to the Commission and the Council to play an active role in the follow‐up to these negotiations.

If we want the European Union to be credible, I believe that it is important to respect the objectives which have been set, and I do not see why – and this is the position of my group – we do not adopt the Commission"s declaration of last October as a starting point. In this it affirmed that we could easily achieve a 15 % reduction in emissions by the year 2010, with little economic cost, as long as adequate technologies and policies were used, and that It was even possible to achieve an economic profit.

On this basis, we consider that regulatory commitments must be immediately defined for Member States and that we should not content ourselves with a White Paper and recommendations intended for Member States but base ourselves on real directives. I believe that this is how we will become truly credible in the eyes of our international partners.

I would like to add that it is essential to include the restrictions which we want respected with regard to the Kyoto Protocol in international negotiations, beginning with the accession negotiations. I am thinking in particular of one large negotiation process, over which there is much question today: the Multilateral Agreement on Investment. There you can search in vain for any reference to this immense problem which we have been discussing here for the past few minutes.

  Weber (ARE). – Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, firstly, I would remind the President‐in‐Office of the Council that the EU target in Kyoto was not 8 % but 15 %. It is important that the EU stick to its initial aim of 15 % reductions of greenhouse gases for the year 2010. This is both technically feasible and economically manageable. It all comes down to having the political will to act. These are not my words: I am quoting the Commission's official publication at Kyoto.

I would like to make some remarks about Article 3 of the Kyoto Protocol which deals with land use change, forestry and, specifically with afforestation, deforestation and reafforestation since 1990. By allowing those Annex B countries, for which land use change and forestry activity constituted a net source of greenhouse gas emissions in 1990, to include them in their 1990 emission basis for the purpose of calculating their assigned amount, the Kyoto Protocol opens up a considerable loophole. Paragraph 3 allows the use of afforestation and reafforestation as a means for carbon absorption to meet the commitments, and also deforestation is taken into account in a negative way.

What does this mean in practice? Countries that are actually converting their primary forest into plantations are encouraged to continue to do so. They are encouraged to replace primary forests by plantations of fast‐growing species in order to absorb the maximum of CO2 . This has certainly not enhanced the biodiversity of the areas concerned.

Which were the countries which made these requests? These were Australia, New Zealand, Canada and Russia. If these countries do not continue their deforestation, they will have an advantage because they will not need to make as much effort as other countries to achieve energy efficiency.

EU countries such as Finland and Austria which achieved net removals of CO2 thanks to a sustainable forest use – in fact they cut less than the annual increment or growth – are disadvantaged by this system laid down in the Kyoto Protocol. When it comes to the clean development mechanism, Annex B countries could even, for instance, benefit from a project which they finance in the tropics, for example to convert primary forests into palm oil plantations – and we know what happened in Indonesia – while, at the same time, benefiting from the emissions at home.

To conclude, the Kyoto Protocol has many loopholes and is open to abuse. It needs to be amended. Let us use our right to amend it.

  Kronberger (NI). – (DE) Mr President, I would like to add another thought to this debate on the European Union's future climate policy. Whenever we talk about targets for reducing emissions of greenhouse gases, we are asked how much it will cost. I think we need to turn that question on its head if we are to get any further. We need to ask what it will cost us if we do not reduce them. Then we will soon realise that reductions make very good economic sense.

With this in mind I would like to refer to paragraphs 8, 9, 10, 11 and 12 of the motion for resolution tabled by Mr Collins and Mrs Roth‐Berendt, which I regard as being particularly effective and positive here. I too fail to share the enthusiasm for emission trading – I actually think it is immoral. If we in the industrialized nations do not try to change and improve our structures, but simply plant replacement trees in the Third World or wherever, that is scarcely a constructive way forward.

I regard this as a modern version of trading in indulgences. Martin Luther nailed his Theses to the door of the church in Wittenberg as a protest against this sort of thing, back in 1517. We do not want a two‐class world in which one class is wasteful and is destroying the world and the other class lives on hand‐outs from the industrialized nations and gives up its own development. No, what we need is a sustainable, renewable economic system.

  Linkohr (PSE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, I would like to remind Mr Kronberger that since Martin Luther's day another problem has come along. I am referring to the idea that anything printed on paper is automatically right. And that is the problem with Kyoto. At present we just have a paper agreement, because in practice energy consumption is growing in Europe as well, despite the warm winter. That is a fact. If we want to achieve the objective that we agreed to in Kyoto, then we will need to make an enormous effort, and go well beyond what we have assumed up to now. We will have to work against the grain of the market, at a time when the oil price is $14 a barrel.

Will we carry on pursuing these objectives in isolation or will we wait until they sign it? That is another question that needs answering.

The last main point that I want to make is this: if we take Kyoto seriously, that means the end of national energy policies. We are all jointly responsible now. From now on, none of the 15 Member States will be able to pursue a policy that goes against the main flow, certainly not on anything climate‐related. The next question is: are there any kinds of sanctions against countries or companies which do not respect these objectives? How will these objectives be implemented? How will all this be managed? What institutional framework do we need? The message that came out of Kyoto is that we need to develop a common energy strategy in Europe.

I would like to come back to a suggestion that I have already made several times: we need something along the lines of European climate management. I have in mind a climate agency with real executive power. Perhaps the Council or the Commission can comment on this.

  Pimenta (PPE). – (PT) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, today, yet again, we meet to discuss climate change and what to do with the Kyoto agreements. Much has been said, and I for my part would like to emphasize three points.

Firstly, there needs to be rigorous verification of the data and methodology employed by countries and by Member States to arrive at the values they declare regarding their respective atmospheric emissions. A number of countries have recently reviewed their emission inventories and, interestingly, these reviews have invariably “benefited’ them in that the resulting values for emissions during the reference year of 1990 are higher than those that they had originally submitted. If each slice of the European cake is to grow, then there can be no doubt that the European bubble will swell like a soufflé in the oven.

Secondly, as regards policy and measures, internal measures and policies to reduce emissions in Europe must be formulated. Our energy, energy conservation and other programmes to date amount to no more than demonstrations of pilot programmes. A decisive impetus should be given to: the use of renewable fuels and to their sale and network connection; to practising resolute energy conservation and efficiency; to enhancing cogeneration facilities; to changing the system of taxation on energy consumption and pollution – as former President Jacques Delors proposed back in 1992; and to changing the way in which we use the transport sector by imposing new standards which reduce consumption and penalize motor vehicle pollution. We know what must be done; most of the requisite technology exists. It has not been done, purely and simply because governments have not wanted to.

Finally, the international negotiations which were decisively advanced in Kyoto must be continued. I will not dwell on this because others have already discussed the matter. I would like to support the President"s idea of carrying out an audit of the way in which each country is implementing its national programme for combating climate change and protecting the environment. We cannot accept that questions which were left unanswered in Kyoto, such as that of emissions trading, should be used as a means of avoiding in‐depth changes that may justifiably be demanded of those who use and consume energy, that is, we the people of Europe and those in the United States, who should consequently make the greatest effort.

  Tamino (V). – (IT) Mr President, while we are discussing this, the climate is changing and is continuing to change day by day. Only the oil industry lobby seems to be unaware of this. But now is not the time for words, but for facts. And the facts say that, within the scope of the OECD countries, 1990 CO2 emissions need to be reduced by 50 %.   Graenitz (PSE). – (DE) – Mr President, at the end of a conference, once the negotiations have been completed, but a good many outstanding remits still have to be tackled, the words of Brecht come to mind: ’After the struggle of the uphill climb comes the struggle over the plain’. I think that there are still a lot of struggles ahead of us, and the next stage of the journey, to Buenos Aires and beyond, will certainly be full of ups and downs. I believe that if the European Union is to be credible and to behave credibly, we need to do two things.

First, we will have to look outwards, to act in a global context, to make a contribution as a global player, just as we did in Kyoto. Second, we will have to make policies within the Union and put the necessary measures in place, with the Commission, the Council and this Parliament acting as a team.

We have already touched upon the legislative measures that will be needed, and I would like to highlight those. We need a legal base for implementing the European bubble, to get all the Member States to meet the obligations they have taken on and to monitor whether these obligations are being fulfilled. Furthermore, we need measures that will make it clear to the citizens of the Union that climate change affects every single person and that it involves perfectly simple things, such as how energy efficient their electrical goods are, what form of transport they choose and how waste is managed.

I believe that we will have to make specific, clear and verifiable proposals in the run‐up to Buenos Aires about how emission trading is to be managed and how the clean development fund will work.   Simpson (PSE). – Mr President, can I thank the President‐in‐Office for his statement in the House today. The importance of environmental issues in the European Union is highlighted by the keen interest that there is throughout the Union, particularly amongst young people. I was delighted to hear the priority put on transport within the environment by the President‐in‐Office, and it is in this area that I want to make some comments this afternoon.

The effects of road transport on emissions and air quality are well documented. There is a need to encourage modal shifts towards a more environmentally‐sustainable method of transport in both passengers and freight. This goal can be achieved through measures which encourage public transport and promote a fundamental shift of freight from road to other modes such as rail, inland waterways and coastal shipping. These are modes of transport that are presently under‐utilized within the European Union but which are crucial if we are to hit our targets agreed at Kyoto and referred to by the President‐in‐Office.

That is our challenge. It is imperative that all three institutions within this Union work together to bring about the changes that we all desire. It is also imperative that within the transport sector we all work together to integrate our transport system so that a credible, safe and efficient alternative is offered to the use of private cars and lorries. But we must, as the President‐in‐Office said today, become more cross‐sectorial in our planning and thinking. Transport, environment and planning are all connected and need to work together. For far too long, both at national and European level, each area has followed its own ideals; this has lead to an uncoordinated approach to what is a very real problem.

We must also, as Mr Collins said, ensure that transport and the environment figure highly in our future policy‐making decisions. I appreciate that the EMU and enlargement are important subjects, but in order to enlarge the EU and to spend the ECU in it you have to have a continent with a clean environment in the first place. I am sure most MEPs, even those from Surrey, would welcome the start made by Kyoto. We must now show the political courage to carry on the fight and secure the future not only for ourselves, but more importantly, for our young people.

  Pollack (PSE). – Mr President, I would like to thank the President‐in‐Office and the Commissioner for their very sterling efforts at Kyoto which were instrumental in brokering an agreement and show the strength of collective bargaining.

We are all conscious of the very difficult path to ratification of the Kyoto agreement and the very great efforts we need to make in Europe in order to meet our targets. At the same time we know that this is only the beginning of efforts to arrest the greenhouse effect worldwide.

The development of a new set of detailed policies and measures by the Commission is now essential and urgent, particularly in light of the news that EU emissions of CO2 are on a rising curve. We also know that the loopholes and ambiguities left over from Kyoto will require intensive further negotiation in the run‐up to COP 4 at Buenos Aires: how to implement emissions trading, for example. We have a responsibility to use our strong European position to help some of the G−77 countries and China to develop clean energy. It must be done in a manner which could act as an incentive to them. This is where partnership with industry is essential, using European external policy to support and steer clean technology investment in the right areas. In this context, the inclusion of climate change on the agenda for the ASEM summit is very important and, in particular, the European Union's tropical forest strategy must be closely coordinated with the post‐Kyoto strategy to ensure maximum efficiency.

Finally the President‐in‐Office spoke of breaking out of the habit of thinking in a fragmented way. I would like to see closer integration of policies. For instance, it is crucial that, for the long‐term success of the battle against climate change, a strong environment protection clause is inserted into the Multilateral Agreement on Investment.

This has had to be a very compressed contribution because of lack of time, but I do urge the Council and Commission to take on board the numerous points in Parliament's resolution.

  McNally (PSE). – Mr President, I thank the President‐in‐Office for coming here today. It is appreciated. The word for today obviously is ‚integration’ and the European Parliament itself should do more to integrate the work of the different committees. I speak about energy. The world of energy is a tough and a nasty world full of vested interests with massive lobbying powers. But most individual decisions are made in energy.

In the United Kingdom last year 33 % of energy went on transport and 30 % on energy in the domestic sector, mainly space heating. Those are decisions which can be made by individuals. The EU has heavy responsibilities but weak powers and that is a reproach to the Member States who have not given it the tools for the job it should be able to do.

We have to do two things in the energy field. We have to use energy more rationally and we have to switch to renewable sources of energy. Let me give you a test of seriousness. If you are serious, you will ensure that the rational planning directive, which enables domestic customers to make sensible choices about their use of energy, is passed and rescued from the black hole it appears to have fallen into. You will put more money into the SAVE II programme and you will help the Fifth Framework Programme.

If you are serious about renewable energy, you will ensure that there is a legal base so that the European Union can do for renewable energy in 1997 what it did for nuclear energy in 1957. A treaty similar to the Euratom Treaty would enable us to do something meaningful in terms of renewable energy. We also need to look at ways in which that energy can have access to the grid.

Most of the things that will happen in response to Kyoto will not happen at our level but at Member State level. You will have our support in this Parliament for those few things we can do up to the end of the British Presidency and beyond. It is far too important to leave when we made such a good start at Kyoto.

  President . – I have received seven motions for resolutions pursuant to Rule 37(2) of the Rules of Procedure.

The vote will be taken tomorrow at 11.30 a.m.

  Prescott, President‐in‐Office of the Council . – Mr President, I thank the honourable lady for her final remarks of support, which are very much appreciated, as indeed are those from all the honourable Members today.

It has been an excellent debate. It has expressed all the concerns we all feel about the Kyoto agreement, but there has been much agreement on these matters. Indeed, the many steps we have taken have been agreed with. It is important for us to bear in mind that Europe has to keep the lead. It has an opportunity to do so. It has set the lead in this matter and we must sustain that, as it is absolutely crucial we get agreement on these measures to get the climate changes that are necessary.

Nobody has thrown any doubt on the science before us on these matters. In America there is a great deal of debate about that, but in the rest of the world the science is absolutely clear, and this is being accepted more in America of late.

I welcome the resolution before us – that seems to touch many of the issues – and the calls for integration, which I mentioned in my speech today. Many honourable Members have pointed out that Kyoto was the first step, and indeed that is my approach to it. Without ratification of this agreement it will not mean a great deal, and it is important that we achieve that ratification.

The next stages are absolutely important. We need agreement on the flexibilities, the sinks, the joint implementation and all the measures that Members have referred to. But be in no doubt about it, if we do not get agreement on those rules, there will be no ratification.

Everybody has a veto in this process. If you look at the terms for ratification – 55 countries or 55 % of gas emissions – we can all play the veto game if we want. In reality, what is most welcome about the Kyoto agreement was the fact that we got an agreement at all! We have to have the same approach to ratification. The forces in America make it clear that if the developing countries do not sign up to the process, they will not ratify it. The period of time between now and ratification may be as much as three or four years, because, in my judgement, the ratification process will not take place in America until after the next American elections. So there is some time for us to work out these rules.

I hear people say that this was perhaps a weak agreement or ask why we did not necessarily keep to the 15 %. But I do not think you would have thanked us if we had come back to this House and said that we had got 15 % but nobody else would sign up for it so we had not got an agreement. Agreement is about reaching agreement with other countries in this matter and on an issue in which there was not to be a vote on matters of division because it required consensus.

I should put on record the tremendous debt we owe to Ambassador Strada who got everybody under these circumstances to come to some agreement, which right up to the last minute was often in doubt. It was his particular skills that played a major part in finding an agreement for this first step. We got a good agreement and the NGOs themselves think it is an agreement far better than they expected.

Of course it is a first step. But it will mean nothing unless we get ratification, and ratification, again, requires us to enter into a process of difficult and complex negotiations, negotiations that now involve deciding the rules to apply to the modalities. We are clear, and America must understand also, that all these issues in determining the rules concerning the modalities have to be agreed by all countries or ratification of the Treaty will not be achieved. Therefore, we are setting out our position ready for Buenos Aires, showing exactly where we stand, how we can get an agreement and using all our abilities to achieve those agreements.

When it is said to me: what if they do not ratify nationally? Well that will be a disaster for the world, the global economy and the global environment. Nevertheless, bear in mind that the question was often posed in our negotiations. What if, in fact, certain countries in the European bubble do not agree to the targets set for them? What will we do with those countries? There are no identifiable sanctions.

When I hear the honourable Member from Luxembourg saying that in his view we should have kept to the 15 % aim, well there is nothing to stop Luxembourg from doing what my country is doing. We have set 15 %; indeed, we have set 20 % on the reduction of CO2 gases. This is only a minimum. There is no reason why a country cannot set higher standards if it so wishes. What we are establishing here is a minimum understanding under the bubble.

I know Luxembourg was good in achieving its target, as indeed was Britain. But that meant we closed most of our coal industry down and Luxembourg closed down one steel industry. There are different consequences in different countries. Perhaps Luxembourg might be able to increase the price of the cheap petrol it sells there as another contribution to reducing greenhouse gases. We all make our contributions in different ways and there is nothing to prevent higher standards being set in these matters.

This gives me the opportunity to point out that when we take certain measures to achieve the overall agreements, sometimes the pain is greater in some of our communities than others. In the pit communities in Britain, where the only means of employment has been torn out, the community is dying. Therefore we have a responsibility to help those countries which have paid a price for the judgement of the coal industry. In Britain they have paid a heavy price which we must seek to adjust, and that is not only applicable to the coal industry.

Mr Simpson made a point basically about our domestic economy and my own government's policies in regard to greenbelt. Whilst we have only been in nine months – and I cannot resist saying this – there are now more areas of greenbelt than when we came to power. Indeed, today we have announced an increase of 12 500 hectares of greenbelt around Durham. That is a substantial step forward. But, as he knows, I shall be making a statement to the House of Commons shortly about that matter. We think greenbelt is an important area.

Transport also has a major role to play, there is no doubt about that. We see the importance of the contribution of transport to the environment and the need to question the role of the car in our cities. We must seek to use public transport more than private vehicles and to try to get more goods moved from road to rail. Indeed our own environmental policies and the White Paper in my own country will seek to achieve that. This has been a very good debate dealing with the essential issues. There have been calls for action for taking Kyoto forward. I would say that we have already spelt out, certainly for the next six months, a programme for action.

Several Members have told us not to rest on our laurels. I do not think any of us want to. Let me remind the House of the objectives we have set ourselves, which have been agreed between the Council and the Commission and in discussion with this Parliament. By June we hope to reach a common position on updating the CO2 monitoring mechanisms. By June we want to see an agreed burden‐sharing for the 8 %. By June we will agree the first tranche of European measures such as reducing CO2 emissions from cars. By June we want the Community and the Member States to have signed the Protocol. By June we will have a common European position for the official negotiations in Bonn on trading and other issues.

This is an ambitious programme for the next four months. It answers some of the queries that have been posed by the honourable Members about the coming few months following on from Kyoto. It is a programme that keeps Europe to the fore in putting across the case, in extending the negotiations that follow on from Kyoto, and in establishing the policies that are necessary to achieve those objectives. We have every reason to be proud of our record. We have a programme that will sustain Europe to the fore in these matters and which I hope will satisfy this House in the future.

IN THE CHAIR: MR VERDE I ALDEA
Vice‐President

  Bjerregaard, Member of the Commission. – (DA) Mr President, I am also pleased at the debate we have had today, and I feel that we have been given an enormous impetus which can be used in further work. Mr Eisma had a question, which I think John Prescott has actually answered already, but I will try and answer it too. It was about the communication which is, so to speak, a follow‐up to the communication of October, and which deals with the situation since Kyoto. This communication covers six gases instead of three, and we expect to have it ready for discussion at the Council meeting in June, which means it can also be put on Parliament's agenda before the summer recess, if Parliament can do this. In other words, we expect to deal with this communication energetically, and I can confidently state that some of the resources mentioned here today will be used in the process, as they were also included in the previous communication – not least the comments made on energy as a whole.

I would like to emphasize once again that it is not immediately possible to transfer the goals we had before Kyoto or the annual figures we were working with. The agreement now covers six gases, and six gases is not the same as three, so the goals cannot simply be transferred. So far, we have seen a number of different calculations at DG XI, and the latest I have seen – although this could well change given the way the work is going – refer to 12 % instead of 15 %, and so the question is, of course, whether to go beyond the 8 % which was agreed or to leave it at 8 %. But 15 % is not something which can be used in further discussions in any case.

I would also like to emphasize – and Mr Tamino also had some remarks on this point – that the Commission has been in absolutely no doubt that taxes are a useful tool. There are proposals on the table which the Council can take up. We all now know what happened to the CO2 emission tax. It is still there and it could be taken up. In other words, if the will were there, it would be quite possible to use this tool.

I would like to finish by saying that, like John Prescott, I am pleased that the overwhelming majority of speakers here today have recognised the results achieved at Kyoto. There is no doubt that we would have liked to have had higher targets, but it would have been totally pointless if there had only been European targets and no targets covering the countries we are comparing ourselves with – mainly Japan and the US. Of course, such targets will not be achieved without negotiation and without the will to fight for our own targets. I would put it like this: I think we have a result which is a good starting point, but which is definitely not the end of the road. I am pleased that the Presidency has emphasized so clearly today that we have a great deal of work ahead of us. Both the Presidency and the Commission are prepared to help with this work.

  Eisma (ELDR). ‐ (NL) Briefly, Mr President, I would like a clarification from Commissioner Bjerregaard. In December, the Commissioner said that this Parliament might receive a post‐Kyoto document in March. Am I to understand now that the postKyoto document has been postponed from March to June?

  Bjerregaard, Member of the Commission. – (DA) Mr President, what I said in December was that we would start examining the implications of the Kyoto agreement, that is, a more technical document. On the basis of this, we will publish a communication dealing mainly with the political resources we can use to achieve our aims. Of course, it is the political resources which are of most interest in this context. As I also said in December, I have promised that this communication will be ready for the Council meeting in June.

  President . – Thank you very much, Madam Commissioner.

The debate is closed.

The vote will take place tomorrow at 11.30 a.m.


8. Air pollution by motor vehicles – petrol quality

  President . – The next item is the joint debate on the following documents:

‐ the report (A4‐0043/98) by Mr Lange, on behalf of the Committee on the Environment, Public Health and Consumer Protection, on the amended proposal for a European Parliament and Council Directive on measures to be taken against air pollution by emissions from motor vehicles and amending Council Directives 70/156/EEC and 70/220/EEC (COM(97)0061 – C4‐0088/97‐96/0164B(COD));

‐ the recommendation for second reading (A4‐0044/98), on behalf of the Committee on the Environment, Public Health and Consumer Protection, on the common position adopted by the Council with a view to adopting a European Parliament and Council Directive relating to measures to be taken against air pollution by emissions from motor vehicles and amending Council Directive 70/220/EEC (C4‐0533/97‐96/0164(COD)) (Rapporteur: Mr Lange);

‐ the recommendation for second reading (A4‐0038/98), on behalf of the Committee on the Environment, Public Health and Consumer Protection, on the common position adopted by the Council with a view to adopting a European Parliament and Council Directive relating to the quality of petrol and diesel fuels and amending Council Directive 93/12/EEC (C4‐0532/97‐96/0163(COD)) (Rapporteur: Mrs Hautala).

  Lange (PSE), rapporteur. – (DE) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, we need clean air in Europe. It is the cornerstone for quality of life and environmental quality in Europe, and it is a European issue, because pollution knows no borders. It is not a question of individual interests or of minority interests, and is not an issue between left and right in the political sense. Nor is it a question of national interests. No, it is in the interests of all the citizens of Europe: the quality of their life is at stake, which means that as Members of the European Parliament we have to deal with this issue at European level.

We know that there are currently 200 million cars in the European Union. We know that the car will be the key to our mobility for the foreseeable future. Now, some people think that the best exhaust gas purification system is the human lung. I say no, we must make the car as environmentally friendly as possible. We need to give the green light. The facts are clear to see. On the one hand we have increasing traffic levels. Although individual cars have become cleaner since 1970 – not voluntarily by the way, but because of our legislation – everyone uses these cars more, so that any benefit is cancelled out. Ozone levels are rising and traffic causes cardio‐vascular disorders, asthmatic conditions, especially in children, and there are lung complaints caused by particles in emissions and the acidification of the environment. On the other hand, there are solutions to these problems. These are underpinned by the legendary Auto‐Oil programme. I say legendary because the history of this since 1993 is indeed the stuff of legends, which is a sign of how much time this discussion has taken. If anyone says to me that we need a second Auto‐Oil programme, I just say: ’Look how long it took to achieve anything last time. Let us get this one right before we start anything else’. We should not abandon the approach adopted in the Auto‐Oil programme, because it represents a reasonable and integrated approach. It is not just a matter of tightening the screw wherever it is technically possible, but of looking at the best way to reduce emissions, be it through fuels, engine technology or traffic management measures.

So there are solutions, and this Parliament noted at first reading that there were some shortcomings in the Commission proposal, which was based on the Auto‐Oil study. In fact, the Council accepted that some of these shortcomings existed and took Parliament's suggestions on board. For example, the Council made cold‐start tests for vehicles a requirement for type‐approval. It does not make much sense if a car has marvellous exhaust gas values at 20°C if you think about starting it in the morning, remembering that an average temperature of 20°C is found in very few areas of Europe; it therefore makes sense to consider a vehicle's exhaust emissions at low temperatures.

However, we want more, and for very good reasons. We want fuel‐related improvements. Recent studies have shown that fuel quality has a direct impact on air quality. London Transport carried out a study which showed that identical buses, without any technical modifications, will emit less sulphur, 40 % less hydrocarbons and 40 % less soot particles, just by using better fuel.

How can anyone who knows cities like Madrid, Lisbon, Athens, Frankfurt or London say that we do not want better fuels, although we know that this is a way to reduce particulate emissions? We also need better fuels for technological development in the future. Sulphur is in fact a killer for catalytic converters, and if we want better exhaust gas standards by 2005, then we need better fuel. All studies into the effectiveness and durability of catalytic converters indicate this. So we must do something about fuels.

However, we also need to do something more about the cars themselves. The Commission has proposed indicative limit values for 2005. We say that there is sufficient data to prescribe mandatory limit values now, and this would give the industry enough time to push ahead with development. A large part of the European automotive industry has in fact already said: ’We are not ecstatic, but we could live with mandatory limit values for 2005’. So we should go for a clear position here.

A second important aspect regarding automotive technology is that practical considerations are important. It is not actually possible to push emission limit values down to zero. We will never see the day when the air that comes out at the back is better than the air going in at the front. But we must ensure that a car not only has good emission limit values at the type‐approval stage, but that these are maintained in practice. That is why we want a reasonable, unbureaucratic system of road‐side testing; we want Member States to have the right to check suspect vehicles. Mr Car, can you still boast the same emission values you had three years ago? And if not, why not?

We also want to introduce an on‐board diagnostics system which allows constant monitoring of exhaust gas values and checks for malfunctions. In addition, we want to allow independent workshops and automobile clubs to maintain and repair vehicles, and to allow independent manufacturers to produce spare parts. The automotive industry is always telling me that will involve giving away all kinds of technical secrets. That is not what this is about. It is just a matter of releasing the information needed to carry out repairs. Some people agree, but it is all covered by block exemptions. My response to that, especially in view of recent debates, is that when it comes to the future of block exemptions I only have to remember the fine imposed on Volkswagen and the way that company treated its dealers. Give me reasonable legislation rather than an exempting regulation any day!

Light commercial vehicles' engines are of course based on car engines. The technology is similar, and so I believe that they are an integral part of this. I would have preferred if we could have treated both as a single package from the outset. We tried to integrate things somewhat, and in particular we tried to make the limit values for 2005 mandatory for light commercial vehicles as well. The European Parliament does not just want more, it also wants a little less, it wants a clear‐out. There is a durability test in which a car does 80 000 kilometres on the test bench. No car has ever failed it, and we want to get rid of this test.

This legislation helps employment, because European manufacturers will gain some competitive advantages. Discussions in Japan and in the USA are still going on of course. This new technology is creating and preserving jobs, and whole new industries are emerging. The catalytic converter industry has created 15, 000 new jobs over the last 10 years – it did not even exist before that. Of course there are some people who want to put the brakes on. The lobbyists are busy at work in the corridors here, especially the ones who work for the European oil industry. Sometimes I feel like getting out my hat and collecting money for the poor oil industry. No, that is not acceptable: we need constructive dialogue, and just saying no will achieve nothing. Our experience with Brent Spar has shown that constructive dialogue with us is needed, not just saying ‚nyet’ all the time.

There are many others who support our proposals, including all the motoring organizations, such as Germany's ADAC. The proposals are supported by 40 million car users, because they want clean, environmentally friendly cars. So I am convinced that this Parliament will stand up to those who want to put the brakes on, ignore their campaigning and pass the Elk Test without turning over!

(Applause)

  Hautala (V), rapporteur. – (FI) Mr President, I wish to thank all my colleagues for the splendid level of cooperation they have shown in this matter, which really concerns two main issues: standards for vehicle emissions, and fuel quality. This huge undertaking has been mine since Mr Mamère was elected to the French parliament.

In the EU, 40 000 people die prematurely every year from air pollution, according to the WHO. Awareness of this health hazard has only grown in recent times. That is why I believe we have a still firmer basis for discussing progressively structured goals for fuel quality than we did last spring, when Parliament first made its position clear.

The biggest problems are particle emissions, nitrogen oxides and hydrocarbons. To bring levels down we need cleaner fuels, especially with so many old cars on the road. Furthermore, there are a lot of diesel‐powered vehicles in many EU countries, once considered economical, but which have now proved to be particularly dangerous for people"s health.

I have been delighted to see that health organizations have recently been very active in areas relating to the Auto‐Oil programme. Motoring organizations have also been active, because they know that, although we need cars, if the emissions are not cleaned up, opposition to cars on the road is likely to grow still greater.

Much has been argued about who is going to pay for these proposed changes, and how much. I am fully convinced that the oil industry has given us over‐estimated figures for investment. There is no way we are going to need ECU 60 billion over a period of ten years, and we have now referred the matter to the Arthur D. Little consultancy agency, for further investigation. There they corrected the consultant"s previous figures and stated that their costs of making fuel cleaner were, for example, in terms of risk to health, 55 % too high and, as regards regular petrol, as much as 17 % too high.

Naturally, there is need for investment, and the oil industry is the one sector in Europe that has not invested a great deal in new technology in the last few decades. But it is not fair to ignore environmental concerns, just because the industry is in trouble. Actually, the oil industry runs at 120 % capacity. It is inevitable that we shall in any case have to close some refineries for structural reasons. But it is certainly now reasonable to invest in cleaner production technology. Apart from for the oil industry this is important for the car industry which is, in an indirect way, the biggest end user of these products. The car industry is convinced that cleaner fuels must be on the market everywhere in Europe by the year 2005.

If we look at costs to the motorist, then I trust the figures which show that the added cost for a normal petrol‐driven car will be about ECU 5 a year. As for diesel car owners the cost will work out at perhaps twice this cost, or about ECU 10 a year. Some countries have derived great benefit from differential tax incentives. In this way, the state has, in a manner of speaking, met industry halfway. It has become clear that these products can be made more attractive through tax relief to the consumer when repayment periods for investment are also shortened.

As for my proposals, the Council of Ministers placed constrictions on the Commission"s proposals last April, and so I will not be suggesting limitation values for the year 2000 either, as it is too soon. Instead, I believe it is essential that the limitation values prescribed for the year 2005 are binding, and not meant as guidelines, as the Commission and Council of Ministers propose.

  Rübig (PPE), draftsman of the opinion of the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy. – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, our job is to set targets and monitor deviations from them. With Euro II we set targets worth aspiring to. And of course Euro III, which is now before us, contains ambitious objectives which are well worth working towards.

Of course, in adopting the exhaust gas legislation under Euro III it will be necessary to take account of the integrated approach of the Auto‐Oil programme so that the emission limit values can actually be achieved. This is the only way to realize the objective of improving air quality in European centres of population. While I fully appreciate the importance of the environment, we should not go too far, particularly at a time of mass unemployment all over Europe. The automotive and oil industries are key players in our economies, as we all realize, so we should strive to reach a solution together with those affected.

You cannot make securing jobs and creating new ones a priority one day and then go all out for environmental objectives the next. We have to try to prevent the scales tipping too far either way, as in either case it will cause damage. But I am confident that if we implement the measures planned intelligently, we can, as it were, combine business with pleasure: by pleasure I mean improving the quality of life of our fellow citizens; by business I mean encouraging the automobile and oil industries to make investments which will simultaneously improve their competitiveness, help the environment and create new jobs.

  Bjerregaard, Member of the Commission. – (DA) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, the Commission would like to take this opportunity to thank the Committee on the Environment and Mr Lange and Mrs Hautala, the rapporteurs, as well as Mr Rübig, for their work on these three proposals which are clearly aimed at making a considerable improvement in air quality in Europe and hence also improving Europeans' quality of life. As the initial negotiations made clear, these are highly comprehensive and highly significant proposals. We are now on the second round of negotiations, and as you know, the Council approved the two joint reports unanimously.

The Council recognised that the Commission's approach was sound, and some of the main elements of the joint approaches are as follows: that cost‐effectiveness be used as a criteria for choosing the best solutions for achieving the environmental targets; that fuel standards be tightened up from the year 2000; and that guideline standards be laid down for fuel in the year 2005. The binding values for 2005 will be set after examining the current and future technological developments in Auto‐Oil 2. The results of this examination, involving the Commission, experts from the Member States, NGOs and industry in an active, highly transparent process, will be available by mid−1999 and so I will of course be happy to discuss these proposals with Parliament in the second half of next year.

The Commission's attitude towards the amendments will be discussed in more detail by my colleagues Martin Bangemann and Christos Papoutsis. One thing I would like to emphasize about these initial remarks is that the Commission has great sympathy for the wishes for environmental improvements behind Parliament's improvements. It is precisely the dramatic developments in the number of cars which makes it urgently necessary for us to consider the environmental effects, and air quality is of course a vital issue in this respect. But it is also clear that the Commission must stand by the principles on which its proposals are based. The aim of the Auto‐Oil programme is to improve the air quality in our cities and we are basing this on environmental and health considerations. The provisional air quality standards on which the Auto‐Oil programme is based have since been confirmed in the directive on air quality standards for nitrous oxides, particles, and so on. I will be happy to discuss this with Parliament, hopefully in May.

Work on these proposals has also confirmed that the air quality standards used as the basis for the Auto‐Oil programme are in accordance with a high level of protection and the latest guidelines of the World Health Organization. When the standards were established, the Auto‐Oil programme was designed to meet them as cheaply as possible, which is why the proposals are also based on cost‐effectiveness.

Let me emphasize that, the way I see it, we are agreed on a high level of environmental protection and that we should achieve this. The methods of achieving this level must of course be systematic, they must be transparent and they must involve as little cost as possible. I do not think that we are not agreement as regards the goal, but perhaps we disagree on how to achieve it. The year 2000 is not far off, and so I would like to see these proposals passed as soon as possible. Industry needs clear signals in good time, and needs a clear framework within which to plan and meet its obligations.

I hope these brief remarks have clarified the background to the Commission's approach and I also hope this opens the way to approving the right proposals.

  Bangemann, Member of the Commission. – (DE) Mr President, there can be no doubt that the rapporteurs and their colleagues in Parliament's committees have once again worked very hard. We have already had an opportunity to exchange views between the first decision and today's debate. But we must not kid ourselves. Our discussions today will probably not lead to any change in Parliament's position.

It seems likely that tomorrow Parliament will once again vote for the same amendments which it introduced at first reading, as they are essentially the same. And we do not need a crystal ball to predict that the Council will once again reject these amendments. This in itself demonstrates the difficulties faced by the Council with both reports debated here, in trying to reach a common position; but it also demonstrates the unanimity which was finally achieved.

I am sure I am right in saying that today we must look beyond this reading and the Council decision to the conciliation procedure that will probably be necessary. I am quite happy to read from my brief and tell you why the Commission will have to reject the same amendments it could not accept at first reading. That goes to show how enduring our powers of reasoning are, they can resist even the best arguments!

(Laughter)

I would therefore like to make two basic comments. First, although we may agree on principles, we still seem to find it difficult, when it comes to converting these principles into specific proposals, to recognize what is in accordance with a given principle and what is not, otherwise we would not argue so much. After all, the principles and the methodology which emerged from the first Auto‐Oil programme were accepted by all the EU institutions. Even Parliament welcomed them, and we found that encouraging, because we thought there was now more common ground between us as a basis for further progress. But there is evidently such a great gap between principle and detail that it is still difficult to reach agreement on such matters. Mr Lange has once again suggested that he has carried these principles over from the Auto‐Oil I, while we think that those principles would not justify the amendments that he has tabled once again.

I would therefore venture to propose the following: we can accept six of the amendments and another six in principle. However, they are the same amendments we accepted at first reading. To be precise I will give you the numbers: 4, 11, 49, 50, 51 and 53 are completely acceptable, and 5, 9, 15, 31, 54 and 69 partly so. We cannot accept the remainder. So we shall meet again, and I hope, especially in the light of the Council's position, which will probably be the same, that we will be able to reach some agreement during the conciliation process, because we need a decision, come what may. If we cannot get a decision or if it the response is “no’, which cannot be ruled out in conciliation, none of us will have done ourselves any favours, and no‐one could be proud about that.

However, it is not my intention to discourage you before the vote tomorrow. I understand that the first President of Federal Germany was a committed civilian and therefore had some difficulties with the military, but as President he was of course nevertheless also responsible for that aspect of public life. He concluded his first visit to the army with the following words, which were not very warmly received: ’Well, have a great victory!’. That was held against him for a long time, because it showed he did not really understand those people. I am mentioning this, because if I exhort you with the words ‚Well, have a great vote!’. I do not want to fall into the same trap!

  Papoutsis, Member of the Commission. – (EL) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, first of all I would like to thank the rapporteurs, and in particular Mrs Hautala for her detailed report.

In the context of the common position adopted by the Council, it is envisaged that already from the first six months of 1999 the Commission will submit a proposal defining compulsory specifications for fuels, scheduled to come into force in 2005. For that reason, the Commission cannot accept from now compulsory specifications for 2005 without the essential scientific backing. From the first six months of next year and on the basis of the Auto‐Oil II Programme, we will have the necessary additional information on technical questions concerning the subject, and will then be able to reach a well‐founded decision about the approved fuel standards to be in use after 2005. The Commission therefore considers it premature to define compulsory fuel standards now, to become applicable in 2005.

The specific problems can be tackled in other ways: the common position allows Member States to use special fuels where appropriate, in response to specific problems connected with the quality of the atmospheric environment in certain areas, especially in large cities. The characteristics of the special fuels will of course be dictated, depending on the case, by the nature of the special atmosphere quality problems of the specific areas in which these fuels will probably have to be used.

The Commission also recognises that tax incentives are a very strong way to encourage the market promotion and use of cleaner fuels.

The European Community already possesses a framework within which Member States can modify the levels of the special taxes levied on special consumer fuels to serve the aims of environmental protection. Indeed, granted that Community measures already exist that concern tax interventions for fuels, the Commission believes that it is useful to reiterate the orders in question in a further directive.

Today's common position also provides for a ban on the sale of leaded petrol on the market. That measure is justified because lead in petrol is the most serious source of lead pollution. Already, since the middle of the 1980s, Directive 85/210/EEC has required Member States to encourage the use of unleaded petrol. Consequently, the Commission considers that the sale of leaded petrol within the Community should be banned after the year 2000. However, the Commission recognizes that because of the existing large fleet of vehicles, such a ban would probably create serious social and economic problems in some Member States, and we therefore support the temporary deferment allowed for in the Council's common position, so that some Member States will have a certain leeway in time to put the requisite changes into effect.

Community specifications on the quality of atmospheric air are based on the maintenance of high standards both for health reasons and to protect the environment. I want to assure you here that the Commission will take care to incorporate that principle in its future proposals as well.

Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, the Commission can accept a number of amendments which improve the Council's common position without modifying its basic philosophy. I am referring to Amendments Nos 11, 13, the second part of No 14, 17, the third and fourth parts of No 23 and the first part of No 24.

The Commission can also accept Amendment No 6 subject to the condition, however, that the following sentence is added at the end: ’... provided that certain specifications do not fall below or exceed particular levels’.

However, the Commission cannot accept the other amendments. Let me explain why. Amendments Nos 3, 4, 5 and the first part of No 23 conflict with the criterion of cost‐effectiveness as a basis for the legislation, despite the fact that this criterion is established by Directive 94/12/EEC.

Amendment No 127, the fourth part of No 15, and Amendments Nos 25 to 29 propose measures which have been shown not to be cost‐effective on the basis of the Auto‐Oil I Programme.

Amendment No 1, the first parts of Nos 14, 15 and 16, No 19, the second part of No 23 and Amendments Nos 30 to 37 are incompatible with the Auto‐Oil programme's methodology, with which we will be able, in the middle of 1999, to examine and propose economically advantageous measures to take effect in 2005.

Amendments Nos 8, 9, 18, 28 and 39 do not take into account the provisions of Directive 92/81/EEC and the Commission's more recent proposal with code number COM(97)0030 on the use of tax incentives for fuels.

The seventh part of Amendment No 15, Amendments Nos 20, 21 and 22 and the second part of No 24 also do not comply with established procedures.

The third and fifth parts of Amendment No 15 do not take sufficient account of particular national, regional or even local situations.

Amendments Nos 2, 10 and 12, the second and sixth parts of No 15 and the second part of No 16 deal with details and require further technical examination.

However, the Commission recognizes that during the examination of the common position, Parliament has proposed particularly interesting ideas. I want to assure you that one way or the other, your proposals will receive further consideration during the preparation of proposals for future directives.

  Bowe (PSE). – Mr President, in ten years time – and it will take ten years – far fewer European children will be suffering from asthma caused by car fumes if we meet the targets of the Auto‐Oil Programme. Towns as far apart as Aberdeen in the north of Scotland and Athens in Greece will be better, cleaner and healthier places in which to bring up children, to live and to work. That should be our admirable objective today.

We must all play our part in achieving the results of the Commission's Auto‐Oil proposals. Those results are just the beginning, the Commission's proposals are just the first step in a process that will take ten years. We have already gone through the second reading in Parliament and I hope that we will shortly go through conciliation and after that an Auto‐Oil II process to follow. The clear objectives of this very ambitious programme will require car manufacturers to make cleaner engines and also oblige oil companies to make cleaner petrol and diesel fuels. It is part of our role in Parliament to ensure that those objectives are met on time. For this reason, Parliament is pressing the industry to go further and faster than the Commission and Council proposals, with which Parliament generally agrees, are initially suggesting.

We recognize very clearly the difficulties of the car and oil industries in reaching these objectives and we have listened very carefully to their points of view – in particular, the oil industry whose interests are very close to my own constituency. However, with regard to the points that they have made, I would say that experience of putting similar measures into place in other parts of the world indicates that the costs they are suggesting are not quite what they claim.

If you examine, for example, independent consultants' reports, they are telling us that those costs are in fact 17 % cheaper for petrol and 55 % cheaper for diesel than the Commission itself is proposing. As regards the cost to the general consumer, the ordinary car driver, we are talking about £5‐10 a year for the average car driver or ECU 5‐8 in some other parts of Europe.

We know that structural changes are foreseen in the oil industry, and we know those structural changes will take place regardless of what happens with the Auto‐Oil proposals which will make very little difference to them. However, the overall benefits of the programme can not be denied, be they an improvement in air quality, in public health and also the avoidance of crop damage and eutrophication. I think it is now time that we all commit ourselves to those objectives so that in ten years' time we have those cleaner cities in which children and adults can live free of respiratory illness.

  Florenz (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, Commissioner, I am delighted to see the Commission so impressively represented here today. It shows how important this report is, and it also shows that there is a diversity of opinion in the Commission as well as in Parliament.

Commissioner, I am pleased to hear you say that although it may not be possible to reach any joint decisions today, there is every chance of finding a way forward in the Conciliation Committee. I am amazed that this House seems to be afraid that if we go into the Conciliation Committee the outcome could be even worse. I am convinced that we would come out of the conciliation procedure in a stronger position, if we try to build on the principles you have described. Your principles were based on the proposed Auto‐Oil II. But that marriage is over – the bonds between the automotive and oil industries have been broken. However, that is no reason for the European Parliament to abandon its parliamentary duty, we have to carry on. The divorce judge gave us a great tip, and we are now introducing Auto‐Oil II. All these plans seem quite logical at first, but they do not really get us anywhere.

There is one thing we must not forget: we have quite rightly dismantled the internal borders within the Community, providing flexibility for goods, services and people. Of course, flexibility includes the free movement of people: that means that there are now 200 million cars on Europe's roads, heaven help us. I am not about to suggest getting rid of them. Nevertheless, these cars have an impact on the economy, on the environment and on health. It is these three points that we need to weigh up. So we do not want the kind of go‐slow proposals that we associate with the oil industry; no, we want exciting proposals which will assist innovative industries.

I would like to mention two or three examples. The on‐board diagnostics system has a great many advantages. I am the last person to tinker with a screw‐driver, but I would like to put a stop to the process of concentration in the car maintenance sector. I do not want to find myself being forced to take my car to the same company that built it, just because well‐qualified workshops and garages do not have free access to this on‐board diagnostics system. I am, of course, against electronically‐blocked car bonnets, because if we have a breakdown we will end up standing there with the man from our motoring organization and wondering what to do next, because the car will not go. They need to have proper access. That does not mean to say that car designers have to give the blueprints away, they do need some protection of course. But we must not throw out the baby with the bath water. So we are ready to compromise too.

We have heard an impressive description of the cold start test. It is a long‐standing myth that you can only test cars in Tuscany, where it is nice and warm – it also has to be done where I live, where it is often very cold, 7˚C or less. The cars should run a few kilometres there; we should carry out some tests somewhere like that. I am sure that is not impossible for our innovation‐minded industry.

As for exhaust gas values, is there really such a big difference between us? I would really like to see that for cars. I can scarcely believe there is a problem. I think it is the mandatory nature of the values where we differ. But those of us who studied business management were always told that mandatory targets were a good thing, because we can make calculations when we have money coming in. Otherwise it sometime suits us and sometimes does not. So I cannot comprehend this, and I am certainly not one of the devotees of social policy in this House, but one of the ones who is self‐employed.

There are some areas, such as the combined approach on hydrocarbons and NOx, which we can discuss. Those are specialist areas where we are always ready to compromise. The story about the durability test has been explained very convincingly. But there must also be a happy medium here – it is another possible case for the Conciliation Committee. Why should we not be just as good as the Americans for once? What is stopping us?

I would like to turn to costs now. Of all the speakers so far, I am the one who has been in this House the longest, and I remember the debate about catalytic converters. To the ladies and gentlemen lobbyists up there, whose role I quite accept, I must say that I do not claim to have the exact figures, and even if I did have them, I would not trust my own figures. However, I can remember the arguments that the industry put forward then, when they told us that lead‐free petrol would cost 18 pfennigs a litre. As the ladies and gentlemen representing the oil industry know very well, the price is now less than 1.8 pfennig a litre! What was it you told us then – we can check in the Minutes – about the cost of catalytic converters? Let us take your figures – today they cost ten times less than the DM 5 000 forecast then. I just do not believe you when it comes to money and costs, because I have learnt from experience. That is why I think we should try to sort things out in the Conciliation Committee. Mr Bangemann, you know full well that up to now, when we have been involved in environmental matters, we have always come up with respectable, dare I say middle‐class results. That is a great incentive for this House. I am not thinking of giving in – the battle is only just beginning!

(Applause)

  Garosci (UPE). – (IT) Mr President, we are in the final stages of a discussion which the European institutions have been dealing with for several years. We will vote and tell the Commission and Council what direction the European Parliament wishes to give to the motor sector and the fuel sector in the near future, a future which has two specific deadlines, according to the reports being discussed: the year 2000 and the year 2005. This latter deadline of 2005 should be fixed by law and not left as a hypothetical deadline: the motor and oil industries should be given a specific time, a specific deadline for the industries themselves to be able to plan investments, developments and research: the date of 2005, to be precise. On the other hand, we need to eliminate all unrealistic measures against the objective of the single market. Members have recalled more than one of these.

What we are dealing with today is only apparently a division between the vehicle and oil sectors. In fact, the two sectors have to be united in their future planning. The objective is to make products – vehicles on the one hand and fuels and lubricants on the other – that are increasingly more ecocompatible and for that purpose increasingly more closely connected with each other. It is unthinkable now, although it has been considered in the recent past, that vehicle and oil should reason without any connection. They are not directly contrasted and it will be difficult for them to be so in the future, in the search for products for final users containing an intrinsic social commitment: the protection and improvement of the environment against pollution.

From an economic point of view, it should be more beneficial for the final user to buy a product – vehicle or petrol – that is increasingly less harmful to the environment and, consequently, the industries involved should invest in quality. Our task and that of the national institutions is to support, assist and direct the qualitative efforts made by the vehicle and oil companies.

There are also other problems to be solved: think of the problem of taxation. There are too many differences within the Community in terms of taxation on vehicles and fuels from one Member State to another, which means that some industries and sectors are penalized and their resources, which otherwise could be allocated to research and technological improvement, withdrawn. We are therefore against the amendments to the Lange report that alter Council"s common position on tax incentives.

With regard to the Auto‐Oil sector, enormous opportunities and potential revolve around the motor product: in employment, no longer in terms of quantity but above all in quality – think of vocational training; in science, with increasing research and experimentation; in the economy, because there are thousands of small and medium‐sized businesses alongside the few large industrial firms, ranging from spare parts to repairs; and, finally, in the social field.

Environmental protection is largely based on a reduction in the pollution generated by vehicles today. In these months" work, several people have regretted the proposals made by the two rapporteurs and the Commission: some industries, some geographical areas, and some old refineries that do not want to be modernized. The search for a better environment is a battle that may leave several wounded on the field. We will be able to help some of them, but we cannot stop the healthy ones who want to move ahead.

  Eisma (ELDR). ‐ (NL) Mr President, I could be very brief by saying that I fully agree with the words of Mr Florenz. To add something to this and to use my two minutes, I would like to say that, should these 314, a minimum of 314 colleagues agree with the Hautala and Lange reports tomorrow, the European Parliament will be writing history. It will mean that Parliament will have a cast‐iron negotiating position to enter into conciliation with the Council. Then we can show the citizens of Europe that we have done our utmost to remove them further from the misery of smog in the large European cities. Mr President, this is all the more true because the rapporteurs have shown that Parliament is able to meet its responsibilities by seeking clear conciliation with the Council at second reading on the standards of fuels.

To get the best end result we will have to water things down a little, however. We have to agree with a transitionary regulation for areas where socio‐economic problems make a quick, complete implementation of these directives difficult. And the Spaniards and other people from the southern Member States along with the representatives in this Parliament should take good note of this if, tomorrow, they will also agree with the amendments of both rapporteurs.

We have to stick with the compulsory standards for the year 2005, Mr President. The industry has shown itself capable of delaying Auto‐Oil I by a few years, with the result that these directives will not enter into force until 2000. The car industry requires low sulphur fuels in order to apply its newest technologies, such as the next generation catalytic converter, and we can only be certain that these will actually come onto the market if we have compulsory standards.

I therefore call on my colleagues not to be misguided by arguments which argue in the contrary. Only through compulsory standards in 2005 will industry be able to prepare itself properly. This is extremely important both for our environment and for the economy. Indicative standards will delay improvement of the environment again by years. I look forward with full confidence to the conciliation after the vote tomorrow.
