9. Question Time (Commission)

  President . – The next item is questions to the Commission (B4‐0017/98).

Part I

 

Question No 60 by Heidi Hautala (H−0006/98)

Subject: Regulation on openness

Under the Amsterdam Treaty, the institutions of the Union are to conduct themselves with considerably greater openness than at present, particularly as regards documents.

Will the Commission draft a proposal for a Regulation as soon as possible, so that the Regulation can enter into force immediately that the Amsterdam Treaty does so?

Is it intended that public access to documents should apply to all documents in the possession of the Union's institutions – i.e. legislative, implementary and administrative documents?

Does public access to documents apply to all the institutions?

What kind of exceptions does the Commission intend to propose?

Will Member States be given the right to prevent publication of documents concerning their positions?

  Monti, Member of the Commission. – (IT) The Commission would remind the author of the question that, according to the provisions of the new Article 255 introduced by the Treaty of Amsterdam, the citizen has the right of access to the documents of the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission, subject to the principles and limits the Council has to determine, according to the codecision procedure, within two years of the entry into force of the Treaty.

On the basis of these general principles which are common to the three institutions, each one should draw up its own regulations on the specific provisions regarding application. The Commission will therefore submit a draft legislation, according to the entry into force of the Treaty. So far, no decision has been taken on the exceptions to the right of access the Commission should propose.

Without any doubt, the Commission considers it essential to develop the policy of transparency and access to its documents, but the future legislation on this matter depends on the result of the interinstitutional negotiations.

Naturally, the declaration included in the final document will be observed, which allows each Member State to ask the Commission or the Council not to disclose a document originating from the country itself without prior permission.

  Hautala (V). – (FI) Mr President, I have three short questions. I would like clarification of whether this document will concern all or just three – the most important three – bodies mentioned in the Amsterdam Treaty: the Commission, Parliament and the Council. Personally, I think it should be a matter for all to consider.

Next, I would like to ask about very sensitive issues. Is it understood that Europe"s future central bank should be subordinate to public legislation? In addition, Europol cooperation has proved to be an area where there is a need for more openness.

Finally, I would like to ask whether the Commission could not put together some sort of Green Paper on the subject of openness so that all bodies, including Parliament, could discuss in broad terms what should be done concerning this document? I think this would be an excellent way to proceed.

  Monti . – (IT) As a general outline to this additional answer, I have to say, Mr Hautala, that the general secretaries of the three Institutions concerned have already held specific meetings to discuss this point and have formed an interinstitutional working party, under the direction of the Commission, which has been given the task of conducting a preliminary study of the problem. The working parties have already been set up and the group is currently reflecting on the questions to be dealt with in the proposal for the future legislation.

I wish to ask you, Mr Hautala, whether it will be applied to the three institutions? As I recalled, according to the provisions of Article 255, it is a question of access to the documents of the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission and there will be general principles common to the three institutions, on the basis of which each of the three institutions will then draw up its own regulations for specific application.

  von Habsburg (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, I would like to ask the Commissioner whether his studies also cover the internal workings of Parliament, because we have a body, the Conference of Presidents of the various groups, which keeps the Parliamentarians in the dark, and takes all sorts of decisions in secret meetings that we hear nothing about. Could this be sorted out? The second thing I wanted to ask is this: what is your attitude to the idea of forcing the European Central Bank to make a public declaration of this kind, which would have the effect of opening the doors to American speculators?. It would not take long to wreck our currency then!

  Monti . – (IT) With regard to the European Central Bank, I have nothing to say, as Article 255 does not refer to this institution. With regard to the first question, it seems to me that, as the Member said himself, it relates to the European Parliament"s internal documents and it is therefore not up to the Commission to give an opinion in this connection.

  Bonde (I‐EDN). – (DA) I would like to ask whether the Commission supports the principle of reversing the burden of proof which was passed by a large majority of Parliament in part‐session and by the Committee on Institutional Affairs by 34 votes to 0. According to this, the basis for any decision must be that the documents and meetings are open, unless a qualified majority of two thirds of those present at Commission meetings, in Parliament or at Council meetings decides that a meeting or document should be closed for good reason. In other words, we should reverse the burden of proof, so it takes a qualified majority to close a document. Can the Commission support this principle which was passed unanimously by our committees?

  Monti . – (IT) Thank you for this additional question. This is a matter I will bring to the attention of the working party formed by the general secretaries of the three institutions. I have no particular information to offer by way of an answer; I therefore take note of it and will pass on the question to the group composed of the three general secretaries.

  President . – Question No 61 by Julio Añoveros Trias de Bes (H−0048/98)

Subject: Internet and Community Law

When will the EuroLaw server be accessible through the Internet? Does the Commission intend to offer free access to the CELEX database on the Web? Do the institutions plan to provide a page providing information for the public on the Community legislative process and on the officials responsible? If so, could the institutions design such a page jointly in order to optimize its usefulness?

  Monti, Member of the Commission. – (IT) The EuroLaw server, the provisional name to which the Member refers, will make the whole text of the Official Journals available, both the L and C series, free of charge on the Internet Europa site, which can be found at http: //europa.eu.int, in the 11 official languages of the EU for a period of 20 days. This site is currently being prepared: the actual date for its opening should be in the spring of 1998 and will be announced officially by the Official Publications Office of the European Communities.

The EuroLaw server will enable users to access the following services free of charge and in progression: the list of Community legislation in force, supplemented by the texts of the documents shown in the list by their title alone, as shown in the CELEX data bank; the collection of EU treaties; the consolidated text of the Community legislative documents; the recent judgments of the European Court of Justice; and the COM documents. Consequently, all the legislative texts in force will be extracted by CELEX and made available to EuroLaw users free of charge.

For the time being, however, the Commission is not planning to offer the CELEX data bank for consultation via the Internet free of charge. It should be recalled that CELEX offers a very wide range of advanced research possibilities and that the proceeds deriving from consultation of the base are used to finance part of the considerable work of documentary analysis required to give CELEX its specific added value.

The Commission shares the Member"s opinion that it is advisable to inform citizens of the Union"s decision‐making mechanisms and the functioning of the interinstitutional procedures. For this purpose, it plans to present the principal interinstitutional procedures in the form of a diagram, in order to be concise.

Moreover, to the extent that resources are available and in cooperation with the services of Parliament, the Commission will try to circulate general information on the state of the current procedures, an objective that will be realized, in particular, through modernizing and putting the APC system that is already accessible to the public on the Internet.

  Añoveros Trias de Bes (PPE). – (ES) Mr President, I do not want to put a supplementary question, but just to say that the Commissioner gave a very full reply, for which I am extremely grateful.

  Thors (ELDR). – (SV) I thank the Commissioner for his answer. I would like to put one more question: when is it going to be clear that through this EuroLaw link people will be able to see how far the Member States have incorporated the law into national legislation? We currently have an experiment as far as Finnish legislation is concerned, but what does the Commissioner think of the possibilities of getting information about the situation in all Member States through the same service?

In addition, I think it is important that in future this server has the technology to make it easy to see what is news, so‐called ‚push technology’, which your colleague Commissioner Oreja has promised to consider, and which I hope can be implemented very soon. I believe that for easier use it is important that people also get news quickly.

  Monti . – (IT) I would like to say to Mr Thors that it had been hoped that the EuroLaw server would be available immediately on the Internet. This is not possible. To do that, preliminary adaptations need to be made to all the technical devices, particularly those of the Publications Office; everything will be ready in a few months" time. We understand the urgency and importance of this tool and every effort will be made to make it available as soon as possible.

I will take this opportunity, and the specific question raised by Mr Thors on the importance to the citizen of knowing whether the Member States have included the Community regulations, to indicate another information tool. In the wide range of material on the internal market, since November 1997 the Commission has been publishing a scoreboard, a periodic report, that indicates the situation of each Member State as regards the inclusion of directives, the observance of infringement procedures, and so on; this scoreboard is already available on the Internet. As you can see, we are certainly heading in the direction rightly hoped for by Mr Thors with all the means available.

  President . – Question No 62 by Ian White (H−0112/98)

Subject: First‐weight mail rates

Would the Commission indicate which EU Member States currently link costs of first‐weight correspondence, usually 20 g letters, for domestic and internal (within the EU) mail? At present, the current UK rate is the same for first‐weight mail, but there are proposals for this to change. Would the UK postal services be acting contrary to Article 6 by charging more for non‐domestic internal first‐weight mail?

  Monti, Member of the Commission. – (IT) In around half the Member States of the European Union, there is a unified tariff for sending correspondence at national and intra‐Community level in the faster standard category belonging to the first‐weight group. In the other Member States, tariffs vary according to the destination, usually depending on whether it is national or intraCommunity correspondence.

Directive 97/67/EC on postal services states that the prices of each of the services forming part of the supply of the universal service should be correlated with costs. This obligation applies to all the correspondence in question, whether national or intraCommunity. This could encourage some Member States to rectify the current charging system for their own postal services to that effect.

The Treaty establishing the European Community, and particularly Article 6, does not prohibit a similar tariff differentiation provided it is commensurate with costs, as laid down by the aforesaid Directive 97/67/EC.

  White (PSE). – I think it would be useful for me to explain the background of the question to the Commissioner. A change in pricing to the structure of the international tariff within the United Kingdom is proposed from 6 April this year. At present the first‐weight rate charge is the same for domestic and EU post. The proposal is for domestic post to stay at 26p but for EU post to increase to 30p. There is also a proposal that the non‐EU rate be decreased from 31p to 30p which effectively classes the EU alongside all other foreign post.

I would submit that is contrary to Article 6 and therefore there is a responsibility upon the Commission to ask for an explanation from the United Kingdom postal authorities on this point. Can you please reply in detail?

  Monti . – (IT) I wish to thank Mr White for further clarifying the context of his question.

There may actually be an apparent contradiction between single tariff and price correlated with costs, but it is only an apparent contradiction. Article 12 of the Directive on postal services states, on the one hand, that prices shall be correlated with costs and, on the other, that the Member States may decide whether to apply a single tariff to the entire national territory. These two statements may seem to be contradictory. However, the obligation on the correlation of costs does not imply a total coincidence between price and cost and leaves the Member State the possibility of applying a system of making tariffs equal for specific provisions of the universal service.

I would add that applying a tariff distinction based on costs between national and intra‐Community post or, possibly, between post to different Member States, does not constitute an infringement of Article 6 of the Treaty, as such a distinction is not based on nationality but rather on the criterion of the actual cost the provision of different services entails.

  President . – Question No 63 by Anne Van Lancker (H−0114/98)

Subject: Preparations for integrating the Schengen acquis in the Treaty

According to the Treaty, the Schengen measures on checks at external borders, asylum, immigration and judicial cooperation in civil matters should come under Title IV of the Treaty establishing the European Community, while the provisions on police and judicial cooperation in criminal matters should come under Title VI of the Treaty on European Union, the third pillar. In the Commission's view, what should the SIS (and later the EIS) come under? Article 286 of the Treaty says that from 1 January 1999 Community decisions on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing and free movement of personal data shall apply to the institutions and bodies set up by, or on the basis of, the Treaty. What arrangements should there be for the protection of privacy in the SIS? Will Article 286 also apply to personal data gathered under third pillar initiatives?

  Monti, Member of the Commission. – (IT) The question raised by Mrs Van Lancker is fundamental in nature and requires a brief but comprehensive answer. The principle of the integration of the Schengen acquis into the EU has been sanctioned in the protocol attached to the Treaty of Amsterdam. To achieve this integration, various measures have been adopted, including the decision by the Council, as provided for in paragraph 1 of Article 2 of the protocol, based on the relevant provisions of the Treaties, establishing the legal basis for each of the provisions or decisions constituting the Schengen acquis.

Pursuant to the provisions of the Treaty on European Union currently in force, the distribution of the Schengen acquis between the first and the third pillars would have been a very difficult task on account of the unclear distinction between the areas of competence of the two pillars mentioned. However, the Treaty of Amsterdam provides greater clarity and the distribution should not raise too many problems.

As regards, in particular, the division of the provisions on the Schengen Information System, the Commission considers that a dual legal base should be established in relation to the first and third pillars. In fact, the Schengen Information System collects data relating to both the first and third pillars. Article 96 of the Schengen Agreement, on the categories of aliens to be refused entry, refers to external frontier controls and visa and immigration policy and therefore to data belonging to the first pillar.

Other categories of data, such as definitions provided for extradition purposes, unquestionably fall under police and legal cooperation in criminal matters and therefore come under the third pillar.

The establishment of a dual legal base should not be detrimental to the operation of the Schengen Information System. The dual legal base is not something new. The Customs Information System is also based on a regulation from the first pillar and an agreement from the third pillar based on the same text.

With regard to specific Schengen provisions on data protection within the scope of the Schengen Information System, a dual legal base should be established, comprising the first and third pillars. The same regulations should be applied to data protection with regard to sectors coming under the first and third pillars, in the exchange of information through the SIS. Finally, the question as to whether Article 286 of the EC Treaty will be applied to the Schengen Information System has not yet been resolved.

  Van Lancker (PSE). ‐ (NL) I would like to start by thanking the Commissioner for his interpretation. I think it agrees fully with my own. The necessary instruments for the implementation of the new Community pillar with regards to free movement of people cannot come under the third pillar. But, Mr Commissioner, I am getting strong indications from the Council, and not only from one Member State within the Council, but from several of them, that the Council has a completely different opinion. It seems that someone in the Council is saying quite forcefully that the entire Schengen Information System, and therefore also the European Information System, should come under the third pillar, with all its consequences for parliamentary and judicial control and for the European Commission"s right of legislative initiative in this rather delicate affair. Can the Commissioner tell me which arguments, legal and political, the Commission can produce to win over the Council, and that it will not start fiddling with the adequate legal base which must be used in this matter so that the Schengen Protocol might be properly categorized?

  Monti . – (IT) I have to say that current efforts and the work I have briefly described is ongoing, despite being complex. In the declaration relating to Article 2 of the Schengen Protocol, the Member States agree that the Council should take all the necessary measures laid down by Article 2 as well as that relating therefore to the distribution just mentioned, by the date of entry into force of the Treaty of Amsterdam. For this purpose, it establishes that the preparatory work should begin in good time to be completed before that date.

I have to say that, after the signing of the Treaty of Amsterdam, the Luxembourg Presidency began work within the Council and that the current British Presidency has taken it over immediately. Various meetings are already planned to observe the timetable which has been established. Some results have already been achieved, and this allows us to believe that the deadlines will be observed. We therefore have no reason to be pessimistic.

I well understand the Member"s concern, but it is obvious that the Commission has taken an active and constructive part in the Council"s work on this matter, to ensure that the integration of the Schengen acquis, within the scope of the European Union, takes place in full compliance with the law of the European Community and the European Union.

It seems obvious to me that the European Parliament is involved in distributing the Schengen acquis, as this distribution will establish the legal base for future proposals seeking to amend the Schengen acquis and therefore relates to Parliament"s role with regard to these future proposals.

I therefore propose that the Council duly submits to the European Parliament the draft decision on the distribution of the Schengen acquis.

  Sjöstedt (GUE/NGL). – (SV) My question about the Schengen Information System concerns the supervisory function of the JSA, the Joint Supervisory Authority . This authority is responsible for checking that the rules which exist for this system are respected. The first report issued by this supervisory authority pointed out major defects in the SIS system, including inaccurate information on those registered. In addition, it states that there is different information about the same people in different parts of the SIS database.

The JSA also emphasizes that it has totally inadequate resources to carry out the investigation it wants, including budgetary resources and resources for translation. It also underlines that it has been denied access to the SIS computer here in Strasbourg on at least one occasion when it has tried to carry out an on‐site inspection with its experts.

My question is whether the Commission intends to strengthen the JSA's budgetary resources and authority to carry out its work. Is it the Commission's opinion that this should happen?

  Monti . – (IT) With regard to the Joint Supervisory Authority, the entry into force of the Treaty of Amsterdam naturally has to be dealt with. However, with regard to the other aspects of the question, I believe that only the actual bodies involved in the Schengen Convention are in the best position to give more thorough answers.

  Posselt (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, Commissioner, I think it has become clear how closely connected Title IV of the EC Treaty and Title VI of the EU Treaty are. I would like to ask you about the connection between the issue of external borders, which is dealt with in one, and the issue of fighting organized crime, which is dealt with in the other. I think we really need to proceed with the Schengen Information System accordingly and see the connection between the two.

  Monti . – (IT) Your comment highlights the very purpose of these constructions: to provide space for freedom of movement and at the same time space for security. Schengen arose within this perspective and is operating within this perspective; its incorporation into the Treaty of Amsterdam is also moving decidedly within this perspective. The information structures should reflect this fundamental dual objective.

  President . – Question No 64 by Graham Watson (H−0147/98)

Subject: Ticket allocation system for the 1998 football World Cup

The French authorities have proposed a ticket allocation system for the 1998 football World Cup which limits worldwide sales for matches to just 40 % of total tickets, while 60 % of tickets will be restricted to the French market.

In view of the precedent set by the “Cassis de Dijon’ judgment, does the Commission agree that the proposed ticket allocation is a fundamental breach of internal market legislation? Does the Commission have plans to investigate the proposed ticket sales operation?

  Papoutsis, Member of the Commission. – (EL) Mr President, the French organizing committee of the World Cup at first envisaged a system of ticket distribution based on territorial exclusiveness which would essentially not have permitted the sale of tickets in other Member States of the European Union. It also envisaged linking ticket sales to other services such as accommodation and travel. Following an approach by the European Commission, the organizing committee agreed to make changes which would enable both specific tour operators and travel agencies firstly, to sell either tickets alone or packages of services, and secondly, to sell them in other countries. This ensures competition between various distributors throughout the European Union.

After complaints in the press regarding the infringement of European rules of competition so far as the sale of tickets for the football World Cup 1998 was concerned, the Commission has approached the French organizing committee and five tour operators selected for the sale of tickets in the UEFA area. Replies to those letters are being awaited and we believe that when we receive them the Commission will be able to form a complete picture of how the French organizing committee set about distributing tickets, and to ascertain whether and to what extent the rules of competition have been contravened. If Community law has been infringed, that will most probably be due to contravention of the European rules of competition, mainly Articles 85 and 86 of the Treaty, rather than an infringement of the law in relation to the Cassis de Dijon affair mentioned by the Member, which covers the elimination of quota restrictions on imports between the Member States pursuant to Article 30 of the Treaty. At any rate, no such infringement has so far been confirmed where the distribution of tickets for the football World Cup 1998 is concerned.

  President . – Thank you very much, Mr Papoutsis.

Mr Watson, the Rules of Procedure do not include any rule prohibiting Members from bringing a ball into the Chamber, but I assume there is no trademark or any subliminal advertising on that beautiful football which I can see from here. In any case, you have the floor to question the Commissioner for one minute.

  Watson (ELDR). – I assure you that I have no intention to kick this football in the Chamber but it is an excellent example of a souvenir World Cup football.

I am grateful to the Commissioner for his answer. Clearly under Article 7a of the Single European Act, and indeed under Article 85, it must be held that any product lawfully put on sale in one part of the Union cannot be barred from being imported into any other. Indeed, the Commission has just fined Volkswagen some £68 million for breach of this principle.

It is my information that the French authorities are preventing ticket agencies and tour operators from selling tickets outside France. A ticket, for example, for the Brazil v Scotland match which would cost £50 in France, has been on sale on the black market in Britain for nearly £800. Did the French authorities consult the Commission before making their arrangements?

Does the Commissioner not agree that this is outrageous and does he not agree that it contravenes EU principles? Will he take action against France if it is found to be in breach of the Treaties?

  Papoutsis . – (EL) Mr President, first of all let me tell you that the ball at the front of Mr Watson's bench reminds me of my childhood years, when I too had the chance to engage in this wonderful sport. I would, however, like to make some comments on what Mr Watson said. As I mentioned before, we are waiting for replies to the letters we sent to the tour operators and the French authorities. So far, we have no information confirming the accusations that a specific infringement is indeed taking place. What I must say, however, as a general principle, is that the Commission believes that competition among the various suppliers and products certainly benefits the consumer compared with a system of exclusive distribution and committed ticket sales. We will insist that the rules of competition must be applied, and we believe we will be successful.

Part II
Questions addressed to Mr Papoutsis

 

  President . – As they deal with the same subject, the following questions will be taken together:

Question No 65 by Ludivina García Arias (H−0066/98)

Subject: Production and job cuts in the Spanish mining industry

On what quantitative criteria is the Commission basing its insistence that the Director‐General for Energy in the Spanish Mining Planning Department call on companies under contract to cut production and jobs by specific percentages? Does the Commission really believe that it is admissible under Decision No 3632/93/ECSC(1) to observe the criterion of “comparable effort to other countries’, given that the concept of “Community average cost’ was dropped while the Decision was being discussed and approved by the Council and Parliament? Does the Commission consider it possible to equate different national situations in terms of energy dependence and employment and economic development indicators? Is it not the case that the abandonment or drastic cut in coal production in the United Kingdom, France, and Belgium resulted from energy policy decisions taken by those countries acting on their own authority?

Question No 66 by Juan Izquierdo Collado (H−0091/98)

Subject: Principle of dialogue between management and labour

What importance does the Commission attach to the principle of dialogue between management and labour, as referred to in the Treaty on European Union, bearing in mind that a mining agreement between the Spanish Government and the trade unions is being challenged by the Brussels authorities?

Has the Commission heard that the letter in which the Director‐General for Energy raises objections to some points in the agreement has led to the prospect of an industrial dispute?

  Papoutsis, Member of the Commission. – (EL) Mr President, thank you for giving me the opportunity to give a global answer to the questions raised by the Members, but of course I hope that I will have your understanding, because my answer will take up some time.

First of all, I want to express the Commission's concern at the social and regional consequences of the recession affecting the European Union's coal industry. That recession is the consequence of the geological difficulties encountered by the industry and the exceptionally high cost of production they lead to. The production costs of Spanish coalmines are unfortunately no exception. The Commission is aware of the difficulties engendered by the necessary economic changes in some areas. That is why, as an exception, it provided in Decision 3632/93/ECSC that Member States had the right to provide aid to companies with no prospect of economic viability for the cessation of their activities, so permitting them to adapt gradually to market realities within the framework of winding up their activities.

On that point, Mrs García Arias asked us whether the abandonment or significant reduction of coal production in France, Belgium and the UK was the result of sovereign decisions by those countries themselves concerning their energy policy. Clearly, the decisions mentioned in the question are based mainly on awareness in the Member States in question that the future of the industry is very bleak in some areas. That is why they decided that the cost of subsidies from overall social resources should be channelled towards public expenditure that supports activities with a guaranteed and secure future. From the Commission's point of view, all those decisions were the subject of examination and conclusions based on Community law. The specific question, along with others put by various Members of Parliament, seems to call the Commission's role into question in relation to coal, when decisions in that branch have an impact on issues of energy policy, social dialogue and employment.

I remind you, therefore, that among the European Commission's direct responsibilities is to approve subsidies which Member States propose to provide for the coal mining industry, pursuant to Decision 3632/93/ECSC. And in carrying out that task, the Commission pays full respect to the national particularities to which honourable Members have referred, and secures the cooperation of the states with due respect for their own respective competences. Besides, the Commission is ready and willing to listen to all interested parties, who have the right to present their views and comments by virtue of Article 46 of the ECSC Treaty.

Mrs García Arias asked about the criteria used as a basis by the Commission within the framework of the Community system for intervention by the Member States on behalf of the coal industry, of 28 October 1993. I would not wish to go into details at this time concerning how that matter is being investigated, since the Commission is doing this at the moment and there need to be questions added relating to competition in the common market for coal. I can tell you, however, that in analysing the programmes for winding up the activities of companies, the Commission will not base its procedures on criteria of comparative efforts with other countries, and I believe that Mrs García Arias is fully aware of developments concerning the coal mines in the countries mentioned in her question.

In the case of Asturias, the Commission takes account of the exceptional social and regional factors mentioned in Article 4 of Decision 3632/93/ECSC. Consequently, it accepts that the units should close down after 23 July 2002, but within the scope of a programme of gradual and continuous running‐down of the activity, which should lead to a substantial decrease well before the aforesaid date when the ECSC Treaty lapses. In that context, the Commission, which is considering a file on programmes for the discontinuation of activities by various companies, has made certain observations and asked for certain information, to leave it in a position to arrive at a specific opinion. These observations are justified, firstly, by the serious deviations noted during implementation of the programmes by specific companies between 1994 and 1997, and secondly, because their aim is to bring the programmes announced into line with the objectives of Article 4 of Decision 3632/93/ECSC. Based on its decisions, therefore, the Commission is entitled, whenever it makes observations or renders an opinion, to refer to quantified objectives. I believe the observations made by the Commission are realistic ones, and ones which will not affect either the social conditions of workers about to lose their jobs, or the regional reconstruction programmes envisaged in the planning.

I think it is also useful to recall that in connection with policy on coal, it did not prove possible in the discussions with the Council to adopt the principle of the Community reference cost, because it was deemed that this would constitute an unduly technical measure. For that very reason, agreement was reached to apply a principle of scaling down aid in the light of coal prices on the international market, a factor which is also a quantitative criterion. Indeed, the aid would not have been regarded as transitional, except only to permit eventual competitiveness or to contribute towards solving the social and regional problems associated with the run‐down of the branch.

Mr Izquierdo Collado wonders about the importance attached by the Commission to the principle of social dialogue. I must say, and I say so here, that the European Commission attaches great importance to that principle, which is embodied in the Treaty on European Union. In the same spirit, it ensures that agreements signed in the Member States, including their legislative orders, must comply with the Treaty on European Union. It is therefore permissible for the Commission to examine the agreements signed between the Spanish government and the trade union organizations, and verify their compliance with the Treaty before they are implemented.

I would like to look at the conflict alluded to by Mr Izquierdo Collado. As the Commission carries out the responsibilities assigned to it under Decision 3632/93/ECSC, it is investigating, and I repeat, it is still investigating, the file with the restructuring plans in relation to which the granting of state subsidies to Spain's coal industry will be analysed. Precisely because of the complexity of the problem, and because of the climate prevailing today in the Community's coal industry, the Commission is compelled, under the provisions of the specific Decision, to carry out in‐depth investigations so that it is in a position to render an opinion on the issue as a whole. If it is confirmed that this is consistent with Community law, the Commission will then give the green light for the provision of that aid. And in that framework the procedures for investigating these matters are covered by confidentiality, and are therefore not the source of social conflict as the Member implied. Since we are speaking about social dialogue, it is appropriate to stress that none of the parties has approached the Commission to investigate in advance, matters that fall within the Commission's authority.

  García Arias (PSE). – (ES) I am afraid the Commissioner has not answered my question. I am asking him to tell me the quantitative criteria, to explain to me why the Commission said that the reduction of several thousand workers, their jobs, and hundreds of tonnes was not enough and instead set out specific percentages. I do not know those criteria and I would like an answer.

Furthermore, since we are being so precise, I should like to ask the Commission why it did not ask the Spanish government, in that same letter, why it has not yet included all the additional aid in the Spanish budget. Why is there so much pressure on one side, while the Spanish government is being shown flexibility in the other respect? I would be very interested to know whether Article 4 actually describes case by case – taking into account the regional criteria – the criteria used by the Directorate‐General for Energy in saying that the reduction had to take one form or another, especially in view of the fact that negotiations had already taken place in Spain.

  Papoutsis . – (EL) Mr President, I want to assure the Member that the European Commission implements the Treaty in all countries and in all cases in exactly the same way. There are no special cases and there are no special interpretations in each case. However, the Commission must render an opinion about the extent to which any programme announced by the Member States responds to the general and particular objectives of Decision 3632/93/ECSC. That is also exactly the lodestone that guides our actions. Naturally, we recognize that the future of the coal industry is something of concern to us all. It is of concern not just to the regions and not just to the Member States. It is a subject to which the European Union should give particular weight, precisely because of the major social and economic problems created in all the countries. It is with this in mind that we will take further action. As for the case in question, there were no prior consultations with the Spanish government. What did happen, however, was that an effort was made by the Commission to get close to the problem, to monitor developments and to examine all the proposals submitted. Of course, we had contacts and cooperated with the unions and with civil servants. But there were no political negotiations or preliminary agreements between the Commission and the Spanish government.

  Izquierdo Collado (PSE). – (ES) Commissioner, I would like to focus on the purpose of the question, so as not to generalize. And the purpose of the question is that an agreement was reached between the mining unions and the Spanish government, and received massive media coverage. Along comes the European Union and starts criticizing and trying to add further cuts in jobs and production; and to do so, it uses the Spaniard, Mr Benavides, the Director‐General of Energy. People in Asturias are saying that Mr Benavides is being encouraged by the Spanish government itself, which had already signed that agreement. There is incredible frustration, huge tension in the sector, acts of extremism, street violence, a death in Asturias, a return to negotiations, a new agreement between the Spanish government and the mining unions, and now the Commission has to say its piece again. The Commission now has to say whether or not it is happy with this second agreement, although it is very similar to the first one.

When are we going to start sticking up for the European Union"s image and stop acting against our own image in such an incredible manner? It gives us a worse image than we already had.

  Papoutsis . – (EL) Mr President, first I would like to make it clear that the European Commission expresses itself through its Commissioners. The Commission's political positions are valid when expressed by its Commissioners and mainly by those responsible for the sector in question.

I am not aware of the comments by the specific Director‐General you have mentioned, Mr Benavides. Mr Benavides is doing his job very properly and I do not know exactly what he said, nor do I know whether his comments were reported correctly. What I would like to confirm, however, and what I know, is what I myself have said: my own comments, and the expression of my own political will, both to the Spanish government and to the Spanish press. And you also know that my positions are very clear. We are waiting for the replies, we are waiting for the agreement to be completed and we will then examine it from the standpoint of its economic, but also more importantly its social consequences. In no case does the Commission intend either to create social explosions or to contribute towards exacerbating the social problems created. On the contrary, indeed, we are striving to find a solution based on the ECSC Treaty, based on the European Union Treaty, and of course based on the Spanish government's priorities, on those of the Asturias region and on local requirements.

We are always ready not only to discuss the specific agreement between the unions, the government and the companies, but are also ready to approve programmes which could contribute towards the development of the area in coming years, alternative forms of employment, the creation of other forms of economic development – and along those lines we are on the side of the people of Asturias –, and, of course, all the social and economic forces in the area. Along those lines, then, and I repeat, on the basis of the legitimacy conferred by the ECSC and the European Union Treaties, the Commission will cooperate both with the government and with the unions.

  González Álvarez (GUE/NGL). – (ES) Commissioner, I am afraid our questions are the same. Mrs García Arias, Mr Morán López, Mr Puerta and myself had presented a question along the same lines, because it is true that there was a letter from Mr Benavides, and that letter was made public and produced a conflict with serious consequences in Asturias.

The question raises the fact that it is not just the mining situation that needs to be taken into account – the mining situation in Asturias is very serious – but also the situations in the iron and steel industry, shipbuilding, the rural areas and arms manufacture. In other words, a whole series of industrial sectors are all in decline, making Asturias a problem region.

The question is: considering not just the European Coal and Steel Community Treaty and the mining situation, but also considering all these aspects of industrial decline in Asturias, will this situation of decline be taken into account in the agreement which the unions have just reached, an agreement which is new and which seems at least to satisfy the hopes of the mining areas?

  Papoutsis . – (EL) Mr President, yet again I would like to confirm that with particular care and political sensitivity the Commission will rely on consideration of the specific file so far as the coal industry in the Asturias region is concerned. I repeat that we are prepared to cooperate in the implementation of special programmes aiming to create new jobs.

However, so far as the agreement is concerned, the Commission has not yet completed its consideration. On the contrary, we are waiting for all the details and information so that we can prepare our recommendation to the European Commission, because as you know, the Commission's decision is the result of a collective process and it will be a collective decision by the body of Commissioners. But we must have all the facts and details so that we can justify the agreement reached.

I sincerely hope it will turn out to be within the legitimacy conferred by the Treaty, and that the agreement will therefore be approved by the Commission. In no case – I repeat – in no case does the Commission intend to cast fuel on the flames, or to contribute towards creating social problems. On the contrary, we will be on the side of the Spanish government, the productive and social forces of the country and the Asturias region, to find the best possible solution.

  President . – As the author is not present, Question No 67 lapses.

Questions addressed to Mrs Bjerregaard

 

Question No 68 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0054/98)

Subject: Community eco‐label award scheme

The Commission's proposal for a revised eco‐label award scheme proposes that all national eco‐labelling schemes should be confined to products for which no Community eco‐label criteria have been established. Moreover, the Commission proposes that the Community scheme should be self‐financing after four years.

It is very difficult for eco‐label schemes to become self‐financing; not even the most well‐established national eco‐label schemes manage to be so.

In the Commission's view, what will be the impact of replacing familiar, well‐established eco‐label schemes, which enjoy a high level of consumer confidence, with a relatively unknown Community scheme which is also to be self‐financing after only four years?

Can the Commission give any good examples of eco‐label schemes which are self‐financing? How is the environmental movement guaranteed influence over the Community's eco‐label scheme?

  Bjerregaard, Member of the Commission. – (DA) Mr President, on the Commission's proposal to review the regulations on ecolabelling schemes, the European environmental marking organization (EMO) will receive financial support from the Commission for the first four years. EMO will also be entitled to 50 % of the annual eco‐labelling charges from the organizations responsible. These organizations will cover any additional costs. It is true that the Commission will not continue supporting the organization financially, but the EMO will be funded via the annual eco‐labelling charges, and the organizations responsible will continue to assist the EMO financially. The proceeds of the Community regulation charges depend on how successful the regulations are.

It may be worth stating that, in a single year and for a single product, the Hoover New Wave washing machine, the British ecolabelling council received more than ECU 250 000. According to information from the responsible organizations in the EC, the total income from EC charges in May 1997 – which involves 30 licences – was ECU 210 000. Since January 1998, we have already issued 35 licences for a total of 192 products. It is therefore highly likely that the EMO will be self‐financing. One positive development will depend on the EMO's ability to establish a number of product groups and the ability of the organizing bodies' markets capacity to acquire licences. It is important to emphasize on this point that a ceiling has been introduced for charges of ECU 40 000.

The Commission does not believe there will be any financial implications for national eco‐labels, as a transitional period has been allowed of five years in which it will be possible to market the European arrangements. In receiving the European credits, which are probably higher than national ones, the authorities will benefit from the new arrangements. And, finally, non‐governmental environmental organizations will be more closely involved with the EMO than under the current system.

  Sjöstedt (GUE/NGL). – (SV) I would like to thank Mrs Bjerregaard for her reply. I have two additional questions. The first question concerns what will happen after the five‐year transition period, i.e. when the Community eco‐label replaces the national eco‐labels where Community labels exist. If I have understood correctly, in practice there will be a ban on national labels in the areas covered by the Union's Community label. Moreover, this ban will apply whether or not the national eco‐labels make tougher demands on the products than the European eco‐label. Is that a correct understanding?

My second question concerns the influence of the environmental movement. There are very big differences between various countries in how much influence the environmental movement has over eco‐labels. How will a national environmental organization actually be able to influence the new eco‐label? How can people actively participate in this process? I think, you see, that this is very important for the credibility of the labelling.

  Bjerregaard . – (DA) In reply to that question, I would like to say that there is at present a Commission proposal which the Council has not yet dealt with at all. In other words, we are provisionally discussing the proposals we have put forward, and of course there has been some consideration as to what will happen about national systems and the European system. When we look at the successful, efficient national schemes which exist – the German system with the blue angel and the Scandinavian system with the swan –, it is actually quite clear that national schemes mainly confer environmental markings on national products. The allocation is very clearly out of balance. If an environmental mark is to have any meaning in European terms, it must of course cover Europe as a whole. So we decided that a five‐year transitional period would make it possible to bring national schemes into line with a European system so we end up with a situation which is better and not worse, as the Member asking the question indicates by referring to a situation where we had higher standards than we would have under the European system. It is definitely not our intention that that should be the result.

Nothing has been said about what the response will be to what happens after the five years. This is a period of adjustment and we believe it can proceed peacefully and smoothly. With the new structure for the proposals, we believe that the environmental organizations will have more opportunity for involvement than they did under the old system. But I am aware that the environmental organizations themselves did not think so, and so we have also been in touch with them to ensure that there are no further misunderstandings as such.

  President . – Question No 69 by Antonios Trakatellis (H−0056/98/rev. 1)

Subject: Infringement of Community environment law by planned relocation of tanneries in the region of Daphni, Viotia

Is the Commission aware of all the facts in connection with the relocation of tanneries and the Greek Government's unlawful approval of the construction of a tannery complex in the region of Daphni, Viotia? Is it aware, in particular, of the protests by the local authorities and residents concerning the site and the infringement of the directives on assessing the environmental impact of certain public or private projects, on the conservation of the natural habitats of wild flora and fauna, the protection of wild birds and the decision concerning the Natura network?

How was joint funding of the project approved without any reference to the manifest public opposition and the risks of impairing the quality of life and the environment? Is it possible for the Regional Fund to finance a project which is in breach of Community law?

  Bjerregaard, Member of the Commission. – (DA) The Commission is aware of the project to relocate tanneries, as it comes under Community funding as part of the operational programme for industry under the Community support framework for Greece. The project aims to set up a tannery at Bootia in Greece, to which old tanneries spread over a number of other areas, including the Greater Athens area, can be moved in accordance with the new technological and environmental requirements. A number of studies were carried out at a previous stage, to ensure that the information required to implement the projects is available, even if they have not actually started yet. The Greek authorities responsible found that it was necessary to assess the effects on the environment on the basis of the project's nature. This assessment was carried out under the national provisions in implementing Directive 85/337: and, at the end of the procedure, the minister approved the project with a number of environmental requirements to be observed.

The Commission does not have any detailed information on the assessment, as this procedure is the responsibility of the national authorities, and the Commission only intervenes in the event of any infringements. A formal complaint about the project, and the EIA in particular, reached the Commission on 22 January 1998. The Commission's departments are in the process of examining this complaint in accordance with current procedures, while at the same time the matter will be raised at the next meeting of the regulatory committee for the operational programme for the industry. The Greek authorities will then provide the information required.

As part of hearing the complaint, the Commission's departments will gather more information on the precise position of the project to establish whether an area which is designated, or which may be designated, by the Greek authorities under Directive 79/409, or which is proposed as a special preservation area of Community significance under Directive 92/43 with a view to inclusion in the Natura 2000 project, should be affected by the project.

  Trakatellis (PPE). – (EL) I thank the Commissioner for her answer to my question. In other words, if I have understood correctly, if these environmental studies which have not been done yet are submitted and there are consequences for the environment, there will be no cofinancing and the project will not go ahead. Because I cannot understand how cofinance was decided upon without a related study of environmental impact, when there is a great deal of opposition from the public and when the area is a known biotope. The Gulf of Evvoia, into which the tannery wastes will be discharged, is a closed sea. How is it possible, therefore, that cofinance has been approved without the related plans the Commissioner has just mentioned? And if there are these impacts on the environment – which there are –, I would like cofinancing to cease.

  Bjerregaard . – (DA) As I said in my reply, the position is that the Commission has received a complaint and has therefore been obliged to follow procedures: in other words, we are in the process of gathering the information required and, if there is any substance to the complaint, we will of course take the necessary action in connection with the project. But, as things are at the moment, I cannot say whether there is any substance to the complaint or not. What I can tell Mr Trakatellis is that we have received the complaint and that we are dealing with it.

  President . – Question No 70 by Anna Karamanou (H−0058/98)

Subject: Pollution of the Aegean

The ecological balance in the broader region of the eastern Mediterranean is seriously threatened by hundreds of pollutant industries located on the Turkish coast which, according to the UN, the OECD and the Ministry for the Aegean, have no sewage treatment facilities, in breach of international regulations. The pollutants discharged into the Aegean archipelago exceed the internationally accepted limits and constitute a veritable toxic bomb in the region. The nightmare is compounded by Turkey's recent decision to set up a nuclear plant on its south coast in the earthquake‐prone region of Akkuyu.

In what manner does the Commission intend to intervene and what measures will it take to safeguard the environmental balance in the region?

  Bjerregaard, Member of the Commission. – (DA) The situation to which the Member refers concerns environmental problems whose origins lie outside the area of the Community, so Community directives do not apply. The Commission suggested going into partnership with Turkey in the energy field as long ago as 1995; but restrictions on implementing the financial partnership with that country made it impossible to make any progress on that point. The Commission's communication on further developments in connection with Turkey of 15 July 1997 also proposed increasing cooperation in energy to safeguard the viable development of that country and integrate this sector into other economic policies. This cooperation means that funds are made available via the Community's budget.

Turkey is, however, a party to the convention on protecting the Mediterranean from pollution, known as the Barcelona Convention, and the special protocol on protecting the Mediterranean against land‐based pollution. Turkey is therefore bound to abide by the conditions of these international conventions insofar as they apply here. On the other hand, it is the Turkish government's responsibility alone to take the necessary decisions with a view to dealing with the pollution threatening the Aegean.

As far as Turkey's plans to build a nuclear power station in the Akkuyu area are concerned, it should be remembered that a decision on this subject, which is Turkey's responsibility alone, is based on an assessment of the country's overall energy requirements. This is not a project, however, which is of significance to the industrialized nations of the Community. The closing date for the Turkish authorities' international call for tenders to build the Akkuyu power station was 15 October 1997; and Turkey is currently considering tenders from the various consortia. The Turkish energy minister has stated that the final decision will be made in April 1998. The minister also said that the station is expected to become operable in 2006. From the information which the Commission has obtained, it appears that the Turkish authorities chose the Akkuyu area after a number of thorough surveys of the seismic, geological and environmental conditions, conducted by the specialist departments of a number of Turkish universities.

  Karamanou (PSE). – (EL) Madam Commissioner, thank you very much for your answers. Being aware of your sensitivity, however, I would have expected you to announce some specific measures to protect the Aegean, that uniquely beautiful archipelago, which is a precious heritage for the European Union. We know, of course, that pollution knows no frontiers, but we believe the European Union, via its cooperation agreements with Turkey, can exert pressure to make that country comply with its international obligations. Turkey has signed the international Barcelona Convention and there is no excuse for it to have such high pollution limits. For example, the cadmium limit set by Turkey is 2 mg, or 10 times higher than the European Union's limit. In addition, only 20 % of its industries have appropriate anti‐pollution installations. I believe the European Union can exert pressure on Turkey to comply with its international obligations, as we do with other countries much further away from the European Union, countries with no shared borders with the European Union, where we impose environmental protection clauses.

  Bjerregaard . – (DA) I note that questions have been raised here in Parliament on this subject on a number of occasions, and I am afraid I really do not have anything further to add. I do of course agree with Mrs Karamanou that we will use the opportunities which international conventions offer, and it is clear that the obligations contained in the Barcelona Convention will, of course, also apply to Turkey. My information is that they will be, but in view of the question and supplementary remarks, I will be happy to make sure once more that we are using the opportunities available.

  Kaklamanis (UPE). – (EL) Madam Commissioner, apart from the issue raised by my colleague, Mrs Karamanou, there are two other problems relating to that plant. The first is that, according to the views of Canadian scientists and Turkish professors Mr Kilik and Mr Kantiroglou at the University of Ankara, those reactors, which are Candu reactors from Canada, can represent a very specific method to enrich their nuclear waste with plutonium so that the plant in question becomes a nuclear weapons plant. That is one problem. The second is that both the International Atomic Energy Agency in Vienna and the corresponding commissions in Canada and the United States have severely criticized the quality of the Canadian Candu stations so far as nuclear safety is concerned.

How can the European Union be providing funds for the closure of the plants in Chernobyl and Koslodui, and not be interested in nuclear reactors which already present problems, as was stressed at the Environment Conference in Kyoto, at which Europe took part and where Argentina and South Korea, countries which have such reactors, decided to close them down for safety reasons.

  President . – Would you all please keep to your allotted time, otherwise other questions will suffer and will not be able to receive an oral reply.

Mrs Bjerregaard, you have the floor to reply, but please could you also be as brief as possible.

  Bjerregaard . – (DA) In return, I can keep it short, because I have nothing to add to the reply which I gave earlier. I am not aware of the particular problems to which the Member refers. They are not apparent from any of the documents I have at my disposal, so unfortunately I am not able to go into these questions in detail.

  Rübig (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, last year the Committee on the Environment, Public Health and Consumer Protection visited Athens. There is an excellent sewage treatment plant there, and they were building the second stage of it at the time. Could you tell us when the second stage will be completed?

  Bjerregaard . – (DA) I must say that Mr Rübig has great faith in my ability to know everything that is happening in the European Union. But I must say that, in the absence of any notice, I am not of course in a position to reply to the question raised.

  President . – Thank you very much, Madam Commissioner.

Mr McMahon and Mr Eisma wish to speak on a point of order.

I will give them the floor, in accordance with the Rules of Procedure, but I would remind them that other questions will be deprived of the time they use up, and the Commission may only be able to reply in writing.

  McMahon (PSE). – Mr President, I rise on a point of order and I make no apology for this. I think there should be some equity in the way you bring your hammer down. I have been sitting here for some time. It took Commissioner Papoutsis twelve minutes to answer Mrs García Arias' question. Surely Commissioners should be taught – and if you check the Rules you will find this in Annex II, paragraph 7 – that Commission answers should be brief. Will the services have a discussion with the Commission? You are always hammering down the Members for one minute and you are allowing Commissioners to get away with blue murder by taking up twelve minutes. Members who have tabled questions and sat here patiently to ask questions have been denied the opportunity to have their European constituents' problems answered.

  President . – Thank you, Mr McMahon. I have made a note of your comment, but let me remind you that Mr Papoutsis was replying jointly to two questions, one by Mrs García Arias and one by Mr Izquierdo Collado. So those twelve minutes have to be divided between two questions.

Mr Eisma has the floor on a point of order.

  Eisma (ELDR). – (NL) Firstly, I would like to suggest to my Greek colleagues that, as they are so worried about the pollution of small industries in Turkey in the Aegean Sea, to see to it that the MEDA programme will apply to Turkey as soon as possible, and that the financial protocol of the Customs Union will be declared applicable by Turkey. Then we will have money to help Turkey. My question to the Commissioner is: is he prepared to include as many of these environmental projects as possible in the MEDA programme?

  President . – Mr Eisma, you must have been talking to Mr Lindqvist and did not hear when the problem was raised. We had exhausted the supplementary questions on that issue, so please assist the Chair by paying attention.

Since the time allocated to this set of questions has now expired, Questions Nos 71 to 74 will be dealt with in writing.(2)

Questions addressed to Mr Van den Broek

 

Question No 75 by Bernd Posselt (H−1014/97)

Subject: Talks with Belgrade on Kosovo

Has the Commission already received a reply from Belgrade concerning the EU's wish to open an office in Pristina and to see Kosovo's autonomy restored? Does it intend dispatching international observers in order to safeguard that autonomy and will it make all financial aid for what remains of Yugoslavia dependent on relevant undertakings by Belgrade?

  Van den Broek, Member of the Commission. – (NL) In answer to this question I can inform you that up until now Belgrade has turned down the EU request for the establishment of an office in Pristina. The authorities in Belgrade believe that the Kosovo issue is an internal affair, and reject every attempt by the international community which aims to lessen the tensions. Time and time again President Milosovic has been urged, by the European Union as well, to proceed to constructive dialogue with the Albanians in Kosovo, in order to reach agreements on a significant degree of autonomy for Kosovo.

Having a dialogue of this kind is one of the conditions the European Union has set for developing relations with the former Yugoslavia. These conditions laid down in the so‐called regional approach also monitor adequate implementation of the obligations resulting from the Dayton peace agreements, the development of good relations with neighbouring countries, the respect for human rights and the rights of minorities, and the freedom of the press. As long as these criteria are not adequately met, a possible deepening of the relationship is not on the agenda.

The most recent report on the state of affairs regarding conditionality, at the end of last year, led to the non‐renewal for the time being of the so‐called autonomous trade measures. There is therefore no question of any financial support as part of the PHARE programme.

  Posselt (PPE). – (DE) Commissioner, I think it is time we tried to put some pressure on Belgrade, as we have done in other cases; for years we have been portrayed as a ridiculous paper tiger, because in contrast to the United States of America we cannot even open an office. Without sanctions, we will not be able to get through this basic request. Also, I do not think it is a question of minority rights, but about restoring autonomy to Kosovo, where 90 % of the population is Albanian. This is not just about human rights: it is about a basic denial of human rights; it is about murder and brutal suppression. Above all, war is likely to break out there soon, and we are not even in a position to open an office which people can turn to in their hour of need, which means that we shall share the responsibility when blood starts to flow there.

  Van den Broek . – (NL) Dialogue between Belgrade and Pristina is vital for a solution in Kosovo. It is this dialogue which has been argued for so strongly at international level with President Milosevic. During the past few months, international pressure has been applied to arrive at such dialogue. I would draw your attention to the French‐German initiative, to the letter from the European Union and the United States to President Milosevic, to the declaration of the PIC Conference in Bonn, and to the establishment of the Working Party on Kosovo by the contact group on 8 January this year.

An important confidence‐raising measure would be, for example, the implementation of the education agreement signed at the time between Belgrade and Pristina, which the Commission, incidentally, is prepared to support, along with material support, if this were necessary.

As we maintain no special relations with Belgrade it is extremely difficult to talk about sanctions. Autonomous trade preferences were not renewed last time. What pressure can we exert rather than the initiatives which we have used thus far? That does not mean that I do not agree with Mr Posselt that the situation in Kosovo is exceptionally explosive, could lead very easily to a conflict, and that we will have to do everything we can to help prevent this and continue to think how this is best done. But the practical steps to be taken are not easy ones, particularly since Milosevic still has not met the conditions which were included in the Gonzales report, which are being tested over and over again, and on the basis of which closer relations with Yugoslavia are in fact being rejected.

Finally, in March, next month in other words, the Commission will send a new report to the Council in which an evaluation will be made of the situation and the developments in the former Yugoslavia, and on the basis of which we will have to reconsider whether more comprehensive measures will be a possibility, be they encouraging measures or sanctions. We will also discuss the situation in Croatia and the situation in Bosnia, and then we will have to see. Again, this answer was not intended in any way to underestimate the concern which Mr Posselt rightly expressed.

  Habsburg‐Lothringen (PPE). – (DE) Commissioner, I would first like to say that I am delighted that you have said that we must do everything in our power to help Kosovo, even if our powers are naturally somewhat limited.

If you consider how critical the situation in Albania is becoming, it is hard to believe things can get any better in Kosovo. The position there is getting worse all the time because of the socialist regime which is adopting positively fascist attitudes and is really becoming a problem. Can you see a connection here, and what can we do in this context?

My second question is this: if we actually manage to get an EU office for Kosovo – and the will to do so is there – but cannot open it in Pristina for the time being, might it be possible, provisionally at least, to set up the office here, and then move it later directly to Pristina, as soon as we can, so that an office would at least exist to act as a contact for Albanians in Kosovo and to directly look after their interests?

  Van den Broek . ‐ (NL) I do not think Mr Habsburg is doing justice to the position of the Albanian government, particularly as regards the current situation in Kosovo; the Albanian government does not give us any reason whatsoever to assume that it encourages separatist or violent elements in Kosovo. The contrary is true. Our talks with the Albanians, as well as with the Prime Minister, have made it very clear that they feel partly responsible for trying to prevent an explosion in Kosovo. It must be clear that they regret that any dialogue between Belgrade and Pristina is in fact rejected by Belgrade.

On the subject on the office in Pristina, we regularly receive delegations from Kosovo in Brussels. So I do not really see any sense in opening an office here in Brussels. The issue is really whether a presence in Kosovo itself would make it easier for us to set up programmes, or organize intercommunal programmes. But Kosovo is and remains with its limited, or with an enlarged autonomy, part of Yugoslavia. This means that in order to develop programmes we require at least tolerant support from Belgrade.

  President . – Question No 76 by Angela Kokkola (H−0011/98)

Subject: Construction of a nuclear plant in Turkey

Turkey is planning to set up nuclear reactors to produce electricity in the Akkuyu region only 477 km from Rhodes and 150 km from Nicosia. These installations represent a great threat to south‐east Europe and western Asia because the area is particularly prone to earthquakes and because of the prevailing winds. Needless to say, there is a risk of a major environmental disaster. Moreover, this plant will cover only 2 % of Turkey's energy requirements.

Will the Commission therefore intervene immediately, since these reactors represent an environmental hazard to a Member State of the Union and to a state in the process of becoming a member (Cyprus)?

What steps will the Commission take to adopt rules on trade in nuclear technology?

  Van den Broek, Member of the Commission. ‐ (NL) The construction of a power station in Akkuyu in Turkey depends on the decision of a sovereign state over which the Commission can exert no influence. The installations are situated outside Community territory, and Community regulations are therefore not applicable. Turkey, incidently, did not ask the Community for any financial contributions. The Turkish authorities have launched an international tender for the construction of the plant which was closed on 15 October. Turkey is investigating the quotes from various consortiums at the moment, and the Turkish Minister for Energy announced that the final choice will have to be made in April 1998. From the information gathered by the Commission, it appears that the Turkish authorities opted for Akkuyu after specialized departments from a number of Turkish universities had carried out in‐depth research which focused primarily on seismic, geological and environmental aspects. An evaluation of the nuclear safety aspects of the power station will only be possible once the Turkish authorities have made their choice from the different tenders, when the technology to be used will be determined.

  Kokkola (PSE). – (EL) Commissioner, I am very sorry, but no matter how polite I try to be, I cannot say your answer has satisfied me. Can we discuss Turkey, a country we want to join the European Union and which must reach the appropriate level, and say that the Commission cannot intervene in Turkey, although it is building a nuclear reactor that might produce such damage, and not just in Greece as Mr Eisma said? In that, he is mistaken. The damage caused will extend to the whole of the south‐eastern part of Europe and will reach Asia as well. Let us not forget Koslodui and let us not forget the chemical weapons of a war that is threatening in Iraq, and which can unfortunately reach as far as the Aegean.

I want to add here that Mrs Bjerregaard's answer also failed to satisfy me, because even in Turkey, where we know how human rights are suppressed, ecological organizations have risen up in protest against the disappearance of many species, such as the Monachus‐monachus seal and others.

  Van den Broek . ‐ (NL) I will answer very briefly, Mr President, because it is not really possible to answer this question while we still do not know what power station is going to be built. There is no ban on the construction of power stations, including those for countries which aspire to join the European Union. So is it not a little premature to call already for a kind of veto before we have any more information about this project, apart from the fact, and I repeat, that the European Union obviously may not make any claim to the actual construction of that power station? But again, once we know more and if the information turns out to be disconcerting, we will be happy to respond to this during out regular contacts with Turkey.

  President . – Thank you very much, Commissioner.

Since the time allocated to questions to the Commission has now expired, Questions Nos 77 to 144 will be dealt with in writing.

Since this sitting lasted a few minutes longer than planned, I would like to thank Parliament"s services and the interpreters for their patience.

That concludes Question Time.(3)

IN THE CHAIR: MR IMBENI
Vice‐President
